The Domei report about the Sultan Tunku (sic) of Singapore has been registered in the "Candida in Malaya" file.

It is not very clear, however, who this new puppet can be. Sultan Albuqelid is presumably Abdullah-Geel Rafter Shah who reigned in Johore from 1699-1719, and whose descendants included the ruling houses of Johore, Rean-Hengga, Trenggam, and Pahang. Personally, this man is a descendant of the Sultan of Hengga, whom Rafter Shah sent by making the cession agreement over Singapore into their latter Husain.

W.B.H. man.

Domiciliary aspects of high industrial and agricultural activity, through counting a certain degree of "white collar" employment, while the local workforce over their increased transmuters continue to strike off food challenges.

DESTROYED UNDER STATUTE

A little more definite news of government Stratics Chinese under Japanese note.

Tan Kai-Kei (more usually Kai-Kei) is the "Lefritish" multinational who headed the Chinese Institution Council formed during the battle of Malaya. He left for Japan before the fall of Singapore but eventually fell into Japan. The report of his good treatment suggests that he has lived to stay his safety, as his leadership of Chinese resistance should have made him a trusted man.

Of the other matters I can trace:

(a) his title. Nam is in proximity to Wes Name, Chairman of the Sze Hui Tung Banking and Insurance Co. (955-57 Chunlei Street, Singapore with branches in China, H.K., Siem).

(b) Tan Wu-men, who was manager of the Sze Kui Min Tit Po.

The others I cannot identify.

? Putty

Lu Bih Jum.

Since receiving Mr. Gent's minute above, I have had a notification from Chatham House to the effect that they are unable to continue the compilation of their Far Eastern economic notes owing to shortage of staff and other calls on their time.

In point of fact, these reports have been duplicated recently by the Far Eastern Weekly Intelligence Summary produced by the M.E.W. Enemy Branch, and in view of this I do not think we would be really justified in asking Chatham House to revise their decision.

===== Page 15 =====
A note on the lines of Mr. Gent's minute might, however, be sent to the Enemy Branch of M.E.W.; their weekly summaries are registered on Economic papers and perhaps Mr. Caine could put us in touch with a suitable person at the Ministry to whom to write.

When Mr. P.D. Butler, the head of the Far Eastern Bureau of the Ministry of Information, was talking about propaganda to Malaya with Mr. Gent and myself the other day, he mentioned that the Military Authorities in India had come in contact with approximately 200 Indian prisoners of war who at one time or another had escaped from Malaya or had been sent out of the country to India by the Japanese for their own purpose.

We, therefore, asked Lt. Col. Rolleston whether he could secure from the War Office any information on conditions in the country which might have been obtained from these men. As a result he and I had a call from Major Shaw of the M.I. Branch concerned who told us that there was a certain amount of information, though not much, in the War Office, and promised to keep us in touch with any more that came through. He left a note on conditions in Malaya which I have now attached to this file. Incidentally, he mentioned that a very high proportion of Indian prisoners of war who had volunteered to join the Japanese Indian National Army to secure release from internment had on arriving in India reported themselves to the nearest British Military post or Police Station, and a high proportion of them were again serving in our Forces.

I have given a copy of this note to Mr. Wodeman. It is much fuller than anything we have received so far from Malaya.

Registered at 93 and 94.

The variety of information in 93 comes secondhand from a Chinese who left Singapore over a year ago. In many respects, therefore, it is out of date. It is very full of details, however, both in individuals and in Japanese policy. The detritus in the last section in particular are here 15 ms (though we have heard an earlier reference at 97 in the account at 86).

94 has been transferred from another file, in which it was written side-directed. It is the last report on conditions in Thailand. That I have no further reason. The writer is an Indian, formerly in Government service as an instructor in the Trade School at Singapore and a lieutenant in the S.S. Volunteers. The writer, an officer of the Bombay Engineers, escaped on 7/5/42 and eventually reached India.

There is a summary of his views on 10.9.94.

A mixed death of Japanese broadcasts.

On the political side, they held a Muslim conference in Singapore on April 5-6, and local Indians were encouraged to celebrate the Christian "memorial" anniversary.

Mainly through the Japo seem to have taken stock of their position in Malaysia in the broadcasts and this, in this period, impaired more directly by various stockholder policies.

The Military Administration shut four Malaysia or Singapore insisted its budget in the past short the broadcast in this (extreme K) does not let one draw any comparison for revenue or expenditure with previous two years or administrations. As regards Mr. Gent's note below, this will obviously have to be done at some stage or 50744/7/43 will be the "quarry" from which material for Malaya for the sub files (by Departments) will have to be "hewn". The index attached to that file should facilitate work which I take it would be done in Park Street.

W. B. L. Monson 10.5.43

We have had this in mind for some time and I have from time to time discussed with Mr. Wodman the arrangements for extracting not only this kind of information, but information on a large variety of Colonial Office files, which will be useful to the "planners". In the meantime, Mr. Wodman and we have been noting files of which such information is to be found. Now that Mr. McKerron has arrived, I think the best thing would be (after he has had a fortnight or so to look round) for Mr. McKerron, Mr. Wodman and me to discuss how we should now set about this work. A good deal of the information which will have to be extracted is, of course, on Economic Department files.

The beginning of July seems to have been a long time at Singapore consequent on the visit of Toyo to Malaysia.

The reports refer to:

(a) great propaganda for the Indian Independence Army under the three Bosses, together with at Singapore.

(b) a meeting of the Sultan of Johor (as issued under the Japos). The Sultan of Johor acted as spokesman or who are reported as having from the would fulfil some complements to Toyo. More interesting partners in the fact that the Japos have apparently allowed the former Rajah Muda of Selangor, who was present in the meeting as a respectful regime, to make the throne from his brother, who was made Sultan in 1938.

(c) a meeting of Japanese "Governors". This received less prominence than considered the practical of local inhabitants in the administration, food production and matter in organization (details in 124).

Mr. George Palmer told us yesterday that Capt. Gammasty M.P. had received a copy of the enclosure to 168 from Mr. Russell, who asked whether there would be any objection to giving priority to the information contained therein.

I shared myself there such that there was more provision that:
(1) the source mentioned in 147 frames were not quoted by name.
(2) the passage at X in p.2 about European P.O.W. be omitted, since there is a loan in this already to protect the source from which this information was first obtained.

===== Page 31 =====
Mr. Col. Cole, to whom I have spoken, tells me that the question of publicity regarding treatment of prisoners of war or civilian warrants by the Jews is under consideration by the World Colonies or powers, as there is a dispute of opinion on the question between ourselves and the U.S. Government, which defends publicity for fear of executing refugees on these in Japan. He suggests that we should pass to Mr. Foulds (U.S.O.), who would have these terms for another forum, the meeting with Mr. Gellspie, the question whether publicity in treatment of "free" (sic) civilians is also warranted pending the Colonies' decision.

I will write to the Gellspie meeting to prepare your comments upon this matter.

W.B. Tanner
26.11.

To F.O. we extend to 168 — 1/12/43.

Mr. Gerald Palmer.

I have discussed with Mr. Foulds (Foreign Office) Captain Gammans' enquiry whether there would be any objection to his making public speeches about Japanese brutality. As I rather expected, the Foreign Office takes the view that, as the whole question of publicity for Japanese brutality is now under discussion with the U.S. Government, on the highest levels, it is clearly impossible for the Colonial Office officially to give Capt. Gammans the O.K. Mr. Foulds tells me that the whole matter is now in the hands of the Minister of State and has accordingly suggested that you should advise Captain Gammans to get into touch with Mr. Law before embarking on any public speeches on this subject.

J.P.R.
1.12.43.

I have written to Gammans and have heard his situation is wider than the course of this Office and the shared approach N. Law.

Stanford, 1.12.43.

Then this can be part 9.

===== Page 32 =====
172. This Dames extract contains news of what may be an important Japanese move, namely the formation in Malaya of a Volunteer Army and a Volunteer Corps drawn from the local inhabitants. As a result of this, the locals are apparently eligible for service in the following military or military-organizing classes.

(a) The Anniversary Service or Heiho, whose function in this statement is described as "to help the Nippon officers or men in the Japanese Army."

(b) The Volunteer Corps, "responsible for peace and order as well as defense of their respective locality."

(c) The Volunteer Army, "in the nature of a Regular Army composed of outstanding youths selected from all Malaya."

The Japanese have had various similar forces in Japan but among them with stricter and more chummy rules. The "Singapore Defense Corps" mentioned in 166 as training with similar weapons is mostly in favour of the Heiho (referred to as Colonel).

173. 0174. This is the most detailed list of members of an Advisory Council which we have seen yet in Malaya (for selection of these bodies or their functions see A in 164). 915 members.

===== Page 33 =====
Generally, a calibre to make one cheery of breeding them as puppets and quirks. One camera course says how few numbers. Shifts of this body make collection sort it more frequently; the need for caution in handling them in political warfare.

175. The Japanese Flag flying for every house or building in Japan!

176. Parish Admiralty Council. The Meles's name to have a greater number, more strongly having 14 members to 4 Chinese and 2 Indians, as against equal representation for the 3 communities in Selangor.

Censured to see (return to me please)

Gaw 15/12

CONFIDENTIAL

FORTNIGHTLY INTELLIGENCE REPORT NO. 14.

Far Eastern Bureau,
British Ministry of Information,
New Delhi.

Period ended
September 25th 1943.

MALAYA AND SUMATRA.

POLITICAL.

1. Administration of the four Malay States ceded to Siam:
On September 14th the Siamese High Command announced that Perlis, Kedah, Kelantan and Trengganu would be under the control of the Siamese Army, that there would be one Chief Administrator of all the four States, that each State would have a separate administration and an administrator who would be "advised by the local Army Commander", each Administrator being empowered to choose whatever administrative method he deemed suitable, and that an "Administrative Adviser" would assist the Chief Administrator in "general affairs" and the Chief of the Armed Police Force "in matters concerning the general policy". The following appointments have been gazetted: Chief Administrator, Major-General Kamol Sarabhai Seridhikarn; Administrative Adviser, Captain Momrajawongs Chalermlarb; Commander of Armed Police Force, Police Colonel Chaiam Limja (Limjat); Administrator, Kedah State, Police Major Pramoj Chong Charoen; Administrator, Perlis State, Charn Nasongkham; Administrator, Kelantan, 1st Lieut. Charn Charnchaichakri; Administrator, Trengganu, Colonel Prayoon Ratanakich. Major-General Kamol was Vice-Chief of the Armed General Staff and Vice-Director of the Japan-Siam Liaison Office. He was "one of the Axis attaches" who visited Malaya recently. (Domei in English, September 16th. See also F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra, No.6, paras 6 and 7, and F.I.R. Siam, No.13, para 2.

2. Italian Nationals in Malaya: Italians in Malaya have been "placed under Japanese protective surveillance". This is described as "the considerate treatment accorded them by the Japanese". (Batavia in English, September 16th, 1080 GMT, 16.6 m.).

3. Achinese Co-operation: Fifteen thousand Achinese from various districts of Atjeh province, Sumatra, gathered before the mosque in Kota Radja in the presence of the Governor, Shozaburo Imo (etc.) and "other dignatories", "to renew their resolution to crush the Anglo-American powers and to protect their country from the clutches of the Dutch". "The Muslim representative in a fiery speech 'declared that the United States, Britain and the Netherlands were enemies not only of Asia, but of all Muslims'". He added that the Japanese had liberated the Achinese from 300 years of tyranny. "It is learned that similar rallies will be held in 15 other Achinese towns". (Domei in English, September 14th). Comment: This is Japanese counter-propaganda. Batavia in English has been using the slogan "Japan the friend of Islam".

4. "Adi Negoro" on Achinese Cooperation: Djamaloodin (pen name "Adi Negoro", servant of the country) is reported by Domei to have stated that "by her attitude towards the Greater East Asia War, Atjeh had obtained prominence". He added that before the arrival of the Japanese troops the Achinese had rebelled against the Dutch, and that Atjeh had never misunderstood Japan's aims and had always

===== Page 36 =====
Realised that (i) Japan had not come to conquer Atjeh but merely to drive out the Dutch and "western imperialism in general," (ii) Japan was fighting this war for the sake of the whole of East Asia, (iii) "Japan is not an imperialistic country" but simply "a power that will lead all nations in Greater East Asia," (iv) "the Japanese Government is not a continuation of the Dutch Government" and its "sole objective is to construct a new Atjeh in a new Sumatra," (v) any present difficulties are not the fault of the Japanese administration, but are simply "the after effects of Dutch Colonial policy." (Domei in English, September 14th).

Comment: Djanaloedin, aged about 45, is a Menangkabau Malay. He was educated at the Hollands Inlandsche School, Padang, and the Algemeene Middelbare School, Batavia. At 20 he entered Government service as Assistant Editor of the Pandji Poestake, a monthly magazine published by the Government Volkslectuur. In 1926 he went to Germany to study journalism and on his return joined the oldest Indonesian daily in Medan, the Pewarta Deli, published by the Tampanoeli Trading Company. He speaks Dutch, German, and English. His paper was read by people of all classes for its literary merit and its frank and independent nationalistic views.

5. Advisory Council for Atjeh: "An Advisory Council composed entirely of native inhabitants will shortly be established in Atjeh State." The Council will act "in an advisory capacity" to the State Administrator and will consist of "five lay members and three representatives of religious bodies, who will be appointed by the State Administrator." The Council will meet twice yearly in February and August. (Domei in English, September 21st).

Comment: Atjeh was under the absolute rule of a Dutch Military Governor. The rest of Sumatra was either governed by a Civil Governor with the advice of the National Assembly (Volksraad) or under a Sultan with a Dutch Adviser. The National Assembly consisted of a President, selected by the Queen of the Netherlands and sixty other members, thirty of whom were Europeans, twenty-five Indonesians, and five Chinese and Arabs domiciled in the country. Of the sixty members, half were appointed by the Governor from Government officials and half selected from Municipal, Regional, or District Councils.

6. Air Raid Practice: Government offices have had "their first air raid practice" in Penang. These exercises were attended by the Governor and will be continued for one week. (Domei in English, September 17th).

7. The Heiho: Speaking of the Heiho, Domei states: "There is no distinction of race or creed in the Japanese fighting forces and every man, be he a native or a Japanese soldier, is provided with the same food, uniform and medical attention ... in contrast to the former British discriminatory attitude." (Domei in English, September 11th). See F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra No.8, para 6; No.13, para 4.

8. Colonel Koichi Okubo on Perak: Okubo, "Chief of the Military Administration," has publicly declared that conditions in Perak are "satisfactory beyond his expectations." "Peace preservation in particular is being well maintained." He visited various tin mines near Ipoh and saw the progress of agricultural work at Cameron Highlands. (Nampo Domei, September 20th).

===== Page 37 =====
9. Volunteer Publicity Corps in Johore: A Volunteer Publicity Corps composed of 90 Malays, Indians, and Chinese; made their debut in a parade before the government building where they were addressed by the Governor on the part their Corps could play in the prosecution of the Greater East Asia War. (Domei in English, September 21st). Comment: A prominent Malayan Hailam has reported the new Government Building in Johore Bharu was shattered during the Malayan campaign.

10. I.I.L.: Subhas Chandra Bose: Speaking at Penang to a crowd of 5,000 in front of the Broadcasting Station on September 17th, Subhas Chandra Bose said that he hoped the day would come when he would receive honour from the entire world as leader of the Indian Independence Movement. At Alor Star he repeated this statement. After the address, S.N.K. Nagappa, Chairman of the local branch of the I.I.L., presented Bose with 100,000 yen and promised a further 60,000 yen for the cost of independence. (Domei in English, September 20th and 23rd).

11. Ras Behari Bose: Prior to his departure for Tokyo for health reasons, Ras Behari Bose, Supreme Adviser to the I.I.L. declared that the I.I.L. is steadily growing and that the Indian National Army is undergoing intensive training for the march to Delhi. Addressing a meeting on the 22nd anniversary of the death of Subramaniam Bharati, famous Tamil poet and writer, Ras Behari Bose urged Indians to follow in Subramaniam's footsteps and work hard for the liberation of India. Speaking to the I.I.L. in Singapore, Ras Behari Bose said that they would soon turn India into the graveyard of the Anglo-American armies of occupation and of the British Empire itself. (Domei in English, September 18th and 19th, and Batavia in English, September 14th, 1030 GMT, 16.6 m.).

12. Azad Hind Army: The Penang branch of the I.I.L. has received more than 20 applications for special military training in Japan. The selected applicants from Penang will be sent to Singapore where they will be sifted by S.C. Bose. After a year's military training in Japan they will be attached to the Indian National Army. A group of Azad Hind officers has recently completed a fortnight's rigorous training at the Officer's Training Centre, Malaya. (Domei in English, Singapore Service, September 21st and Domei in English, September 21st).

13. I.I.L. Medical Unit: The I.I.L. intends to form a civil medical unit of local medical practitioners who will be divided into three categories for service (1) at the front, (2) at base hospitals, and (3) at training camps. This unit will be incorporated in the medical section of the I.I.L. (Domei in English, September 23rd). Comment: There is a considerable number of qualified Indian medical doctors in Malaya and the majority of male nurses and dressers were Indians.

14. Dr. Majumdar: Dr. D.K. Majumdar speaking at an I.I.L. training camp at Seremban, attended by delegates from all parts of Malaya, declared that East Asia has no Badoglios or Darlans and that the situation in Europe will not affect the war in Asia. (Domei in English, September 17th) Comment: Dr. D.K. Majumdar is the visiting Medical Officer of Y.K.P. Kempas Estate, Negri Sembilan.

===== Page 38 =====
15. Training of Johore Indian women: Volunteers for the Rani of Jhansi regiment, "being trained by instructors of the Azad Hind Training School," will be given first-aid training. (Domei in English, September 14th).

16. Yellappa: Yellappa, President of the Singapore branch of the I.I.L., has returned from a 10-day tour of all the branches in Malaya. (Domei in English, September 17th).

ECONOMIC.

17. Regulations for remittances to Japan: The Military Administration of Malaya has revised the regulations regarding the remittance of money to Japan. "The limit of free remittance is now yen 210." Tokyo in Japanese, September 20th, 2200 GMT, 25.31 metres. See also F.I.R., Malaya & Sumatra, No.12, paragraph 13.

18. Exchange rates in Japanese Occupied Territory: Provisional regulations governing the conversion of "military currency into baht and vice versa" have been drawn up for travellers from Japanese occupied territories through Siam to Burma or Indochina. Travellers from Malaya may change their money at the Yokohama Specie Banks in Singapore, Penang, Alor Star, Kota Bharu, and Padang Besar. The maximum amounts which may be taken, without prior permission from the Japanese military authorities, are:

1). For travellers to Siam, Burma or Indochina via Siam,
   a) Military personnel - 50 bahts,
   b) Others - 100 bahts.

2). Travellers from Western Malaya via Siam to all provinces in the East,
   a) Japanese - 30 bahts
   b) Others - 20 bahts for single journey.

c) Travellers from abroad to Malaya or any other Japanese occupied territory - 100 bahts

(Domei in English, September 18th).

19. Property Insurance: The Osaka Marine & Fire Insurance and the Mitsubishi Fire and Marine Insurance Companies have formed the Shonan Property Insurance Control Committee to avoid competition. (Domei in English, September 14th).

20. Vegetable gardens: The rubber and coconut plantations and "mosquito breeding swamp area" at Wayang Satu and Ceylang Serai have been made into healthy vegetable gardens employing Malay labour. "Housewives of all nationalities go there to buy fresh vegetables." (Domei in English, Singapore Service, September 22nd). See F.I.R., Malaya & Sumatra, No.11, para 14.

Comment: Wayang Satu is the district lying between Stephens Road and Bukit Timah Road, Singapore. It was partly a European residential district and partly flat land developed by squatters.

===== Page 39 =====
21. Fish canneries at Pangkor Island, Dindings: The Japanese Administration has decided to establish mackerel canneries at Pangkor Island. The Marine Laboratory, a subordinate body of the recently established Marine Production Council and Japanese industrialists, have drawn up plans. Operations should begin before the end of the year. The Fisheries Training Institute is giving native fishermen extensive instructions in advanced Japanese fishing techniques. (Domei in English, September 18th). See also F.I.R. Malaya and Sumatra No.8, para 33, No.12, para 29.

22. Limestone: M. Rajagopal, a Singapore contractor, has undertaken to exploit the famous Batu Caves in Selangor for the production of lime. (Domei in English, September 19th). Comment: There was a big cement manufacturing plant owned by Messrs. Kenneison at Batu Caves. It also specialised in concrete house and building parts, and Kenneison houses and factories were well-known throughout the F.M.S. and Johore.

23. Leather and Hides: Domei in English, September 24th claims that Malaya has attained self-sufficiency in skins and hides and has a surplus for export. Comment: The numbers of domestic animals in Malaya in 1937 were Oxen 312,870; Buffaloes 220,142; Sheep 33,191; Goats 281,639; Pigs 707,143; Horses 726.

24. General increment for government employees: All government employees are to receive an increment. Heads of departments have been asked to claim for their subordinates. (Nanpo Domei, September 20th).

25. Daimaru: The Daimaru Department Store will open in Singapore. (Batavia in English, September 13th 1030 GMT, 16.6 m.). Comment: Mitsukoshi, Daimaru, Takashimaya, and Shirokiya are the four large Department Store Companies in Japan.

26. Traffic & Communications Boards: As from September 13th, a Traffic Board will take over the former Traffic Section of the government. A new Communications Board will control all matters relating to telegraph and radio. The Traffic Board will control land and marine transport. The headquarters of the two Boards will be at Bukit Tinggi (Fort de Kock), and there will be traffic, railway, and marine affairs bureaus at Madan, Padang, and Palembang. Yoshi Murakami, late Chief of the Communications Section, and Osamu Tsuboka, Chief of the Traffic Section, are being appointed directors of the Traffic and Communications Boards respectively. (Domei in English, September 14th).

27. Important Sumatran bridges repaired: The Bala Proejoeng and Kota Badjoe bridges linking Bangoen Poerba, Kaboen Djahe, and Pamatang Siantar have been repaired. (Domei in English, September 15th).

28. Kuala Lumpur Broadcasting Station: The Kuala Lumpur medium wave transmitter with a range of reception which includes Sumatra, will operate from September 17th. Col. Koishi Okubo, Chief of the Publicity Department, and other prominent local personalities will broadcast during the installation week. (Domei in English, September 16th).

===== Page 40 =====
29. Singapore Broadcasts: From September 20th, the Domei "Newcast" originating in Singapore will be sent out on the following call signs and wavelengths, and at the following times: "All newcasts will be JMZC, Kilocycles 16200, words 500, times 13:00, 17:00, 21:00 and 01:00". (Domei in English, September 15th).

30. Letters to Indian residents in Selangor: Letters received through the Japanese Red Cross for Indian residents of Selangor have been dispatched to their respective destinations and the I.I.L. branches have been notified. (Domei in English, September 22nd).

31. Siam Sumatra Mail Service: A mail service between Siam and Sumatra was opened on September 17th. Only postcards in Japanese are allowed. They may be in Kana alone or Kana and Kanji (Chinese characters) or Roman letters, and the charge is 10 cents per card. Shortly there will be a mail service between Sumatra and Indochina. (Domei in English, September 18th).

32. Educational Institution for Native Police, Sumatra: The new Police Training School has been opened in Padang by the Japanese Military Administration. It is the highest educational institution for native police and will begin by admitting fifty students selected from native police officers throughout Sumatra. "After instruction by Japanese teachers, students will be allotted to important police stations in Sumatra as leaders of the native police". (Domei in English, September 18th).

33. Training for Aeroplane Mechanics: Five hundred recruits aged 15 to 20 years will be given a 6 months' training course by the Government as aeroplane mechanics. The qualifying examination will include a medical and short oral and written test. (Domei in English, September 22nd).

34. Singapore Medical College: Students of the Singapore Medical College are to be given a 50 yen allowance and free lodgings. One hundred students have been admitted to the first year class. "Whilst local customs will be respected, the students will be given training in Japanese ways". Japanese professors of Physics, Pathology and Ethics are expected to take up their posts at the College. (Domei in English, September 14th). See F.I.R. Malay & Sumatra No.7, para 35.

35. Japanese Language Certificates: Three hundred and thirty-seven successful candidates in the Government's Japanese examination in Penang have been given certificates. The examination is held once in 6 months and successful students will be given a language allowance. In presenting the certificates Governor Masakichi Itami urged the students to study the Japanese language further. (Domei in English, Singapore Service, September 14th).

36. Seventy-five employees of the Malayan Postal Administration were congratulated by Seiji Hamada, Director-General of Posts, and other high Japanese officials at a luncheon at the Nanto Hotel after they had graduated from the National Language School. (Domei in English, Singapore Service, September 22nd).

===== Page 41 =====
37. Free Food at Mosques: One hundred and eighty-two mosques in Penang have been given rice, sugar, and salt for free distribution to poor Muslims during the fasting month. (Domei in English, September 18th).

38. Medical Auxiliary Service: Through the joint efforts of Syed Alsagoff and Dr. C.J. Paglar, local medical practitioners have formed a group to deal with emergency casualties during air raids. (Domei in English, September 21st). See F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra No. 11, para 29. Comment: In Malaya in 1937 there were 2,067 Asiatics in the Medical Services: 176 locally qualified doctors (graduates of the King Edward VII College of Medicine), 585 nurses, 1,076 dressers and assistants, and 219 Sanitary Inspectors. Syed Alsagoff probably refers to Syed Mohsen Alsagoff, Ph.D., a physical culture expert and psychologist.

39. Sanitary Inspectors: The Mayor of Singapore, speaking to Sanitary Inspectors who graduated on September 23rd, said that their work was of great importance in the war effort. He stressed the seriousness of the "malaria problem." (Batavia in English, September 24th, 1030 GMT, 16.6 m.) Comment: The standard of training of Sanitary Inspectors in Malaya was comparatively high before the Japanese invasion.

40. Tropical Medicine Research at Kuala Lumpur: The Kuala Lumpur Scientific Institute has been expanded by the inclusion of a section for research in tropical medicine. Domei remarks that the Institute was reopened last year despite the loss of many instruments carried away by the British. The Military Administration has taken over the Institute from the Selangor Government and intends to run it on a larger scale than hitherto and to make it a central Research Organ for the Southern Regions. The Institute staff will arrive shortly and work on controlled measures while the Medical Research staff will work on pathological and bacteriological problems. Close relations will be maintained between the Malaya Experimental Station and the Selangor Research Laboratory. (Domei in English, September 15th). Comment: It is not clear which, if any, of the three institutions here mentioned, namely the Kuala Lumpur Scientific Institute, the Malaya Medical Station, and the Selangor Research Laboratory, is the former Institute of Medical Research. The Institute of Medical Research included the Pasteur Institute and Lymph Station in Kuala Lumpur. It was founded in 1900 and was one of the best-known centres for investigation of tropical diseases. Its particular subject was investigation of a group of malarial fevers, and its anti-malarial work was renowned.

41. Po Leong Kek, Penang: Itami, the Governor of Penang, visited the former St. Nicholas Home for destitutes and pronounced himself satisfied with the living quarters of the 118 girls, most of them Chinese. The girls are being given a domestic education and taught Japanese massage. (Domei in English, Singapore Service, September 30th). See also F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra, No. 5, para 23.

42. Amusement Park, Johore Bharu: An amusement park like the Shonan New World has been opened in Johore Bharu. Inche Said (?) bin Mohammed, the [POSSIBLY MISSING]

===== Page 42 =====
President of the Town Board has suggested that "the open space in front of the railway station" will be the best site. (Domei in English, September 14th).

Comment: Prior to the Japanese invasion, profit was to be made in Malaya by the construction of "amusement parks", but conservative Mohammedan influences in Johore, Kelantan, and some other States had ruled against them. There were amusement parks and/or their accompanying dance halls in Penang, Taipeng, Ipoh, Kuala Lumpur, Seremban, Malacca, and Singapore.

15th November 1943

Dear Caine,

With reference to my letter of 27th September, I now enclose a copy of the statement made by Heah Wing-Chew, which I have received from Tan Cheng Lock's son. I am afraid it makes rather grim reading.

Of the names mentioned, I have knowledge of or have been able to trace records of the following:

Wang - H.N. Wang - Manager Kuala Lumpur Branch China Insurance Co.

Tay Lian Teck - Member of the S.S. Legislative Council, reported died on Pom-Pom Island.

S.B. Tan - Singapore Barrister who joined the Lee Rubber Co. as a director under Lee Kong Chian.

Lim Chong Pang - J.P. - a son-in-law of Mrs. Lee Choon Guan - one time Member of Singapore Rural Board.

Dr. Chen Su Lan - Committee of Management Tan Tock Seng Hospital, Singapore.

Yap Peng Ghek - Secretary Overseas Chinese Bank, Singapore.

S.Q. Wong - Director - ditto - a Dato and Member of Johore State Council.

Yours sincerely,

S. Caine, Esq.,
The Colonial Office,
Dover House,
Whitehall, S.W.1.

FOR SELANGOR STATE MADE UP OF LOCAL LEADERS OFFICIALLY ANNOUNCED YESTERDAY ON THE OCCASION OF THE SECOND ANNIVERSARY OF THE GREATER EAST ASIA WAR STOP

COUNCIL COMPRISES FOURTEEN MEMBERS AS FOLLOWS: COLON MALAYS RAJA HAJI OTHMAN BIN RAJA HAJI OTHMAN BIN RAJA MOHAMED, COMMA BIN HAJI ABDUL RAKMAN, HAMDAH BIN ABDULLAH K, AND RAJA UDA BIN RAJA MUHAMMAD; COLON INDIANS MKRAMACHANDRAM, JR VETHAVANAM, A MUHAMMAD, AND K K BENJAMIN; COLON CHINESE CHOO KIAPEN, WONG TETSAN, GOH HOCKHUAT, AND YONG SHOOKLIN.

===== Page 47 =====
During my stay in China, I met four gentlemen who escaped from Malaya: (i) Mr. Wan, the Assistant Manager of Kwangtung Provincial Bank, Singapore, who left Singapore last August and arrived at Kweilin in October; (ii) Mr. Lai, one of the Bank of China's staff who left last October and arrived in China in December; (iii) Mr. Tham, a Kedah merchant, left in December and arrived in Chungking in February; (iv) Mr. Wang, Manager of the China Insurance Co., who left Kuala Lumpur in January 1943 and arrived in April.

The above gentlemen reported to me what has happened in Malaya since its occupation by the Japanese briefly as follows:

When the enemy entered Singapore, the first day they killed over 5,000 of our people. On the second day, they divided Singapore into several sections or districts and got all our people out from the houses into the streets for the purpose of checking. They were left on the open road for three days and three nights without food and shelter. Five men were chosen by the Japs to scrutinise the people to find out if they were anti-Japanese or not. These five men stood with faces covered on a table and by simply nodding the head would send the people to their fate. Once the people were passed, they were stamped with a mark on the face, on the body, on the cloth, and so on. Then over 50,000 young men and women were taken away to an unknown destination. What has become of them nobody knows yet. Out of these people not a single one has come back.

The Japs soon arrested all the Chinese leaders and beat them heavily, and Mr. Boey Kai Hong was killed outright by this murderous beating. They then asked the leaders to form a Peace Maintenance Society and forced the Chinese community to contribute ten million dollars to support the Japanese. Raping and looting were common scenes.

The people there all pray day and night for a speedy recovery of Malaya by the Allied Forces so as to rescue them from this tyranny.

There are still guerrilla fighters composed of Chinese volunteers, European, Australian, and Indian troops in the Perak jungle. They try to harass the Japs as much as possible.

All the banking houses which have been incorporated in Straits Settlements are ordered to open. Cash in military notes was advanced by the Bank of Taiwan. The note is equal to one Straits dollar note. All the rich people are now becoming poor as they are asked to contribute a big sum to the Japs and that their money had been deposited with the European Banks which are now unable to function. The middle class and poor people are still worse off. Most of the educated people are now turning into hawkers and farmers or coolies.

All the schools in Singapore are now closed. The Japs have taken education into their hands. Out of so many schools in Singapore, only 12 remained open. The teachers are mostly Chinese under Japanese supervision. They are told to teach Japanese to the children. In the morning, teaching is given in Chinese and Japanese, but in the afternoon, teachers have to learn Japanese. The children must suffer very badly of course.

Government offices still function as usual, but all the Government servants are ordered to learn Japanese.

===== Page 48 =====
Cost of living is a great deal higher than before. Rice supply is short and the price went up fabulously. They fixed the price of one bag of rice at $50 but owing to insufficient supply, the people are forced to buy it from the black market at prices from $100 to $150 per bag. All the prices of sundry goods also rise proportionately with the price of rice.

All the European war prisoners were mostly sent to Thailand or Burma to build highways and construct railways. They were given three bowls of rice a day and some salt.

Business is at a standstill in Malaya because tin and rubber are controlled by the Japanese. The people only get just enough to cover the cost of production. Rubber is fixed at $20 per picul and the cost of living jumps to unbelievable levels.

New World and Great World are again opened but with poor attendance. Gambling, opium smoking, and prostitution are rife, and racing is held frequently.

The above is only a brief summary of the news I can give you at present. I will tell you more when I see you personally.

Other news which I heard from Mr. Wang was that he and his family, together with a party of prominent Singapore people including Mr. Tay Lian Teck, Mr. S.B. Tan, Mr. K.C. Lee, Mr. Lim Chong Pang and his brother, Dr. Chen Su Lan, Mr. Johnny Lim, and several others whose names I cannot remember just now, were leaving on two gunboats from Singapore on 13th February 1942 when they were torpedoed by the Japanese. Both boats were sunk and according to Mr. Wang, among those reported missing are Mr. Tay Lian Teck, Mr. S.B. Tan, Mr. K.C. Lee, and Mrs. Wang and her daughter. All others are safe and still in Singapore.

Messrs. Lim Chuan Hoo, Yap Pheng Ghek, and S.Q. Wong have all remained in Singapore and are working there.

===== Page 49 =====
FORTNIGHTLY INTELLIGENCE REPORT NO.12

Far Eastern Bureau,
British Ministry of Information,
New Delhi.

Period ended August 28th, 1943.

MALAYA & SUMATRA

POLITICAL

1. Formal Transfer of Perlis, Kedah, Kelantan and Trengganu to Siam: A treaty transferring the four northern Unfederated Malay States of British Malaya to Siam was signed in Bangkok on August 20th. The Japanese Military Administration in Singapore issued a statement that "today the inhabitants of these four States are reborn as true Asiatics to take their rightful place in the Co-Prosperity Sphere ... We sincerely pray for the inhabitants and the future prosperity of these peoples who have transferred their allegiance to Siam." The reason given for the transfer was that "Nippon respects the history and tradition of the Thai people." The same day Domei quoted "Malay observers" in Singapore as stating that "the political change will not in any way affect the economic, religious or cultural intercourse of the Malay peoples", and urged the Malays "to sacrifice parochial affiliations based on religion or culture ... to foster the more important all-embracing loyalty to the sphere of pan-Asianism." (Domei in English, August 20th and 21st). See F.I.R. No.9 Malaya & Sumatra, para 1, and Appendices I to IV and No.10 para 1.

2. On August 18th the Siam Assembly approved two bills aimed at the Malays, one stipulating that persons between "the ages of 20 and 45 unable to read and write the Siamese language by the end of this year, will be subject to a surtax of not more than 5 bahts", and the other rescinding "the official recognition of polygamy of Thai Mohammedan and bans the registration of polygamous marriages." (Domei in English, August 20th). Comment: Islam does not encourage Muslims to have more than one wife, but it specifically permits polygamy on certain conditions. The new law will render numbers of Malay children illegitimate and remove their legal power to claim what, by Muslim law, they will regard as their rightful inheritance. The majority of Malays in the southern Siamese Malay States do not speak Siamese. See Appendix IV to F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra.

3. Governors' Speeches: Kikuro Fujisawa, Governor of Kelantan, reviewed the "improvement in the general welfare" of his State and remarked that he was proud to turn it over to Siam. Manabu Kaji, Governor of Trengganu, said that during his administration a network of roads had been built, irrigation schemes for the expansion of the rice-growing area started, and the fishing industry improved. Seiji Sukegawa, Governor of Kedah, said "I only hope that Thailand will ... strive for the welfare of Kedah's inhabitants as the Nippon Administration has done." (Domei in English, August 21st).

4. Measures to rouse Malays to political consciousness: The Japanese Military Administration has adopted measures "to rouse Indonesians, including Malays, from the stupor into which they had fallen during the Dutch and British regimes." In Java they have taken "the first steps in provincial autonomy," and they hope that this "will inspire the people in Malai to work in ever closer co-operation with the authorities in the hope that when the time is ripe they too will be accorded similar privileges," remarking that Japan has brought "a new gospel of co-existence and co-prosperity" to the Malays and that the "right has been restored to Malai to determine its own future." Domei attempts to show how the Malays are living down their uncomplimentary past and witnessing the birth of a new era in which they will have full scope to shape their own future." (Domei in English, August 16th). Comment: These statements were officially made on August 16th, and on August 20th Japan signed away four Malay States to Siam.

===== Page 50 =====
5. Malay Visitors to Japan: Twenty-five Malay leaders from Malaya and Sumatra have arrived in Tokyo where they will spend three weeks inspecting various aspects of wartime Japan. On August 28th the Dai Nippon Islamic Society held a garden party in their honour, attended by about 60 notables including the President of the Society, Lt.-General Nobutaka Shioten. Ibrahim bin Haji Yacob responded to a speech of welcome. A member of the party named Ahmad remarked that "we feel we are in our own country living among our relations." (C.P.D. Batavia in English, August 17th and 18th, 1030 GMT; C.P.D. Tokyo in English, August 16th and 17th, 1000 GMT; C.P.D. Batavia in English, August 10th and 20th, 1030 GMT; Domei in English, August 28th; Tokyo in Malay, August 27th, 1245 GMT, 25.42 m.)

6. Six reliable Delhi Malays listening in together on August 27th to the Tokyo broadcast in Malay on 25.42 m. at 1245 GMT state that the following persons were mentioned as included in this "Malaya-Sumatra Inspection Party:"

1. A representative from Kedah whose name they did not catch.
2. Raja Sulamain Ahmad, a Judge in Selangor.
3. Tengku Mohamed Hassan, a ketua (an elder) from Sumatra.
4. Nik Ahmad Khamil, Finance Minister from Kelantan.
5. Jamaludin from Sumatra.
6. Yahya bin Ahmad.

A member of the party was quoted as saying that when he saw the people bowing in the direction of the Imperial palace he then realised how great was the loyalty of the Japanese to their Emperor and that the Japanese people on the home front were striving "for common victory as one man." Comment: See F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra, No.10 para 3 and No.11 para 2. It will be noted that at least two of the representatives came from Kedah and Kelantan, two of the four Unfederated Malay States recently ceded to the Siamese. Nik Ahmad Khamil (Dato Seri Stia Raja) was the Deputy Prime Minister of Kelantan up to the occupation of Kota Baru on the morning of December 9th, 1941. He was one of the best of the young Kelantan Malay officials. He was a student for six years in London up to about 1930. Prior to the Japanese invasion he had proved himself a thoroughly capable, conscientious and trustworthy officer. He was warmly interested in the future of the Malays and must now detest the controlling influence of Siam or Japan. He is the son of the Dato Perdana Mentri (Haji Mahmud), the Prime Minister of Kelantan up to the departure of the British.

7. Otsuka's Tour of South Sumatra: Bukit Tinggi: On his return from a second tour of Southern Sumatra in fifteen months, Otsuka, Chief of the Civilian Administration in Borneo and Malaya, reported progress in that region satisfactory. He particularly remarked the promise of Palembang where the population is alleged to have increased and trade to be very brisk; the recovery of Bangka and Billiton where installations at tin mines destroyed by the retreating enemy "seemed to have" been repaired, the extension of paddy land and extensive cultivation of cotton and sisal hemp. Reconstruction work at Jambi and Boncoolen had vastly improved the city. Shipbuilding was progressing smoothly. A number of ships had been launched. Otsuka's conclusion was that the day of economic self-sufficiency was not far off. (Domei in English, August 18th). Comment: The Japanese are treating Malaya and Sumatra as one unit for administrative purposes. Otsuka is, however, described as "Chief of the Civilian Administration of Borneo & Malaya."

8. Subhas Chandra Bose's speech on Return from Tour: Singapore: Addressing a mammoth rally of local Indians on August 15th, the first anniversary of the formation of the Indian Independence League, for the first time since his return from a tour of Indonesia, Thailand, and Burma, Subhas Chandra Bose stressed the importance of military action. Acts of civil disobedience and sabotage had continued since the imprisonment of Gandhi and other

===== Page 51 =====
Nationalist leaders but they were not enough. "Only military action will achieve our purpose" and the time is now ripe for the march on Delhi. The stiffening attitude of the Government of India due to Allied successes in Europe and the economic crises have aroused the hostility of the Indians in British India. He said that reliable sources had informed him that in the event of an invasion of India the Indian Army would desert and join the Indian National Army. He had long discussions with Dr. Ba Maw and come to complete agreement with him on problems of common interest. It was realised that Burma's Independence was not secure till India was free. Dr. Ba Maw had promised men, rice, and other war supplies for the Indian Army of Invasion. As Burma was the logical base for operations against India, Bose planned the removal of headquarters to Burma in the near future and advised all Indians to be ready for the move. He also hoped "that the day is not far off, when we shall be able to set up a provisional Government of Free India. When this is done we shall march into India under the leadership of our own Government." Burma's attainment of Independence should be an inspiration to all Indians, Bose declared. He had witnessed the formation of the new cabinet with Dr. Ba Maw as head, the handing over of all former Anglo-American properties including factories, harbours and public services. He had seen the Rangoon Jail formerly used to hold Indians and Burmese prisoners now converted into a British Prisoners of War Camp. He looked forward to a similar state of affairs in New Delhi. For the realisation of "the second front plan" the mobilisation of at least 300,000 men and women and the collection of $15 million would be necessary. Support so far, though enthusiastic, was inadequate. Bose thanked the Muslim Community for their subscription of a huge purse. This showed that all Indians were united, not, as their enemy the British insinuated, divided. (Domei in English, August 16th). On August 17th in an interview with the Press Bose stated that Ba Maw and Pibul Songgram had assured him of their fullest support. He was returning shortly to Burma to resume preparations. (Tokyo in English, August 18th, 0940 GMT, 19.86 m.)

9. Women's Section of the Indian Independence League for Johore Bharu:
Mrs. A.B.C. Durai has been appointed member in charge of a projected women's section of the Indian Independence League to be formed in Johore Bharu. (Domei in English, August 5th). See also F.I.R. No. 10, Malaya & Sumatra para 13.

10. Dr. Lakshmi & the Indian Women's Movement: In a press interview Dr. Swaminadhan Lakshmi told Roy Ferroa, Domei Staff Correspondent, that the Indian women of Singapore were casting aside family ties to join the Indian Women's Regiment, a Corps of women freedom fighters affiliated to the Indian National Army. She said: "This new regiment when formed will be composed not only of women soldiers but also of others who may be employed as first aid nurses, cooks, orderlies or special staff all serving in the front line. The regiment will not work behind the lines but right in front. When we come into contact with the Indian Army on the other side, the very presence of Indian women armed for the freedom fight will, I am sure, have a very great effect on the British-Indian Army and the moral effect of their presence right on the front line will be very great for those of the Indian National Army fighting side by side with them. The battle cry of the Rani of Jhansi Regiment is "Rani Lakshmibai - die like her." (Domei in English, August 16th). See also F.I.R. No. 11, Malaya and Sumatra, para 9.

ECONOMIC

11. Postal Savings: Postal savings in Malaya passed the one million yen mark on August 20th. When originally started in September last year, the savings averaged 30,000 yen monthly. "In view of the popularity of the thrift movement," the Military Administration has decided to sponsor a thrift week starting from September 1st. Memorial stamps of eight and fifteen cents will be issued. Printed in purple and red respectively, both the stamps carry the recent prize-winning design of a farmer at work against a map of Malaya. Contests will be held for the best thrift posters and slogans. (Domei in English, August 22nd, 23rd and 26th). See F.I.R. No. 10, Malaya & Sumatra, para 17 and 18.

===== Page 52 =====
12. Lottery issue to be increased: The authorities have decided to increase the second issue of lottery tickets to ¥100,000 as the greater portion of tickets issued on August 10th for sale in northern and southern Sumatra have already been taken up. (Domei in English, August 25th). Comment: Lotteries are one of the methods devised by the Japanese to reduce the amount of currency in circulation. See F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 31.

13. Remittances to Japan: Remittances by Japanese residents in Malaya to their families at home aggregated ¥1,828,000 during July. The total amount of these remittances from January to July this year was approximately ¥9,096,000 of which 80% was sent from Singapore. (Domei in English, August 18th).

14. Plans for further Javanese emigration to Sumatra: Batavia: In view of the splendid results attained by the first farming emigrants, 400 Javanese in 100 households, who are cultivating mulberry on reclaimed wild land in Meterosu (sic) and Kanpon (possibly Lampong) districts, the Military have decided to allow 350 households consisting of about 970 "acquaintance" emigrants to enter Meterosu, Kanpon and Batansarii (Batang Hari?) districts during September and October. On entering into Sumatra the emigrants will be provided with 1 bouw (about 7096.5 sq. metres) of untilled land and 1/4 bouw of land for a homestead. (Domei news in Japanese, August 19th). See F.I.R. No.4, N.I., para 13, No.8 para 28.

15. Agricultural propaganda: Malacca: Paddy fields and farm land have been increased respectively by 3,300 and 10,000 acres in the one and a-half years of Japanese Administration, due to the enthusiasm and propaganda of the State Office, the activity of the 36,000 members of the Vigilance Committee, and the inauguration of financial corporations. Sugar cane cultivation is increasing, tapioca production has more than doubled and the production of vegetables one-and-a-half times the pre-war output. Under the British out of 400,000 acres under cultivation, some 200,000 were rubber plantations, 29,000 paddy fields, 19,000 acres farmlands, 13,000 orchards, 40,000 reservations and the rest waste land. The "Increased Production Corps" are in charge of a project sponsored by the State Office to clear 3,500 acres of waste land in two months. 80,000 men are employed on this work. (Domei in Japanese, August 27th).

16. Grow more food campaign: The Chinese and Malay communities of Singapore are holding periodical meetings to encourage the growing of vegetables. The Overseas Chinese Association is donating prizes for farmers producing the best vegetables between now and October. The Agricultural Department has stated that "the acreage under food cultivation has increased fourfold and will further increase twofold." (Domei in English, August 21st).

17. Cockles: In an attempt to improve the quantity and quality of cockles "which were once abundant there", the Government has deposited 3 tons of cockles of the best variety from Taiping in certain specified areas along the Malacca coast. (Domei in English, August 19th). Comment: Shell fish are generally collected and consumed by the Chinese and are of some economic importance.

18. Timber Control: The Military Administration has decided to establish a special council for controlling forestry and the lumber trade throughout Malaya. The organization will comprise forestry experts, owners and dealers in lumber and officials who will control the production and distribution of lumber, firewood and charcoal, the standardization of the products and the fixing of selling prices. It will also conduct extensive research work on afforestation projects. (Domei in English, August 25th). Comment: See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sumatra, para 34 and Appendix I.

===== Page 53 =====
19. Coconut Oil and Palm Oil: An attempt is being made to encourage the use of palm oil as a substitute for coconut oil, "which is vital for war industries," and to this end raised the price of coconut oil to 15 ¢ a lb. and lowered the price of palm oil to 10 ¢. The authorities are taking the necessary measures to increase production of coconut oil. (Domei in English, August 25th). Comment: See also F.I.R. Malaya and Sumatra No.8 para 21 and No.10 para 21. Malaya is one of the world's foremost producing centres for coconut and palm oils. In 1937 Malaya produced 66,769 tons of coconut oil, 210,020 tons of copra and 42,787 tons of palm oil. In the year or two prior to the Japanese invasion of Malaya, the price of Malayan copra had fallen from above $11 per picul to below $4 owing to the ban on export to Axis countries and the shortage of shipping. At this time the price of copra in Thailand varied from $12 to $18 per picul. Siam was the only free supplier of copra to Japan. Coconut oil is used in cooking, manufacture of soaps, etc., and in tin plating where it was supplanting the more costly palm oil as an agent for the removal of sulphuric acid and the prevention of oxidisation in the process of tin plating. It is also used in the manufacture of ammunition in which it is better than palm oil. Hitherto the price of coconut oil has been approximately three-fourths of that of palm oil. The machinery for extracting palm oil is too expensive and the process of manufacture too complicated for the small-holder.

20. Rubber Prices: The Shonan Rubber Association has decided to purchase third-grade rubber for military use "to enable producers of third-grade rubber to maintain their livelihood." Buying and selling prices per lb. offered by the Shonan Rubber Association have been revised as follows:

| Buying   | Selling |
| :------- | :------ |
| 1st grade| 20 ¢    | 22 ¢    |
| 2nd "   | 19 ¢    | 21 ¢    |
| 3rd "   | 11 ¢    | 16 ¢    |
| 4th "   | 10 ¢    | 13 ¢    |
| Scrap not exceeding | 7 ¢ | 9 ¢ |
| Rubber for making oil | not more than 5 ¢ |

(Domei in English, August 25th). Comment: See also F.I.R. Malaya and Sumatra, No.4, para 35, No.6 para 15 and No.7 para 25. Small cultivators, principally Malays, with holdings of less than 25 acres produced about 27% of Malaya's rubber, and Malay and Chinese owners of holdings from 25 to 100 acres produced approximately another 7%. The remaining two-thirds of Malaya's rubber production came from estates, mainly European of over 100 acres. Before the war when the small Malay cultivators received anything approaching satisfactory prices for their rubber, which is of the poorest grade, they tended to neglect the production of rice and other foodstuffs beyond their own immediate needs. See F.I.R. No.1, Malaya and Sumatra, para 18, No.10, para 24.

21. Malacca Industrial Promotion Committee: The Malacca authorities have decided to create an "Industrial Promotion Committee" early in September to include the leading industrialists. The Committee is to promote small-scale domestic industries such as the manufacture of paper, soap, bicycle tyres, hats, wooden footgear, rubber boots, brandy, whisky, ice and tobacco. (Domei in English, August 24th). Comment: Soap was manufactured on a small scale in many of the towns in Malaya - including one factory in Malacca. Wooden clogs were made locally everywhere. Ice was largely a Chinese-controlled industry throughout Malaya except in Kelantan. Tobacco was generally grown throughout Malaya, but the influences of climate and soil tend to deteriorate tobacco grown from good seed. The leaves are cured by the crudest methods of sun-drying with no provision for fermentation.

===== Page 54 =====
Consequently, "tobacco prepared in Malaya is scarcely a trade article." Malayan tobacco is largely locally consumed in cheaply produced cheroots and rokok, the palm-sheath cigarettes of the Malays and Sakai. Production of hats, bags, and baskets woven by Malay women from strands of the mengkuang palm is one of the established kampong industries of Malacca and Port Dickson. See F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra No.2, para 24, No.7, para 21.

22. Wages and Labour Control Committees: On August 16th the Military Administration promulgated an Ordinance governing wages of all workers in Malaya. "The immediate effect will be a considerable improvement in the wages and allowances of manual labourers." A Central Labour Control Committee and Regional Labour Control Committees consisting of representatives of the Military Administration, government officials, and civilians are to be organized. An "all out advance" is to be given to labourers with a knowledge of Japanese. (Domei in English, August 16th). See F.I.R. No.1 Malaya & Sumatra para 10.

23. Survey of Stocks: On August 20th all shops were ordered to report all commodities in their possession within ten days. The object of the proposed survey "is to adjust the supply and demand of daily necessities, so as to stabilise the daily living of the inhabitants." Commodities to be "regulated" include textiles, tyres and tubes for bicycles and automobiles, paper and medicines. (Domei in English, August 20th).

24. Industries in Selangor: Project of Oji Paper Manufacturing Co. Ltd.: Selangor, "the most industrialised State in Malaya" has now 40 types of light industries, "coconut oil and ground-nut oil heading the list." The Agricultural Department is producing soap, as are a number of licensed private factories. Paper from bamboo and local woodpulp is a private enterprise. The Oji Paper Manufacturing Co. proposes to construct a huge paper factory, "to play a vital part in the paper industry in Malaya." (Domei in English, August 17th). Comment: The Oji Paper Manufacturing Co. Ltd., was established in 1872. Its capital is greater than that of all the other 18 pulp and paper Companies in Japan put together. Its annual output is 900,000 tons of paper and 850,000 tons of pulp. Recently there has been a big reduction in the production of paper in Karafuto (Sakhalin), as part of the policy of converting existing plants to heavy industry and transferring light industries and part of the cotton industry to the occupied territories. See also F.I.R. No.5, Japan, para 36. For Selangor industries see F.I.R. No.2, Malaya & Sumatra, para 25, No.6, para 13.

25. Electric Bulb Factory in Singapore: The Tokyo Shibaura Denki Kabushiki Kaisha, manufacturers of Mazda bulbs, will shortly open the first electric bulb factory in Malaya. The factory equipment has already arrived from Japan and is now being installed by experts. The output is intended to cover the needs of Malaya and Sumatra. Electric bulbs used to be imported from other countries "as no encouragement was given to local industry." (Domei in English, August 27th). Comment: The Tokyo Electric Co., established in 1896, were Japan's manufacturers of Mazda products. The Shibaura Engineering Works, founded in 1904, have long manufactured electrical apparatus. The Tokyo Shibaura Electric Co., established in July 1939, with a capital of 87 million yen, as manufacturers of electrical apparatus, may well have absorbed these two concerns. For industrialisation of Singapore see F.I.R. No.7, Malaya & Sumatra, para 20.

26. Japanese goods released in Penang: There was a run on "The Daimaru Department Store and other authorised local retailers" recently when Japanese goods were released for sale. The goods included toilet requisites, enamelware and crockery. (Domei in English, August 24th). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sumatra, para 28 and No.9, para 27.

===== Page 55 =====
27. Communications - Postal Services between Thailand and rest of East Asia resumed: It is reported from Bangkok that on August 15th the general postal service between Thailand and other East Asian countries, including Burma, Malaya, Sumatra, Java, the Philippines, Hong Kong, Borneo, Celebes, the Moluccas and the Lesser Sunda Islands, was resumed. (Tokyo News in German, 013 GMT, Aug. 16th/17th. C.P.D. Monitoring.)

SOCIAL & CULTURAL.

28. Training Shipbuilders: An inaugural class of the Shipbuilders' Training School will be formed on August 31st. Owing to progress in shipbuilding technique the men will graduate in several months less than the scheduled period of one year. (Domei in English, August 26th)
Comment: There were eight shipbuilding yards of any size in Malaya.

29. Fisheries School: A fishery school will be opened at the beginning of October under the guidance of the Fisheries Experimental Station in Malaya to provide free education to local fishermen in Japanese fishing methods. During the six months' course, students will be given free board, lodging, uniform and monthly pocket allowance. (Domei in English, August 21st). Comment: See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sumatra, para 33. A characteristic of the Malayan fishing industry hitherto has been waste owing to lack of suitable refrigeration and transport facilities. In 1937 as much as 16,000 tons was lost out of a catch of 88,500 tons. Before the Japanese invasion the Japanese were the only users of power vessels serving as tenders to fleets of non-powered sampans manned by highly specialised crews and divers who drove the fish into bag nets. The Malayan Fisheries Department was aiming at the elimination of waste and the introduction of power and modern methods of fish preservation. Power craft were under construction for certain regions, such as Kelantan, the intention being to provide a parent vessel to fleets of non-power craft, to secure the catch at sea by applying methods of preservation economically suitable to the region concerned, either refrigeration, canning, cooking or salting.

30. Education: Special Training for Singapore Teachers: The spokesman of the Special Municipality of Singapore revealed in a press conference that all teachers in Singapore are receiving special training in Japanese methods of education during their summer vacation. (Domei in English, August 18th). See also F.I.R. No.2, Malaya & Sumatra, para 38, No.8, para 51.

31. Military Administration's News Organ "Sakura" to be published in Hiragana and Elementary Kanji: Singapore: The Publicity Department of the local Military Administration have announced that from early September their publication Sakura, of which 120,000 copies are distributed twice monthly in Malaya & Sumatra, will be published in Hiragana and elementary Kanji instead of merely in the Katakana hitherto used. Domei remarks that this reflects the enthusiasm and progress of the Indonesian population in Japanese language study. (Domei in English, August 18th). Comment: Modern Japanese is normally written in Kanji (Chinese characters) and the two syllabaries, Katakana and Hiragana. The proportion of kana symbols to the characters can be increased to simplify the written language for foreigners and uneducated Japanese.

32. Anti-Malarial Steps in Sumatra: Steps are being taken to exterminate malaria in Sumatra. The Sanitary Bureau of the East Coast Provinces is founding an institute to train assistant malarial bacteriologists. About 50 local youths will be given two years' training following which they will be sent to hospitals and research institutes in the various provinces. (Domei in English, August 16th). See F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 31.

33. Malay Women's Association: Malay women of Batu Gajah have organized a Malay Women's Association to discuss the welfare of the community and country. Over 140 women were present at the inaugural meeting held in Batu Gajah when the Governor of Perak, Naokata Kawamura, in an address stressed the part to be played by Malay women in wartime. (Domei in English, August 19th). See F.I.R. No.10, Malaya & Sumatra, para 31.

===== Page 56 =====
34. Films for Malaya: Singapore: In connection with the banning of Anglo-American films, Col. Koichi Okubo, Chief of the Publicity Department, stated that the necessary measures had been taken to supplant, as from September 1st, pictures produced by enemy countries. (Domei in English, August 26th). Japanese films supplemented by German, Italian, French, Chinese, Malay, Indian, and Arab films will be shown. The film distributors are experimenting with the "Neinsha" system which will embody a running commentary in Malay or English over the microphone. (Domei in English, August 20th).

35. Musical Appreciation lectures for Ipoh: To enable musicians to appreciate Japanese music, a course of lectures will be given by Makoto Watanabe. Japanese melodies and dances were featured in a variety concert given on August 25th by the women's section of the local branch of the Indian Independence League, in aid of the Subhas Chandra Bose Fund.

23.9.43.
