SHONAN 4/10

ANNOUNCEMENT BY MALAI MILITARY ADMINISTRATION ESTABLISHMENT REGIONAL ADVISORY COMMITTEE ENTHUSIASTICALLY ACCLAIMED BY ALL SECTIONS LOCAL INHABITANTS. LEADERS OF VARIOUS COMMUNITIES ALL PLEASANTLY SURPRISED AS THEY DIDN'T EXPECT ANNOUNCEMENT SO SOON. THEY VIEW IT AS TANGIBLE EVIDENCE OF MILITARY ADMINISTRATION'S CONFIDENCE IN NATIVE INHABITANTS.

ISHAK HAJI MOHAMED, WELL-KNOWN MALAI JOURNALIST, OPINED THAT WITH RESTORATION OF PEACE AND ORDER IN MALAI, IT IS ONLY NATURAL THAT LOCAL INHABITANTS BE ALLOWED TO PARTICIPATE IN ADMINISTRATION'S ACTIVITIES. SAID STEP IS IN ACCORDANCE WITH PREMIER GENERAL TOJO'S PROMISE AT THE EIGHTY-SECOND DIET SESSION THAT THE PEOPLE OF SOUTHERN REGIONS WILL BE ALLOWED TO PARTICIPATE IN ADMINISTRATIVE AFFAIRS OF THEIR RESPECTIVE COUNTRIES.

ANNOUNCEMENT GREETED BY MALAI COMMUNITY AS TRUE EVIDENCE OF CONFIDENCE OF JAPANESE AUTHORITIES IN LOCAL INHABITANTS, ADDED THE MALAI JOURNALIST.

ST. ALSAGOFF, PROMINENT MEMBER OF ARAB COMMUNITY, DECLARED, QUOTE, "THIS IS VERY GOOD" AND "I'M VERY GLAD THE AUTHORITIES HAVE TAKEN IT," UNQUOTE.

S.C. GOHO, INFLUENTIAL MEMBER OF INDIAN COMMUNITY, SAID, QUOTE, "THIS IS A VERY GOOD GESTURE ON PART OF JAPANESE AUTHORITIES AS THIS STEP ALLOWS CIVIL ADMINISTRATION OF COUNTRY IN ACCORDANCE WITH" [POSSIBLY MISSING]

===== Page 68 =====
WISHES OF PEOPLE

C.J. PAGLAR, LEADER EURASIAN COMMUNITY, SAID: "WE ARE DEEPLY GRATEFUL TO JAPANESE GOVERNMENT FOR THIS GESTURE OF CONFIDENCE IN INHABITANTS OF MALAI. THERE CAN BE NO DOUBT THAT THE ESTABLISHMENT OF THE ADVISORY COUNCIL IS THE BEST METHOD OF VOICING SENTIMENTS OF PEOPLE AND ENSURING SMOOTH WORKING OF THE ADMINISTRATION."

MEANWHILE, LOCAL CHINESE LEADERS HAILED THE ANNOUNCEMENT AS VERY WELCOME NEWS INDEED. THEY UNANIMOUSLY OPINED IT WAS AN IMPORTANT STEP TAKEN BY AUTHORITIES AS A REWARD FOR THE PAST WHOLEHEARTED COOPERATION GIVEN BY LOCAL INHABITANTS, AND HOLDS THAT IT WILL PROVIDE FOR GREATER COOPERATION IN THE FUTURE BETWEEN THE PEOPLE AND ADMINISTRATION.

GH KIAT, PRESIDENT OF SHONAN SPORTS ASSOCIATION, DECLARED: "THIS IS A MOVE THAT WILL BRING THE BIGGEST BENEFIT BOTH TO AUTHORITIES AND THE PEOPLE THEY GOVERN. ONE CAN ONLY DO HIS BEST, HIS UTMOST, WHEN HE IS CONVINCED THAT HIS COOPERATION IS WANTED. CHINESE WILL GIVE THEIR VERY BEST TO SUPPORT AND COOPERATE WITH AUTHORITIES."

===== Page 69 =====
FORTNIGHTLY INTELLIGENCE REPORT NO.11.

Far Eastern Bureau,
British Ministry of Information,
Delhi.

Period ended August 14th, 1943.

MALAYA AND SUMATRA.

POLITICAL.

1. Central Council, Singapore: As a result of Premier Tojo's visit to Singapore, a Central Council will be established shortly. This council, to be composed of senior government officials and representatives of the different communities, will advise the Administration. Members of the Council will hold office for a year, but they may be re-elected. The Overseas Chinese Association has been asked to nominate three members. (Batavia in English, August 7th, 1030 GMT, 16.6 m. C.P.D.)

Comment: This appears to be an attempt to emulate the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, for which provision was made in the Constitution of the Colony, declared in Letters Patent, and passed under the great seal of the United Kingdom on the 17th February, 1911. The colony's Legislative power was vested in the Legislative Council, subject to powers exercised by the Secretary of State. The Council was composed of 27 members representing officials and non-officials, and the Governor, its President, was one. This was after 1924 when an amendment was made by which two elected Europeans were added to the non-officials to make them equal in number to the official representatives. The officials still retained the majority as the Governor held the casting vote. In 1941 the composition of the Legislative Council was as follows:

Official Members.

The Governor,
The General Officer Commanding the Troops,
The Colonial Secretary,
The Colonial Treasurer,
The Attorney General,
The Resident Councillors of Penang and Malacca.

2 Nominated Officials
Heads of the Department of Education,
Health and Chinese Affairs

2 other officials, by appointment.

2 Europeans elected by the Chambers of Commerce of Singapore and Penang
3 Europeans (nominated) from Singapore
1 European from Penang
1 European from Malacca
3 Chinese from each of the Straits Settlements.

1 Malay
1 British Indian
1 Eurasian.

2. Malay Mission to Japan: In an interview with Domei, Ishak bin Mohamed, editor of Berita Melayu, a Malay vernacular paper in Singapore, spoke of the progress of the Malays after a year and a half of Japanese administration. He also said that the Malay Mission's violet-coloured banner symbolised loyalty and that this banner which is to be presented to the people

===== Page 70 =====
1. Of Japan will express the goodwill of Malays. (Donei in English, August 4th).
Comment: Ishak bin Mohammed, who is still in his middle thirties, studied at the Malay College, Kuala Kangsar, and joined the Malay Administrative Service, from which he was promoted into the Malayan Civil Service. Later he became editor of Warta Melayu when Ibrahim bin Haji Yacob purchased the paper in August 1941 with Japanese funds. Like Yacob, he was arrested on December 7th, 1941. He wrote a book, "Persatulah Sekarang" (Unite Now!). Berita Melayu may be the new name for the "Warta". (Berita = news, Sanskrit).

3. Japanese Inspection Party to Japan: Led by Soetardjo Karto, twenty Javanese, consisting of lawyers, doctors, journalists, engineers and educationalists, arrived in Singapore en route for Japan to study war-time conditions. The party paid their respects at the Singapore Shrine and the War Memorial at Bukit Timah. A member of the party is Perada Harahap, a veteran Indonesian journalist who visited Japan in 1934; (Donei in English; August 10th, 11th and 13th). See F.I.R. No.4, Borneo, para 5.

4. Burmese Ambassador to Sian in Singapore: U Ba Lwin, the first Burmese Ambassador to Sian, is in Singapore after his visit to Japan. He will go direct to Sian instead of returning to Burma. (Donei in English, August 11th). Comment: See F.I.R. No.5, Burma, para 13.

5. Governors' Speeches: The Governor of Penang, Masakichi Itani, made a speech "to dispel fears arising from disconcerting rumours". The Governor of Malacca stressed that "emancipation of Asiatic peoples depended on Japan's final victory". The Governor of Selangor emphasized the importance of agriculture in reconstruction. (Donei in English, August 13th).

6. Indian Independence League: Subhas Chandra Bose who left on a visit to Sian and Burma on July 25th returned to Singapore on August 11th. (Donei in English, August 12th and 13th). A mass meeting of Indian residents in Johore Bharu will be held on August 15th to observe the first anniversary of Gandhi's "Quit India" ultimatum and to commemorate the imprisonment of Gandhi and other Indian leaders on August 9th, 1942. (Donei in English, August 11th). According to Donei in English, August 2nd, N. Muttutanby, Chairman of the Johore Bharu Branch of the League was to have presided at the rally on August 9th, which was postponed. Comment: Dr. Moothuthanbi, J.P. and Member of the Johore State Council, is a Jaffna Tamil private practitioner aged about 40.

7. Women's Section, I.I.L., Negri Sembilan: Mrs. G.V. Devar was elected Chairman when the Women's Section of the Indian Independence League was inaugurated in Seremban, capital of Negri Sembilan, on August 8th. Mrs. Chidanbaran, President of the Women's Section of the I.I.L., Singapore, addressed the meeting. (Donei in English, August 4th). See F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 11.

8. Indian National Army: A steady stream of recruits from Malaya, Java, Sumatra, Borneo, Hong Kong and Shanghai has joined the Indian National Army in Singapore. (Donei in English, August 1st). A contingent of Indian Volunteers from Tokyo and Yokohama has left Japan for Singapore. (Donei in English, July 31st). See F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 12.

===== Page 71 =====
9. Rani of Jhansi Regiment, I.N.A.: This regiment of Indian women, under the leadership of Dr. S. Lakshmi, is being trained by army instructors from the Indian National Army. Dr. Lakshmi plans to travel to other parts of East Asia to recruit women for the regiment. (Donei in English, July 31st and August 10th). This regiment will make its first public appearance at a parade of the Indian National Army to commemorate the imprisonment of Gandhi. (Donei in English, August 5th).

Comment: Dr. Lakshmi, aged about 30, came to Singapore in 1940 from Madras. She had a private practice and did voluntary work with the passive defence service in Singapore. For Rani of Jhansi see F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 11.

ECONOMIC.

10. Adjustment of New Currency: The Military Administration is encouraging a savings movement as a means of absorbing the new currency which has been put into circulation. Chinese and Indian banks will have their pre-war savings restored from 1st August. On July 20th, 1943, six Chinese banks had total savings of 23 million dollars. (Donei in English, August 11th). Total savings on July 10th in three local Indian banks amounted to $9,730,000 in the new currency, whilst the total in the Japanese-owned banks was $64,480,000. The Postal Savings system, which was started in September 1942, showed a total of $800,000 on June 30th. There is also a postage stamp savings movement for children. Lottery tickets, resold at high premiums, are playing their part in absorbing public money. Lottery tickets to the value of $400,000 were issued for August and it is expected that the total figure for the fiscal year will be $3,000,000. The Administration anticipates an increase to $5,000,000 for the next fiscal year. The administration is endeavouring "to maintain the balance between the volume of currency on the one hand and the available commodities for purchase on the other." (Donei in English, August 11th).

Comment: See F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra, No.2, para 10; No.3, para 15; No.8, para 30.

11. Banks in Malaya: The first banks to do business after the occupation were the Yokohama Specie Bank, the Bank of Formosa, the Bank of China, and the South (Kanan Ginko). Then six Chinese Banks started business on "security provided by the Japanese banks." Later the Southern Development Bank was founded, followed by two Indian Banks, and finally the People's Bank was opened. From September 1st, 1942, the Southern Development Bank became responsible for the issue of bank notes. (Tokyo in Japanese, August 13th, 0800 GMT; 25.37 n.)

Comment: See F.I.R. No.3, Malaya & Sumatra, paras. 12 and 13.

12. Bank Deposits: Since the beginning of the year, bank deposits have shown substantial increases each month. The total deposits in Chinese and Indian Banks in Singapore and the Malay Peninsula amounted to 59,239,000 in June 1942; 93,861,000 in December 1942; 98,840,000 in January 1943; 111,528,000 in February 1943; 125,182,000 in March 1943; 136,462,000 in April 1943; 143,747,000 in May 1943; 152,276,000 in June 1943. (No denomination given). (Donei in English, August 3rd).

===== Page 72 =====
13. Loans by People's Bank, Johore Bharu: This bank has given loans amounting to over 126,000 yen to the local people for the opening of new businesses, repairing houses, cultivating land, etc. (Donei in English, August 3rd). See F.I.R. No.3, Malaya & Sunatra, para 13.

14. Malay Farmers' Post Office Savings: 138 members of a Malay Agricultural colony at Geylang Serai have opened post office savings accounts. Their leader, Inche Onan bin Siraj, will make their deposits each month. (Donei in English, August 4th). Comment: Geylang is an agricultural settlement of small holdings situated between Kallang at the east edge of Singapore city and Changi. The majority of the settlers were Malays and Chinese.

15. Liquidation of Enemy Banks, Sunatra: The Japanese Military Administration has decided to liquidate eight enemy banks, including the Chartered Bank of India, Australia and China, Nederlandsche Handels Maatschappij and the Nederlandsche Indische Handels Bank. The Yokohama Specie Bank will take over the business of these banks. (Donei in English, August 7th). Comment: See F.I.R. No.1, Malaya & Sunatra, para 21.

16. Exchange Transactions between Malaya and Sunatra: The Singapore Military Administration which had prohibited exchange at par of guilders and Straits Dollars on November 7th, 1942, will now establish exchange bureaux in Bengkalis, Selat Panjang, Tenbilahan, Rengat, Bagansiapiapi by September 15th in addition to those in Pakan Baroe, Medan, Padang and Palembang. This exchange arrangement will facilitate the interchange of goods between Malaya and Sunatra. (Donei in English, August 12th). Comment: These five towns are all ports on the East Coast of Sunatra. Bengkalis, Selat Panjang and Tenbilahan are in the Straits of Malacca. Bagansiapiapi, Rengat and Pakan Baroe are river ports.

17. Self-Sufficiency in Sunatra: The Military Administration reports that Sunatra is rapidly becoming self-supporting in foods and other daily necessities. Rice growing is being encouraged on the Western Coast of Atjeh and in Lampong. Cotton was planted in the Lampong and Palembang areas last year and will supply fibre for the cotton spinning industry. Wheat and barley are being grown around Lake Toba. The authorities are encouraging the production of sweet potatoes, wheat, barley and rice for export to Malaya. The scheme for improving the breed of horses is under way. Sunatra's underground wealth is also being prospected. Reports are favourable. (Donei in English, August 1st). See F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sunatra, para 22. Comment: The country around Lake Toba is of varied level and physical character. To the south and north lie plateaux at 4,000 ft. having the character of steppes with scant cover; they are suitable for cattle rearing. The plains of the east and west lie at a lower level and are eroded by large rivers, clothed with forests and show more sawahs (wet rice) and ladangs (dry ricefields) and, near the rivers, are planted with jagang (maize), coffee and fruits.

===== Page 73 =====
18. Rice in Lower Perak: The rice crop in the 30,000 acres in Lower Perak is in excellent condition. Irrigation works in Sungei Manik and Changkat Jong are progressing smoothly and are expected to be completed ahead of schedule. When completed, Lower Perak, the second largest rice-producing district in the State, will help to make Perak self-sufficient in rice. (Donei in English, August 5th). Comment: Sungei Manik, near Telok Anson, Lower Perak, became a useless swamp through the silting of the Batang Padang river, which flows through it. A new channel was dredged and this lowered the water by four feet. About 1935 the Government decided to convert this area into padi land for settling Malay peasants. Gradually the jungle was felled, drainage and irrigation started and the land allotted. The scheme when completed would have provided 25,000 acres of padi land and was capable of extension to 45,000 acres. Changkat Jong is about six miles from Sungei Manik.

19. Japanese Agriculturists invited to Malaya: The Japanese Administration has invited a number of agriculturists from Japan who will be sent to each province to direct agriculture. The Military authorities have already assisted the movement to develop food production by allocating more than 100 officers and men (soldier farmers) to the agricultural districts to supervise the local agriculture. (Donei in Japanese, July 13th). Comment: See F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 25.

20. Coconut Milk for Feeding Infants: Japanese research workers claim that coconut milk supplants cod liver oil as a vitamin B source, and that it is suitable for infant feeding. (Donei in English, August 5th). See F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sumatra, para 14. Comment: Cod liver oil has no vitamin B (possibly a monitoring error). This milk is presumably the white milk-like fluid which comes from the flesh of the nut after it is grated and squeezed. It carries a certain amount of oil in emulsion. The cell sap intended for the use of the seedling is known as milk too. This contains 95% water, holding in solution proteids, sugar and salts. It contains Vitamin A but not C.

21. Fibre Industry in the 5-year Plan: "Several tens of thousands" of idle spindles from Japan are being transferred to Malaya, Sumatra, Java and Burma for spinning cotton, kapok and other fibres for supplying war materials and the requirements of the local inhabitants. This scheme has been under way since April 1943. In Malaya preparations are already in progress for receiving the 20,000 spindles and spinning machines to be transferred within this fiscal year. Former enemy factories have been taken over for this industry and other factories are to be built throughout Malaya. The ultimate aim is to produce "about the same quantity as the pre-war consumption of fibre manufactured goods". To feed these factories the production of cotton, kapok and other fibre plants will be increased. The industry only requires a small proportion of "leading technicians" and native workers will supply the bulk of the necessary labour. (Donei in Japanese, August 3rd, and Donei in English, August 11th).

22. Fibre Industry in Sumatra: Sarongs and clothing material are being manufactured in a factory in Padang. (Donei in English, August 3rd).

===== Page 74 =====
23. Cultivation of Castor Oil Plant, Sumatra: When the necessary preparations are completed, the castor oil plant will be grown at the foot of Bukit Barisan, near Loeboek Aloeng. (Donei in English, August 6th). Comment: The Castor bean was grown as an experimental field crop in Malaya at the Serdang Experimental Station, Selangor. The plant rapidly exhausts the richest soil and is very subject to pests. See F.I.R. No.5, Malaya & Sumatra, para 18.

24. Control of Production Materials: The Military Administration announced and put into force on August 7th, "regulations for the control of production materials", as a fundamental step in promoting the industrial development of Malaya. Iron, copper, lead, and some fifty other commodities are now under control of the Military Administration without whose permission they cannot be transferred, lent or used as securities. The object of this regulation is to limit the supply of commodities from Japan and territories in the Co-prosperity Sphere at the present stage of the war. Points stressed are: (1) The need to control, in order of priority, the local industries started and their steady development. (2) The need to employ and concentrate machinery for the priority industries, and to see that no machine is idle as there is a dearth of machinery at present. (Donei in Japanese, August 7th).

25. Industrial Conference, Sumatra: The Chiefs of the Industrial Department from ten districts in Sumatra held a two days' conference which closed on August 4th. They discussed "commodities for export to Japan, increased production of foodstuffs, distribution of daily necessities, self-sufficiency and utilisation of firms in Sumatra". A quota has been allotted for copra, tin, rubber and palm oil for export to Japan. The machinery for ensuring the smooth distribution of daily necessities will be organised and it will co-operate with the Imports Control Guild. The provinces will concentrate on increasing the area under rice with a view to Singapore's needs. These provinces will also engage in small-scale manufacture of everyday necessities. Agricultural and mining companies will also organise a guild similar to the Imports Control Guild. (Donei in English, August 7th).

26. Chinese Industrialist from Singapore in Shanghai: Aw Boon Haw, at one time the leading Chinese industrialist in Singapore, is now in Shanghai consulting President Wang Ching Wei and Shanghai financiers. Chang Kuang Yen, a journalist and associate of Aw Boon Haw, said that this visit to Shanghai will influence overseas Chinese in Malaya, Burma, Java and Thailand. (Donei in English, August 8th and 9th). See F.I.R. No.1, Malaya & Sumatra, para 30. Comment: Aw Boon Haw was in Hong Kong when the Pacific War broke out. He is a Fukien Hakka, born in Burma. His background is somewhat obscure: He and his brother left Burma for Singapore under a cloud as they were suspected of being engaged in smuggling. He rapidly amassed a fortune through the manufacture of an ointment known as "Tiger Balm". At the height of the depression in 1938 he was reputed still to have a cash reserve of something like $2,000,000 in the Malayan Banks. He made magnificent gifts to the cause of education and charity but always stipulated that they should be publicly acknowledged. He headed a China Relief Fund Committee in rivalry with Tan Kah Kee's Relief Fund Committee. He owned the Sin Chew Jit Poh - a Chinese vernacular morning and evening daily.

===== Page 75 =====
27. Japanese Goods for Singapore and Penang: A varied consignment consisting of rayons, textiles, medicines, glassware, toys, etc., will be available for distribution this month in Singapore and Penang. In Penang the authorities have made three large business establishments responsible for the sale. (Domei in English, August 2nd and 4th). Comment: See F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 27.

28. Electricity for Malaya: An electric light service network for the whole of the Malay Peninsula will be completed by the middle of this month, when the Japanese Generation and Transmission Company will inaugurate a service for Perlis. Since work started on December 15th, 1942, the service in Singapore has been restored almost to pre-war level. Kuala Trengganu's electric service was opened on July 2nd. (Domei in English, August 11th). Comment: Before the Japanese invasion most towns of any size in all the Settlements and States of Malaya were electrically lit except in Trengganu and Perlis, and the Trengganu electrical installation was in process of construction. See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 36.

SOCIAL AND CULTURAL.

29. Imperial Rescript Day in Malaya: Victory rallies will be held throughout the country. (Domei in English, August 6th). In Singapore representatives of every community attended the "Crush the Anglo-Americans" rally which coincided with the observance of Imperial Rescript Day. Dr. Lim Boon Keng presented 100,000 yen from the Chinese community towards a fund for the purchase of war-planes to be named "Singapore Overseas Chinese Planes". Mayor Kanaichi Naito addressed the meeting, and special war films were shown. Amongst the community leaders present were Dr. K.J. Paglar, leader of the Eurasian community and J.A. Thivy. (Domei in English, August 9th). Comment: See F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sumatra, para 32. Dr. Lim Boon Keng, O.B.E., was born over seventy years ago, and is believed to be Malaya's first Queen's Scholar. He studied medicine in Edinburgh and was the first Chinese from a British University to practise in Malaya. He was a member of the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements. At an advanced age he became principal of Anoy University, China, where Lin Yutang was one of his pupils and followers. Subsequently Dr. Lim Boon Keng and Lin Yutang drifted apart because the young man considered that the Doctor put too great an importance of Confucianism as the means for the regeneration of China. Dr. Lim Boon Keng returned to Singapore when the Japanese landed in Anoy. He was on the committee of the "China Relief Fund" and was an ardent Chinese patriot and social worker. His son, Dr. Robert Lin, is head of the Chinese Red Cross in Chungking. A gathering of Malays, Chinese and Indians in Johore Bharu made obeisance in the direction of the Imperial Palace, after which they sang Kinigayo (Japanese national anthem) and the Governor of Johore read the Imperial Rescript. Community leaders promised cooperation with the Administration. (Domei in English, August 9th).

30. Agricultural Training School in Kuala Lumpur: Certificates were presented to 50 graduates; the second batch to pass out of the Agricultural Training School. These graduates will be posted to different parts of Malaya to encourage farmers to adopt modern methods. (Domei in English, August 2nd). In the presence of the Governor of Selangor

===== Page 76 =====
50 new students were enrolled yesterday for a six months' training in the Selangor Agricultural School. (Donei in English, August 12th).

Comment: The principal Agricultural School in Malaya was at Serdang, 12 miles from Kuala Lumpur. This school, for the building of which both the F.M.S. and S.S. Governments contributed, was opened in 1931. A large Agricultural Experimental Station of over 1,500 acres, started in 1921, was attached to the school, and it included a stock farm, a tea factory, and an oil palm mill.

31. Higher Normal Training School, Singapore: At the official opening of this school on August 1st, 40 masters and headmasters were enrolled in the presence of senior government officials. It is understood that the school will later admit women teachers for courses in handicrafts, domestic science, mothercraft, nursing, and general educational methods. (Donei in English, August 2nd).

Comment: Classes for the training of teachers for English schools were first established in Singapore in 1905. Gradually there were classes in the principal towns of the F.M.S. and the Straits Settlements. European instructors trained the students in the theory and practice of teaching, and the three years' course included the English language and literature, art, hygiene and physical training. From 1919, promising candidates were given scholarships which enabled them to go to Hong Kong University; but after Raffles College was opened in 1928, the scholarship students went there for their training. See F.I.R. No.10, Malaya & Sumatra, para 36.

32. Malay Students in Japan: Five Malay students - two girls, Che Yahya, aged 21 and Rosna Mahyudin, aged 15, and three young men, Ungku Abdul Aziz, 22; Ungku Muhsin, 21, and Wan Abdul Hanid, 21 - stated in a press interview that they are already able to speak Japanese. These students, who, since their arrival from Shonan in January have been under the care of Marquis Yoshichika Tokugawa and other Japanese officials, have adapted themselves to the Japanese way of life. The girls were enrolled at an accredited school in April and can now write the Japanese syllabary. They are also having lessons in Japanese art. The three young men are relatives of the Sultan of Johore.

33. Training Youths in the Martial Arts: A gigantic hall patterned on the Butokuden in Tokyo is to be built in Singapore. In this hall Japanese experts will train Singapore youths in "judo, fencing, archery, and other arts of the Samurai". It is understood that another hall will also be built for the training of girls. (Donei in English, August 11th).

Comment: Judo is a form of Japanese wrestling.

34. Menangkabau Tribe's Gift acknowledged: In March this year the Menangkabau people sent gifts to the Japanese Emperor. These were formally acknowledged by the Japanese authorities at a ceremony held at the Governor's official residence in Padang yesterday. Marquis Yoshichika Tokugawa, Adviser to the Japanese Military Administration in Malaya, and Governor Kenyo Yano of the West Province of Sumatra were present. (Donei in English, August 13th).

35. Kuala Lumpur Shinto Shrine: The shrine to the Japanese war dead will be in Bungsar Estate in the outskirts of Kuala Lumpur. (Hong Kong News, February 28th, 1943).

Comment: Bungsar, a very hilly estate, is about three miles from the Kuala Lumpur Post Office and was one of the estates belonging to "Socfin", a big French rubber and oil palm company, with interests in Malaya, F.I.C., Sumatra, and Africa. See also F.I.R., No.6, Malaya & Sumatra, para 3.

===== Page 77 =====
CONFIDENTIAL.

FORTNIGHTLY INTELLIGENCE REPORT NO. 10.

Period ended July 31st, 1943.

MALAYA AND SUMATRA.

POLITICAL.

1. Cession of four Malay States: A preparatory Committee for taking over Kedah, Perlis, Trengganu and Kelantan has met three times since the first conference on July 17th. The general principles of handing over have been agreed upon. The Siamese Foreign Ministry at the plenary session of the Siamese Assembly stated that the Japanese asked absolutely nothing in return but Siam's cooperation. (Domei in English, July 22nd and 23rd). Comment: See F.I.R. No.9, Malaya & Sumatra, para.1.

2. Kanichi Naito, Mayor of Singapore, whose appointment was announced last October, assumed office on July 19th at 08.30 GMT. He succeeded Shigeo Odachi who has been made Governor of Tokyo metropolis. Naito is a graduate of the Tokyo Imperial University and joined the Home Ministry. He was appointed Governor of Ibaraki Prefecture in 1941. Previous to that, amongst other posts, he was Chief of the Wakayama Prefecture Police Bureau and Sectional Chief of the Home Ministry Police Bureau. He had been Director of the Welfare Bureau of the Japanese Military Administration in Malaya and later Director of the Industry Bureau. As Chief of this Bureau, Kanichi Naito was largely responsible for drawing up the scheme for the Five Year Industrial Plan for Malaya. On July 27th the new Mayor of Singapore invited Malay, Chinese and Indian leaders of the city to see him and asked for their collaboration in the administration of Singapore. (Domei in English, July 19th and 27th).

3. Malay Mission to Japan: A party of Malay leaders from Malaya and Sumatra left Singapore on July 28th for a two months' inspection tour of Japan under war conditions. They were given an official farewell luncheon by the Military Administration at the Tonan Club. The Malays from Singapore and the Malay States are led by Ibrahim bin Haji Yacob, and the Sumatran Malays by Mohamed Shafei. (Domei in English, July 18th, 19th and 29th). Comment: Ibrahim bin Haji Yacob was born in Pahang and has Rhio connections. He graduated from the Sultan Idris Training College, Tanjong Malim, Selangor. He was on the teaching staff of the Kuala Lumpur Police Depot. After this he took up journalism and became sub-editor of "Majlis", a vernacular Malay paper in Kuala Lumpur. Later he became sub-editor of "Utusan Melayu", a Malay vernacular paper in Singapore. Finally, in 1941, he became owner of "Warta Melayu", which had formerly belonged to Syed Hussein bin Ali Alsagoff. This purchase was proved to have been subsidised by the Japanese. Ibrahim bin Haji Yacob is the moving spirit of the "Kesatuan Melayu Muda" (Malay Youth Movement), a nationalist movement preaching nationalist and Pan-Asian propaganda. This movement originated in Selangor in about 1938 and was registered in Kuala Lumpur in 1940. Later the movement spread all through the country. The majority of members were educated Malays and there was a great deal of dissension in the various camps between the extremists and the moderately inclined. Ibrahim bin Haji Yacob was subversively engaged in Japanese propaganda and worked hard to foster the idea that the Malays would gain independence through Japanese help. He was

===== Page 78 =====
Put under arrest on December 7th, 1941. Mohamed Shafei, aged about 50 years, is a prominent Indonesian scholar and musician. He studied music and art in Europe. On his return to Sumatra, he established the "Sekolah Nasional" (National School) in Kajoe Tanam, Minangkabau. Here Sumatran and other youths of Indonesia were trained in music, art, and handicraft. This school was run on the same lines as Rabindranath Tagore's Shantiniketen. The school was self-supporting. Shafei never touched politics though he was an ardent nationalist and did all in his power to promote the art and culture of his country. He is held in high esteem by the people of Sumatra.

4. General Affairs Department Conference, Singapore: A two-day session started on July 20th. The Chief of the General Affairs Department and section chiefs reported on their various departments and schemes were discussed for simplifying the administrative system and improving economic self-sufficiency. (Domei in English, July 20th).

5. Japanese Officials for the South: Graduates from Japanese universities and technical schools under the age of 35 are eligible for service in the Southern Regions after training in the Singapore training department. Ninety students are expected to graduate at the end of September. (Tokyo in Japanese, July 22nd, 0800 GMT, 25.37 m.)

6. Southern Development National Service Association, Singapore: A young people's department of this Association was inaugurated on June 22nd on the sports ground opposite the Municipal Offices. President Itoya addressed the 200 young Japanese residents who attended. (Tokyo in Japanese June 22nd, 0200 GMT.)

7. Journalists from Siam Visit Malaya: A party of newspaper reporters under the leadership of Nai Chalerm Wudikosit of the Siamese Publicity Bureau arrived in Singapore on July 23rd for a tour of Malaya. Amongst the journalists are Carl Meljer, a Trans-Ocean News representative, and Nai Isara of the Press Section of the Department of Publicity. Nai Chalerm Wudikosit, leader of the party, is the editor of Pramuan Wan. They were entertained by the publicity section of the Japanese Military Administration in Malaya, which also arranged their itinerary. The party called on the military authorities and on chiefs of the Administration. The party left for Kuala Lumpur on July 29th, and, after visiting Cameron's Highlands and Penang, will return to Thailand. (Domei in English, July 24th, 25th and 29th). Comment: Pramuan Wan is a daily paper. Before the Pacific War this paper was Anglophile. It is run by a company and now, like all Siamese newspapers, it is under strict Government censorship.

8. Vice-Speaker of the Japanese House of Representatives visits Malaya: Sakasaburo Uchigasaki, Vice-speaker of the House of Representatives, arrived in Singapore on the 27th July to visit the Japanese forces in Malaya. He was accompanied by a party. (Domei in English, July 27th). Comment: Sakasaburo Uchigasaki was born in 1877. A graduate of Tokyo Imperial University and Manchester College, Oxford, he was a professor in Waseda University. Six times elected member of the Lower House from Miyagi Prefecture, he was Parliamentary Vice-Minister of Education in 1937-1939, and Chief Secretary of the former Minseito Party.

===== Page 79 =====
9. Peace Preservation Corps: The Governor of Penang, Masakichi Itami, reviewed the Penang Corps of over a thousand strong and urged their cooperation with the police for the maintenance of peace and order in the streets and villages in which they lived. (Domei in English, July 23rd). He also visited Butterworth and inspected members of the Peace Preservation Corps there. He is reported to have sent a gift of money to the family of a Malay member of the Corps who was killed on his way to parade. (Domei in English, July 26th). See F.I.R. No. 9, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 15.

10. Indian Independence League, Singapore: Subhas Chandra Bose paid an official call on the newly appointed Mayor of Singapore on July 24th. (Tokyo in English, July 25th, 0905 GMT, 19.86 m.) See F.I.R. No. 9, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 10.

11. Indian Muslims: A.K. Kassim, the Penang Indian Muslim representative, has returned to Penang after seeing Subhas Chandra Bose in Singapore. He said that the Indian Muslims in Singapore had decided to support fully the Indian Independence League (Domei in English, July 24th). A. Yellapa, President of the Singapore Branch of the Indian Independence League, stated that it was the duty of Indian patriots in essential war jobs to continue to support and cooperate with the Japanese authorities. (Domei in English, July 21st). See F.I.R. No. 9, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 13.

12. I.I.L. Selangor: Rama Chandra: Rama Chandra, aged 48, from Tanjore, Madras, was elected President of the Selangor Indian Independence League at a regular general meeting on July 25th. (Domei in English, July 26th). Comment: L.R. Chandra was a lawyer in the firm S.C. Goho & Co., Klyne Street, Kuala Lumpur. He was a member of the deputation sent by the All Malayan Indian Association in 1939 to make representations to the Government of India regarding treatment of Indian labour, the purpose being to stop indentured labour.

13. I.I.L. Women's Section, Selangor: A women's section of the Indian Independence League was inaugurated in Kuala Lumpur, and four prominent Indian women who have formed a provisional committee will recommend suitable women for the permanent committee. J.A. Thivy spoke at the meeting. It is thought that the formation of this section of the League will stir up enthusiasm for the cause and raise funds for charity. (Tokyo in English, July 25th, 0905 GMT, 19.86 m. and Domei in English, July 26th). See F.I.R. No. 2, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 8.

14. I.I.L. Women's Section, Perak: A women's section of the Indian Independence League was inaugurated in the Ipoh Town Hall. Mrs. J.A. Thivy was elected president and her husband was present at the meeting. (Domei in English, July 30th). Indian youths led by Chowdhari, leader of the Youth Section, Indian Independence League, held a mass meeting in Telok Anson. J.N. Bhadari, President of the Telok Anson Branch of the League spoke, and at the end of the meeting many youths volunteered for the Army. (Domei in English, July 29th). Comment: Telok Anson in Lower Perak is the centre of a large rubber and coconut growing district. It is situated on the Perak river, down which all produce goes to the port of Bagan Datoh. It is also connected by rail to the main Singapore-Penang line. There is a large Indian (Tamil) population in the district, mostly estate labourers.

===== Page 80 =====
15. Indian National Army: Subhas Chandra Bose presented certificates to graduates from the Officers' Training School on the 20th July. He spoke of the activities of the Indian National Army in Greater East Asia as a "second front" in the Indian cause. (Domei in English, July 20th). See F.I.R. No. 9, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 12. A new training school, run on the same lines as the Azad, is to be opened shortly for volunteers for the Indian National Army. See F.I.R. No. 8, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 12. 30 out of 1000 applicants have passed the entrance examination for the school. (Domei in English, July 30th). The training camp of the Indian National Army is under the charge of Dr. M.K. Lakshmayya. (Domei in English, July 29th). Comment: Dr. M.K. Lakshmayya was a medical practitioner in Kuala Lumpur. He took an active part in Malayan Indian affairs and had been President of the Central Indian Association.

ECONOMIC.

16. Chinese Banks in Singapore: Five Banks, the Sze Hai Tong, the Overseas Chinese Banking Corporation, the Lee Wah Bank, the United Chinese Bank and the Ban Hin Bank are making an official release on the 1st August "of the balance of 50% of depositors' original blocked accounts". (Domei in English, July 19th). Comment: Early in 1933, when Malaya was in the throes of a great slump, three Chinese Banks, namely, the Ho Hong Bank, Ltd., the Chinese Commercial Bank, Ltd., and the Overseas Chinese Bank, Ltd., decided to amalgamate into one large Bank, the Overseas Chinese Banking Corporation, Ltd. Its Chairman, Chee Swee Cheng, the representative of a long line of families which had made their mark on the industrial life and progress of Malaya steered the bank through those difficult days until his death in 1938. Just before the outbreak of hostilities in the Pacific the total deposits amounted to $80,000,000 and were actually larger than those of the Chartered Bank of India, China and Australia, Ltd. Eighty percent of these deposits were in liquid form, so that, in case of a run on the Bank it would still be able to fulfil all its obligations. The Directors included Lee Kong Chian (the present Chairman) who came to Malaya with about $3.00 in his pocket some 20 years ago, and was worth something like $20,000,000 before the Japanese invasion; Chan Kang Swi, who founded and practically owned the whole town of Muar, Johore; Yeap Chor Be, the biggest shareholder, reputed to be worth over three million sterling, and to own streets of houses in Penang; Tan Cheng Lock, G.B.E., Member of Legislative Council 1923-34 and Executive Council 1933-35, who is also a Director of 20 other industrial companies in Malaya; S.Q. Wong, a Director of 30 other companies in Malaya; Aw Boon Par, brother to Aw Boon Haw of "Tiger Balm" fame, and others. The Bank owns the Eastern Realty Company, Ltd., which was formed from rubber plantations mortgaged to and foreclosed by the Bank, and the Chinese Bankers' Trust Co., Ltd. The issued capital is Straits $10,000,000 and the profit for the year before Pearl Harbour was over $1,000,000 net and if the bank had been allowed to continue its operations for another year the net profit was expected to be in the region of $2,000,000. Since the Pacific War an office has been opened in Sydney, Australia, under the management of Tan Chin Tuan, Joint Managing Director, and another office has been opened in Chungking. There is a possibility of this bank opening a branch in India. Of the group of Directors listed above, Chan Kang Swi evacuated to Kunming and died there, Tan Cheng Lock is in Bangalore, Tan Chin Tuan in Australia, Lee Kong Chian in the U.S.A., Aw Boon Par left for Rangoon with his family but his whereabouts are now unknown.

===== Page 81 =====
17. Savings Campaign in Penang: Post Office savings have shown an increase of more than 10,000 yen in less than three weeks since the start of the "Save your money" campaign. (Domei in English, July 20th). Comment: See F.I.R. No. 1, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 22.

18. Savings Campaign in Kedah: Students from the Police School in Kedah have volunteered to save a fixed sum monthly to help in the savings drive. (Domei in English, July 17th).

19. A Three Year Agricultural Plan for Perak: To stimulate food production and to increase the yield of crops, a two-day conference was held in Ipoh of agricultural officials in Perak. (Domei in English, July 19th). Comment: See F.I.R. No. 2, Malaya & Sumatra, paras. 15 and 16; No. 3, para. 21; No. 4, para. 39.

20. Agricultural Colony for Penang Chinese Community: 12,000 acres of land in Province Wellesley have been allocated to three thousand Chinese families from the crowded town areas of Penang. This new colony, which will be started on Bertam Estate at Kepala Batas, will farm, grow rice and engage in village industries. (Domei in English, July 20th). Comment: Bertam Estate, European-owned and situated on the border of Kedah and Province Wellesley, was a rubber estate on flat land. The rubber trees have presumably been felled for firewood and the extension of rice land.

21. Flour Substitutes in Singapore: Bread and other foods can in future be made from a flour-like substance obtained from the residue left after oil is extracted from palm nuts. (Domei in English, July 22nd). Comment: The oil palm, a native of West Africa, was originally planted in Malaya as an ornamental tree. In 1912 the Agricultural Department and various rubber companies in Selangor planted several acres of the palm as an experimental crop. Henri Fauconnier of "Malaisie" fame planted the first oil palm estate in Malaya, in Selangor, in 1917, but it was not until 1926 that there was any great increase in the area under oil palm. The industry was almost entirely in European hands and of the total area of over 72,000 acres planted, about half was in Johore, and Messrs. Guthrie and Soefin Co., Ltd., were the largest owners. Soefin Co., Ltd., was a big French rubber and oil palm combine which absorbed Compagnie du Selangor for whom Fauconnier worked. In 1938 Malaya produced over 51,000 tons of palm oil. The palm fruit produces two oils, palm oil from the pulp of the fruit and palm kernel oil. Before the war there was a great demand for palm oil in England, Germany and U.S.A. for the tin plate industry. Very like coconut oil, the kernel oil is used for the same purposes and both oils are used in the manufacture of soaps, candies and margarine, and as fuel for internal combustion engines. Feeding cake for cattle is made from the kernel after the oil has been extracted. The palm oil has the same nutritional value as cod liver oil, and it was extensively used throughout Malaya in clinics, hospitals and schools for that purpose.

22. Flour manufacture in Kuala Lumpur: The manufacture of flour in Kuala Lumpur has been encouraged by the food control department. Manufacturers have made flour from maize, bran and tapioca. (Domei in English, July 22nd). Comment: Tapioca and maize flour have always been handmilled in Malaya.

===== Page 82 =====
And Chinese homes for certain foods. There was no need for milling on a big scale as the flour needs of the country were supplied by imports of wheat flour. The fact that bran and "palm oil residue" now used seems to indicate that maize and tapioca are insufficient to replace the wheat flour previously imported for bread-making. Certain sections of the Indian community depended entirely on flour for their staple food.

23. Food and Clothes Shortage in Sumatra: Several areas, particularly in North Sumatra, are suffering from food and clothes shortages. The Japanese attribute this to the Dutch, who, they say, neglected rice plantations and village industries. (Domei in English, July 24th).

Comment: "The N.E.I. Government's policy has been one of fostering local production for local needs. For a dense population with little capital there is only one way to adapt its agriculture to the necessities of life, i.e., the cultivation for its own use of foodstuffs which give a high yield." ("Western Enterprises and the Density of Population in the N.E.I." Van Gelderen). 70% of the adult male population of the N.E.I. were engaged in agriculture, mostly for local food supply. (See Appendix I for N.E.I. food and clothing statistics).

All of the rice imports of the N.E.I. came from Burma, Siam, and French Indochina. It is interesting to note that in 1929, 153,000 tons of rice were handled in Singapore for the N.E.I., and that this figure dropped to 81,000 tons in 1939.

With reference to clothes, in 1939 the Netherlands supplied 17,430,000 kg of cotton goods to the N.E.I. as compared with Japan's 59,023,000 kg. In 1940, the Indies produced 128 million metres of cloth or about one seventh of the total consumption. This production included cottage industry.

24. Rubber: The Military Administration has reduced the price of rubber to "14" - "15" cents (Straits currency) per lb. (Tokyo in Japanese, July 26th, 1000 GMT, 25.37 m.)

Comment: Before the Japanese invasion, standard ribbed smoked sheet was 42 cents per pound and native rubber (unsmoked) 32 cents per pound. See F.I.R. No. 1, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 18.

"Owing to about 15 cents per lb. reduction in price, the production of rubber goods will be accelerated. Crepe soles will be manufactured on a large scale as a substitute for leather soles. Scrap and surplus rubber will be manufactured into substitute gasoline and anti-malarial oil." (Tokyo in English, July 27th, 0905 GMT, 19.86 m.)

Comment: A Mandarin teacher of a large Chinese school in Penang reports that up to April 1942, "the public was not allowed to get a single drop of petrol from the Japanese".

The 1940 production of oil from the area now under Japanese control was 86 million barrels: - the N.E.I., Sarawak, and Brunei, 68 million; Burma, 7 million; Japanese domestic production, 9(?) million; Sakhalin, 2 million, making 86 million in all. (About 72 barrels = 1 ton).

Japan's pre-Pacific war needs were 45 million barrels, so she would need more than half of the above to meet her pre-war needs. In 1940 she imported 40 million barrels, and 60% of this came from the United States of America.

The N.E.I. output before the war represented 3% of the world's production but the capacity of her wells was large enough to meet the needs of the Far East. If Japan has restored 50% of the demolished installations in the N.E.I., Sarawak, Brunei, and Burma, she will have only enough oil for her pre-war purposes.

The fact that she considers manufacturing substitute gasoline and anti-malarial oil for Malayan use rather belies her vague and optimistic pronouncements regarding the re-opening of oil wells and may imply shortage of both oil and tankers and conservation of supply for war needs.

One third of Japan's purchases in the U.S.A. was carried by chartered ships, mostly British.

===== Page 83 =====
and Norwegian. She had a tanker tonnage of only 440,000 tons in 1940. Rubber oil is also suitable for cooking, lamp oil, and, when mixed with linseed oil and perilla, for paint-making. "With the production of this oil, the quantity of lamp oil (which is rationed) available to native people will be increased." (Tokyo in Japanese, July 27th, 0600 GMT, 25.37 m., and Domei in English, July 27th). Comment: Perilla Ocinoides LINN. is cultivated in Northern India, Manchuria, Japan and China. The oil extracted from the seeds is used for cooking, for burning and a little in the Arts. Being a drying oil it is useful for varnishes. In Japan it is used for mixing with cheapest lacquers, for waterproofing paper and initiating leather. (References: I.M. Burkill and Chambers). Malaya has never lacked vegetable cooking oil. The need was supplied by ground nuts, coconuts and oil palms.

25. Tanning Industry: A Sino-Malay syndicate encouraged by the Government has started a tannery near Tanjong Rambutan (Perak) for curing skins of iguanas and hides of cattle. (Domei in English, July 27th). Comment: Before the war the curing of reptile skins was mainly in Japanese hands. The business of curing hides can only be a limited industry. Cattle raising was on a very small scale as Malaya has no natural pastures. Most of the open spaces grow lalang, a rank grass which cattle dislike. Imported fodder grasses have little chance against the vigorous local grasses unless grown in rows and weeded. Before the war live cattle were imported from Siam, India, Bali and Australia for slaughter. Monitor lizards were protected in Kelantan where specimens up to 3 ft. long were commonly seen and did havoc to poultry.

26. Pineapple Fibre: Mitsui Bussan Kaisha are experimenting with pineapple fibre for making canvas for sails. (Domei in English, July 17th). Comment: Mitsui Bussan Kaisha, general exporters and importers with head offices in Tokyo, was established in 1876. They are also insurance and shipping agents, general contractors, ship owners and builders, saw-mill owners and wharfingers. They had branches all over the world and were well-known in Burma, Malaya, Siam and the N.E.I. See also F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra, No. 1, para. 25; No. 3 para. 27; No. 4 para. 46.

27. Iron and Steel Industry: The Nippon Iron and Steel Manufacturing Company and the Nippon Steel Tube Manufacturing Co., are preparing to utilise iron ore in Malaya. The production of pig iron will be facilitated by the plentiful charcoal supply. These Companies are also studying the possibilities of the Steel industry in Malaya. Teikoku Steel Manufacturing Company will co-operate with the Nippon Steel Company and the Osaka Special Steel Company with the Nippon Steel Tube Company. The Nippon Steel Company and the Nippon Steel Tube Company are already functioning. (Tokyo Domei in English, to East Asia, June 24th, 2200 GMT). See F.I.R. Malaya & Sumatra Index Nos. 1 - 6, Iron and Charcoal. Also Appendix II of this report.

28. King George V Dock, Singapore, refloated: Japanese engineers and mechanics aided by thousands of Chinese, Malays and Indian workers have salvaged this dock in three months and it is expected to be in commission shortly. (Domei in English, July 22nd). Comment: Situated at the Naval Base Seletar, Singapore, this dock is about 900 ft. long and 200 ft. wide and is capable of lifting any battleship afloat. It is the third largest floating dock in the world. Built in England, it was towed

===== Page 84 =====
Out in sections from Wallsend-on-Tyne in the autumn of 1929. The Queen Mary, 81,235 tons, was in this dock for intensive repairs in October 1941, and it was thought then that the dock was fully capable of lifting the Queen Elizabeth, 83,000 tons.

29. Communications: Malaya's railway timetable will be revised from August 1. "Trains will leave Singapore for Bangkok at 1600 GMT, instead of 0314 GMT. Henceforth, trains leaving Singapore on Wednesdays and Saturdays will arrive in Bangkok on Tuesdays and Fridays. Trains leaving Bangkok on Wednesdays and Saturdays will arrive in Singapore on Tuesdays and Fridays. The Express will now stop at twice as many stations as before." (Domei in English, July 28). Comment: Before the Japanese invasion, there were two trains a week from Singapore to Bangkok and vice versa. Trains leaving Singapore on Sundays and Thursdays arrived in Bangkok on Tuesdays and Saturdays, and trains from Bangkok on Wednesdays and Saturdays arrived in Singapore on Fridays and Mondays. It was a journey of 48 to 50 hours. From Padang Besar on the Perlis-Siamese border to Bangkok the line was diesel-electric. The new express timetable seems inordinately lengthy. There is the possibility that the section from Padang Besar to Bangkok is now running on coal because of the shortage of fuel oil. There was a marked reduction in rolling stock. The public was allowed to use the railway for traveling only, and fares were the same as before. All nationalities worked to restore communications and were paid 60 cents per day. See F.I.R. No.1, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 10.

30. Singapore Labour Bureau: 6,000 people are supposed to have found employment through this Bureau since it was started last April by the Special Municipality. (Domei in English, July 23). See F.I.R. No. 3, Malaya & Sumatra, paras. 11 and 33. Comment: The same source as above reveals that most of the Eurasians and English-educated section of the Malay and Chinese population were out of work with little chance of obtaining employment from the Japanese. Some of these must have been from the educational services as all English schools were closed down.

31. Malay Office Girls: For the first time in the history of Johore, more than 50 Malay girls from comfortable homes have taken appointments in Government and commercial offices. (Domei in English, July 28). Comment: Every encouragement was given to Malay girls to enter the Educational and Medical Departments as teachers, nurses and midwives. The Malays were sympathetic towards employment of girls in the Welfare and Social Services, but not in offices. They did not like their womenfolk to mingle freely with men.

32. Japanese Goods for Sumatra: Bicycles, electric bulbs, matches, medicines, and other Japanese goods are now on sale in Sumatra. (Domei in English, July 29). Comment: Compare F.I.R. No. 9, Malaya & Sumatra, para 27 and No. 8, para. 28.

SOCIAL AND CULTURAL.

33. Passive Defence Week in Singapore: The Military Administration of Singapore held the first full-scale Passive Defence exercises on July 28. On this last day of Defence Week, which started on July 21, members of the

===== Page 85 =====
General public, the police and the auxiliary police forces took part. (Domei in English, July 28th). Comment: Allied aircraft bombed Macassar (990 miles from Singapore) on July 20th and Sourabaya (760 miles from Singapore) on July 22nd. The raid on Macassar may well have been the reason for these exercises. The Sourabaya dock area was lit up when the Liberators arrived.

34. Health of Singapore: The Military Administration claim that not a single case of malaria or dengue fever has been reported this year. Compulsory inoculations for malaria, typhus and dysentery and vaccinations for smallpox have been carried out. (Domei in English, July 20th). Comment: In 1941 Singapore was one of the healthiest cities in the tropics. This was the result of a health policy that had laid emphasis on preventative rather than curative measures for over forty years. The Municipality's and Government Medical Department's allocation of funds always gave preference to places where the largest number of people would benefit. The Municipality was responsible for the health of the urban areas, and the Government Health Department for the port, rural areas and schools. So strict were the port regulations that there was comparative freedom from infectious diseases. Smallpox was virtually absent since 1922, and cholera last appeared in 1928. Injections against typhoid, dysentery and cholera were given to the general public after the Japanese invasion.

35. Ipecacuanha shipped to Japan: Several scores of tons of dried ipecacuanha will be sent to Japan for the manufacture of the drug emetine which is used in the treatment of dysentery. Research by Japanese reveal that the drug is obtainable from the leaves and stem. (Domei in English, July 21st). Comment: "Cephaelis Ipecacuanha" was introduced from Ceylon into the Singapore Botanical Gardens in 1875 and was grown in Negri Sembilan in 1877. Pengerang Estate in South Johore grew the plant in 1885 and marketed it in 1887. Later the whole stock of plants from Pengerang Estate was moved to Selangor, where cultivation continues to the present day. The cultivation of ipecacuanha requires great care. It owes its curative property to the presence of rather more than 1% of alkaloid emetine which, with the exception of traces, occurs only in the cortical portion of the root. B.J. Eaton, Chief Chemist of the Agricultural Department, Kuala Lumpur, in 1921 found 0.45% emetine in a sample of leaf plucked. Before the war ipecacuanha was marketed from Malaya.

36. Higher Normal School in Singapore: The new Mayor of Singapore will attend his first public function when this school is declared open on August 1st. The Koa Kunrenjo (leading officers' training school) will be transferred to Kajang, and their building in Singapore will be taken over by the Higher Normal Training School. Three courses of study will be available to the native (Malay) students, a full three years' course for Malay school teachers and a year's course and a half year's course for training teachers in elementary schools. K. Sunasawa, Principal of this new school stated that it was the aim of the school to instil the Japanese spirit and method of education into the students. (Domei in English, July 26th). Comment: The Government English School, Kajang, (14 miles from Kuala Lumpur) was a large modern building just outside Kajang on the main road south to Singapore. This is the most likely place for the transfer of the Koa Kunrenjo.

===== Page 86 =====
37. Officials Training Centre, Singapore: Eighteen youths from Malaya and Sumatra, the first batch selected for training as future leaders of their people, graduated from this school on July 23rd. Important government jobs will be given to the five Malacca students who have trained at this institute on their return to Malacca. (Domei in English, July 23rd). See F.I.R. No.8, Malaya & Sumatra, para. 44.

38. Singapore Nautical School: This school held a commemoration ceremony in honour of Marine Day. (Domei in English, July 20th).

39. Catholics in Malaya: Monseigneur Adrian Devals, Bishop of Malacca, and Reverend Father M. "Cordoza", Superior of the Portuguese Mission in Singapore, expressed indignation at the bombing of Rome. (Domei in English, July 20th). Comment: The Rev. Father Coroado came to Malacca in 1909 and has been there continuously since.

40. Mobile Film Shows: Between August 1st and 10th the local inhabitants of the southern part of Malaya are to see films depicting armament production and industrial and other war-time activities in Japan. (Domei in English, July 21st).

41. Malacca Athletic Meeting and Concert: The Military Administration and the Japanese Residents Association promoted a sports contest and concert in which Japanese and local youths took part. (Domei in English, July 23rd).

42. Suno Contest: Masakichi Itami, Governor of Penang, witnessed local young men, students of the Penang Officers' Training Institute, who had taken part for the first time in Malaya in a Suno contest. (Domei in English, July 22nd).

APPENDIX II.

Note on charcoal producing methods in Malaya by Major Colin Marshall.

The production of charcoal in Malaya has in general been on a very small scale. The best charcoal comes from mangrove wood, though this suffers from the defect that if used in steam engines, the tubes may be corroded by the salt in the wood. Charcoal burning can be divided into three types. First, ordinary pit burning, which means that the charcoal is stacked in a shallow pit in the forest near the place where the wood is cut, covered with a thin layer of earth and decayed leaves, and ignited to char slowly. The yield of this type of burn is usually poor, and a considerable proportion of the wood is not burned into charcoal and therefore is wasted. The second type of charcoal producer is a similarly constructed kiln of bricks or (in the metal portable type) of iron. The bricks may be either burnt brick or merely dried mud slabs. In this, the charcoal is produced from wood cut some distance away and brought to this central point for conversion. The by-products are wasted, and though the out-turn of charcoal is good, it is not the most efficient. It is, however, the method that was in general use in Malaya in the mangroves.

The last and most modern method is to heat the wood in a retort and to collect the by-products (pyroligneous acid - a mixture of acetic and other similar acids with tar). This was used by the Batu Arung Collieries and is very similar to the production of coke and gas from coal.

Mangrove trees take forty years to reach their optimum size. Thus, to ensure an equal annual out-turn from any area, it is essential that one-fortieth of the area shall be subjected to fellings in any one year. Thus, after forty years the falling cycle will return to the first area on which the young seedlings will be now forty years old. The British policy in Malaya has been to work all forests so as to ensure a sustained yield. The Siamese mangrove forests north of Penang were over-cut and ceased to yield in 1932. From that date till 1940, there was a great emigration of charcoal burners to the Matang mangroves of Perak until there were approximately 160 kilns in use. One kiln turns out about 70 piculs per burn, and each burn takes about a month.

Inland charcoal is burnt only in a small way, usually by the first and most inefficient method. It was of very little importance in 1941 but is capable of great expansion should it be decided by the Japanese to open the forests for unrestricted cutting.

In this connection, during the British regime, the burning for charcoal of any timber which was valuable for structural purposes was forbidden (ends and branches could, of course, be used). If unrestricted charcoal cutting is allowed, the timber supplies to Malaya in future years will be greatly imperilled.

(Marshall).

September 13th, 1943.

Dear Monson,

With reference to my letter of August 30th on the subject of the replacement by the Japanese of the Sultan of Kelantan by his brother the Rajah Kelantan, I attach copy of a reply that I have just received from the Far Eastern Bureau of this Ministry at New Delhi from which it emerges that there is no real evidence to confirm the rumour. I do not think, therefore, that we are justified in giving any publicity to it.

Yours sincerely,

H.R. Bird

W.B. Monson Esq., Colonial Office, Downing Street, S.W.1.

CYPHER TELEGRAM

From: Government of India, Dept. of Information & Broadcasting

To: Secretary of State for India

Dated: New Delhi, 04.45 hours, 7th Sept. 1943.

7512. 702 Empax.

Following for Miniform London from Far Eastern Bureau. Your telegram No. 566.

Rumour arose among Malays engaged in monitoring at Delhi owing to passage in Domei in English July 9th regarding Tojo's visit to Singapore. After referring to Premier's reception of "Malai Sultans from nine different States" including Kelantan and statement by one of their number as spokesman, it continues "The Rajah of Kelantan, Tongku Ibrahim, declared that the State continued to flourish during the one and a half years under Japanese administration."

===== Page 94 =====
2. Assumption from this that the Raja has replaced Sultan Ismail seems unjustified. No other reference to the Raja has been noted and so far as we are aware there is no other foundation for the rumour.

===== Page 95 =====
Dear Monson,

Your letter on the subject of the replacement by the Japanese of the Sultan of Kelantan by his brother the Raja Kelantan. It is odd that we have not heard of this from New Delhi.

We have, however, been through all monitoring and intelligence reports received from the Far Eastern Bureau since last April and can find no mention of it. Had there been mention of it in the monitoring reports, it would in any case have been flashed to you by the B.B.C.; we will in future send you a copy of the Fortnightly Intelligence Report produced by the Far Eastern Bureau. These arrangements, I think, meet the point raised in the second paragraph of your letter under reference.

I agree that if the Kelantan incident is correct, it would be worth giving publicity to it. I am accordingly sending a telegram to Far Eastern Bureau enquiring into its veracity.

Yours sincerely,

W. B. Monson, Esq.,
Colonial Office,
S.W.1

DRAFT AND RECORD COPY

REGISTERED No.

LETTER SENT

Colonial Office,
Downing Street,
S. W. 1.

21 August 1943.

DRAFT

Monson.21/
Dear Redman,

MR.

I was shown yesterday a private letter from our old acquaintance, Tengku Mahmoud Mahyiddeen, in which he mentioned that the Japanese had replaced the Sultan of Kelantan by his brother, the Raja of Kelantan. This, if true, is a point of considerable local political significance. Could we ask you to help us by ascertaining from Butler whether he can confirm this report and state the sources on which it is based?

ENCLOSURES

Incidentally, it is somewhat unsatisfactory to us that we should first hear of matters of political importance like this, which have real significance for our post-war planning, in a haphazard way from private sources. I have not seen any reference to this deposition in the digests of monitory reports. We wonder, therefore, would it be possible for the Far Eastern Bureau, who seem to pick up a good deal of news of this kind, to make a special report to your Ministry on any important political developments of this kind which may come to their notice.

FURTHER ACTION

To return to the Kelantan incident, if it is confirmed there seems to us to be a good deal to be said in favour of giving some publicity to it in this country, since it throws light on what is happening in Malaya under Japanese rule, and may help to correct some hastily conceived ideas about the Malayan rulers.

Yours sincerely,

H. V. REDMAN, ESQ.

===== Page 97 =====
22.57 (D.B.S.T.) MALAY'S BANKING PROGRESS
Shonan: Malay's monetary system has made phenomenal progress since the establishment of the Japanese military administration a little over a year and a half ago, and is now undergoing a change from mere commercial finance to planned finance. Malayan financial institutions have considerably helped Japanese forces to attain self-sufficiency in war needs, to develop resources and vital strategic materials, and to maintain peace and order.

There have been three stages of development: the first period, from the Japanese occupation of Malaya to July last year, when Japanese banks, including the Yokohama Specie Bank, devoted their efforts towards restoring pre-war activities; the second period, from July last year to March this year, when the Southern Regions Development Bank opened branches, and the Peoples' Bank was established; and the third period, from April to the present time, when the Malayan branch of the Southern Regions Development Bank, as the Central Bank in Malaya, was authorised to issue notes.

During the first period, the Yokohama Specie Bank and the Bank of Taiwan opened branches in March and the Kanan Bank in June. Six Chinese Banks also opened with a guarantee by Japanese banks to accommodate loans to a limit of 10,000,000 dollars. Japanese and Chinese Banks devoted their energies chiefly to restoring their business to pre-war status and accommodating funds needed for reconstruction in Malaya.

During the second period, the Malayan office of the Southern Regions Development Bank was established in July to exercise control over other banks. The People's Bank opened in Singapore on 1st September with branches in each State in Malaya. Thus, the Malayan financial machinery was virtually completed.

The third stage began with the establishment of the Malayan Bankers Council and the authorisation of the Southern Regions Development Bank on 1st April to issue notes to replace the military script. This Bank thus became the sole central bank. Meanwhile, steps had been taken to check the rising tendency of commodity prices, and the self-sufficiency structure of Malaya was steadily strengthened by increased funds and the smooth interchange of goods.

In the past, Malay's economic activity has been one-sided, as the country exported raw materials and relied on imports for the supply of daily necessities. With the successful termination of reconstruction work and the start of industrial construction, the task of financing Malayan organisations has assumed increasing importance.

The necessary expansion of the financial machinery is expected to take the following forms: firstly, the proper geographical distribution and expansion of existing facilities between head offices and branches of the Japanese, Chinese and Indian Banks; secondly, the strengthening of the organisation and increasing activities of the Malayan Bankers' Council as the pivot; thirdly, the speedy expansion of the Peoples' Bank and the establishment of Cooperative Credit Societies to minimise the dependence of natives on usurers and pawnbrokers for financial accommodation.

16.25 BOSE'S SINGAPORE SPEECH
Shonan: Bose, addressing a mammoth rally of the local Indian Independence League held here in Farrer Park this evening, declared that Burma had won her independence by blood, and Indian freedom must be won in the same manner.

The gathering, which was originally scheduled for yesterday week, was sponsored to commemorate the first anniversary of the Indian National Congress "Quit India" resolution, as well as the imprisonment of Gandhi and other Indian leaders.

Prior to the meeting, crank troops of the newly-formed Indian Independence Army marched into Farrer Park, amidst the cheers of those present. At 19.15, the rally opened with a huge audience joining in the Indian anthem.

As Bose and other leading members of the Indian Independence League appeared on the platform, the massed assembly shouted in unison: "On to Delhi!"

Following a greeting by the Chairman of the Malay branch of the Independence League, Bose was welcomed to the speaker's rostrum by a roaring wave of applause.

Referring to the National Congress Party's "Quit India" resolution directed at the British, Bose asserted that 8th August is one of the most significant days in India's three centuries of anti-British struggle, and will live in the minds of all Indians.
