CONFIDENTIAL

FORTNIGHTLY INTELLIGENCE REPORT NO. 9

Far Eastern Bureau, British Ministry of Information, New Delhi.

Period ended July 17th, 1943.

MALAYA AND SUMATRA

POLITICAL

1. Transfer of 4 Malay States to Siam: A joint communique from Japan and Siam on July 5th stated that the four (recently British Protected) Malay States of Perlis, Kedah, Terengganu, and Kelantan would be ceded to Siam. These states will be administered by the Japanese until the Siamese Government is ready to take control. The inhabitants were urged to cooperate in this scheme for strengthening the Great East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere. (Domei in English, July 7th).

Comment: The historical background of these four British Protected Unfederated Malay States is given in Appendix I, and relevant statistical information on them in Appendix II. Appendix III is an extract from the Encyclopaedia Britannica and Appendix IV concerns British and Siamese influence on the Peninsular Malays.

2. Tojo's Inspection Tour: General Hideki Tojo, Premier and War Minister of Japan, arrived in Singapore on the afternoon of July 5th, after his visit to Saigon and Bangkok. An official army announcement gives the following reasons for his tour: (a) to confer with Field-Marshal Count Hisaichi Terauchi; (b) to inspect conditions in the South; (c) to meet officers and men of the Japanese fighting forces; (d) to prepare the new policy for the Southern Regions. Tojo met Field-Marshal Count Hisaichi Terauchi, Supreme Commander of the Japanese forces in the Southern Regions, and other notables, and then proceeded to the Padang (sports ground) opposite the Singapore Municipal Building for his official reception. All the Malay Sultans were present. Tojo declared his determination to bring about the unity of various nations and peoples of East Asia. The same evening the Malayan Sultans and Sumatran princes paid courtesy calls on the Premier. The Premier visited the War Memorial at Bukit Timah, and the Ford Motor Plant where General Tomoyuki Yamashita dictated the terms of surrender to Lt. General Arthur Percival. In the afternoon the Japanese Premier had a meeting with Malay Sultans and was welcomed by the Sultan of Johore on behalf of the Sultans. (Domei in English, July 5th and 6th).

3. Field-Marshal Hisaichi Terauchi was given the Field-Marshal's sabre on July 5th by Tojo at Singapore. (Tokyo in Japanese, July 6th, 1000 GMT, 25.37 m.)

4. On July 6th the Sultans of Perlis, Perak, Selangor, Kedah, Negri Sembilan, Pahang, Kelantan and Terengganu, called to congratulate him on his promotion. At tea the Sultans met the departmental chiefs of the Administration. The same evening the Sultans attended a dinner given in their honour at the Konan (Shonan?) Club. (Domei in English, July 6th).

5. Field-Marshal Hisaichi Terauchi arrived by air at Padang, Sumatra, on June 25th, on an inspection tour. He conferred with Kenzo Yano, the local Chief Administrator, and then travelled north to Medan and Kotaradya, thence returning to his headquarters in Singapore. (Domei in English, July 5th).

===== Page 99 =====
6. General Iwane Matsui's Tour of the Southern Regions:
General Matsui last visited Singapore on his way to the Disarmament Conference in Geneva in 1919 and is now on an inspection tour of the Southern Regions. (Tokyo in Japanese, July 11th, 0300 GMT, 25.37 m.). In a press interview Matsui emphasized the need to strengthen the unity of East Asia. (Domei in English, July 12th).

Comment: Born in 1879; a graduate of the Military Academy; Military Attache in France; service in Mongolia, China and Taiwan; Commander-in-Chief of the Japanese forces in Shanghai, 1937-1938; appointed Cabinet Military Adviser in 1938. Matsui is one of the leading chauvinists in the Japanese Army. As Commander-in-Chief in Shanghai he was a threat to foreign interests and was recalled after the Panay and Nanking affairs. He founded the Great Asia Society for the subjugation of China in 1933.

7. Malay Sultans:
Before returning to their respective States, the Sultans expressed their satisfaction at their meeting with Tojo. The "Raja of Kelantan," Tengku Ibrahim, and another member of the Sultans' entourage also paid tribute to the Japanese administration. (Domei in English, July 9th).

Comment: A member of the ruling house, Tengku Ibrahim, Raja Kelantan, is next in succession to the throne of Kelantan. He has never been on good terms with his brother, the Sultan, Ismail. An unscrupulous man and a bad Muslim, the Raja Kelantan might be a ready Japanese tool. See also F.I.R. No.7, Malaya and Sumatra, para 8.

8. Sultans' Donations:
Japanese Army and Navy hospitals in Singapore have been given donations by H.H. Sultan Ibrahim of Johore and nine other Malay Sultans. The decision to make the gift was taken during Tojo's visit to Singapore. (Domei in English, July 15th).

Comment: Most Malayan Sultans derive the bulk of their income as a monthly allowance from the State and, like lesser Malay folk, most of them are improvident. In most of the States the Government is called upon to assist by direct or indirect means in keeping members of the Royal House or influential Malays on their financial feet. If on this occasion their Highnesses' contribution was material it may be expected that they will have to be reimbursed.

9. Dr. Ba Maw Visits Singapore:
Dr. Ba Maw, head of the Burmese Government, met Tojo in Singapore on July 5th and spent half an hour with him discussing Japanese-Burmese co-operation. (Domei in English, July 12th).

10. Indian Independence League, and Subhas Chandra Bose:
Indian representatives from Manchuria, Hongkong, Indochina, Borneo, Burma, Malaya and other parts of East Asia at a meeting in the Dai Toa Hall, Singapore, elected Subhas Chandra Bose President of the Indian Independence League after his nomination by Ras Behari Bose, former President of the League. The official Japanese blessing came in congratulatory messages from Tojo and Shigenitsu, the Japanese Foreign Minister. (Domei in English, July 4th, and Tokyo in English, July 5th, 0905 GMT, 19.86 m.). The following day Subhas Chandra Bose called on the Japanese Supreme Commander, addressed the delegates from the Indian Independence League branches in the Dai Toa Gekijo (Cathay Theatre), and in the evening broadcast a special message in Hindustani to India (Domei in English, July 5th). Subhas Chandra Bose worshipped at the Soldiers' Monument on the Bukit Timah Heights and also at the Shonan Shrine, and in the evening attended the mass reception.

===== Page 100 =====
Held in his honour. (Domei in English, July 9th). 100,000 members of the Indian Community rallied on July 9th in the Padang before the Municipal Office to welcome Subhas Chandra Bose. In the course of his speech Bose expressed confidence that in the coming fight his countrymen in Malaya would play a leading part. "What once was the bulwark of British Imperialism has now become the bulwark of Indian nationalism." (Domei in English, July 10th). Bose stressed the need for "the establishment of a Provisional Government of Free India to bring about the total mobilisation of all resources of Indian residents in East Asia by thorough organisation." This Provisional Government will make way for a permanent government of Free India. Bose stated that Mahatma Gandhi's civil disobedience campaign was inadequate for winning India's freedom. An army of 300,000 would have to be raised in East Asia for India's last battle for freedom. (Domei in English, July 10th). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 11.

Comment: Tokyo in Japanese, 0730 GMT, June 19th, 1942: "The local Indians are anxious to support the Independence Movement openly. So, unless it interferes with our plans ... the administration will encourage them to take part in the building up of Greater East Asia."

11. Indian Women welcome Subhas Chandra Bose: Over a thousand Indian women members of the Indian Independence League gave a reception to Bose on July 12th. The military aspect of the League was emphasized by a guard-of-honour of armed women. Mrs. M.K. Chidambaram, the Chairman of the Women's Section of the Indian Independence League, welcomed Bose and presented him with a purse. Bose announced that the first Indian Women's Regiment would be called the "Rani of Jhansi" Regiment. (Domei in English, July 13th). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 15.

Comment: Mrs. Chidambaram is much younger than her husband (see F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 11) and is a well educated woman from Kuala Lumpur. She used to appear in Indian musical entertainments at the Victoria Memorial Theatre, Singapore. The Rani of Jhansi died in battle, leading her own troops during the Mutiny. On the death of the Raja, she had wished to adopt an heir but the Government had refused to permit it. She had also objected when cattle were slaughtered in Jhansi territory.

12. Indian National Army: A communique from the Indian Independence League Headquarters on July 8th announced the formation of the Indian National Army: "The Indian Independence League in East Asia has organised an Army. This Army will be known as the Indian National Army. The Indian National Army has sworn allegiance to the cause of India's complete independence." This army of volunteers was created in May 1942. On the morning of July 6th Premier Hideki Tojo reviewed the newly organised Indian National Army, under the leadership of Subhas Chandra Bose, who welcomed him. After the march past, the Premier spent some time with Bose. (Domei in English, July 8th). Since the review of the army on July 5th by Subhas Chandra Bose, "young Indian volunteers from all over Malaya have applied to join". There have also been gifts of money for the organisation. (Domei in English, July 10th). The training camp of the Indian National Army is situated in Central Malaya and the majority of the recruits are still in their twenties. (Tokyo in English, July 16th, 0905 GMT, 19.86 m.)

Comment: The Indian population of Malaya, which in 1941 was three-quarters of a million, is predominantly Tamil or South Indian, with some Sikhs, Bengalis and Punjabis. The Tamils are mostly estate labourers, traders, clerks and junior Government employees.

===== Page 101 =====
Officials, and are not of the stuff of which fighting men are usually made. Probably unemployment and not patriotism has gathered them to Bose's colours.

18. Indian Leaders: Indian leaders who have expressed satisfaction at Subhas Chandra Bose's arrival in Malaya are: K.S. Anwari, "Leader of the Singapore Muslim Community"; A. Yellapa, Chairman of the Singapore Branch of the I.I.L.; J.A. Thivy, Chairman of the I.I.L., Malaya Branch; S.C. Goho, Former Chairman of the Singapore Branch of the I.I.L.; S. Rajaram, Leader of the Indian Youth Movement. (Domei in English, July 5th, 0800 GMT). Comment: (a) S.C. Goho is a brilliant and politically-minded Indian barrister and was one of the Indians nominated by the Governor to arbitrate with the leaders of Indian labourers during the riots of early summer 1941. He was acknowledged by the Government as head of the Malayan Indian community from about the beginning of 1941. He courageously allowed his photograph to be used on anti-Japanese posters for rallying the Indians against the Japanese. He was President of the Indian Youth League and Indian Association. Before the fall of Singapore he was appointed Agent of the Government of India in Malaya. His two wives are now in Calcutta and have received financial assistance from the Government of India. There have been reports of his "co-operation" with the Japanese but it is very doubtful whether he could be of much use to them as an amenable Quisling. He was much too independent an individualist with respect for the fundamental merits of British rule. He is probably one of the few with an anti-Japanese record who will be clever enough to survive. Any judgment against Goho should be postponed. (b) A. Yellapa is a barrister aged about 30, formerly of Ipoh where he was clerk to S.K. Das, a solicitor. Thence he went to England to study law and came to Singapore about 1937 to join Braddell Bros. He was a friend of Goho and Roland Braddell who is now in India. Yellapa used to be pro-British and regarded as trustworthy. (c) J.A. Thivy is an advocate and solicitor of Ipoh. (d) S. Rajaram was Secretary of the Indian Youth League before the fall of Singapore. (e) K.S. Anwari: Publisher and printer and a man of no principles. He was prosecuted in 1938 for publishing a seditious book for a Singapore Japanese, Koguchi, and criminal proceedings against him for cheating his partner had not been completed at the fall of Singapore.

14. Administrative Conference in Singapore: Future administrative policy was discussed at the headquarters of the Military Administration on July 11th, and the governors of all the Malay States reported on conditions in their respective territories. (Domei in English, July 12th).

15. Peace Preservation Corps in Perak: This organisation has started to compile family registers of all communities, prior to instituting the "Neighbourhood Association" system. (Domei in English, July 16th). Comment: Penang in English, October 4th, 1942, 1200 GMT, reported that the Chief Police Officer had called for co-operation from the people of Ipoh for the formation of this corps. The "Tonari-gumi" system of dragooning groups of households in Japan and the "Pao Chia Chi Tu" (ward protection system) and other similar systems of family registration, particularly of Chinese in Malaya, are typical Japanese methods of controlling elements hostile to Japanese administration. (See also F.I.R. No.7, Malaya and Sumatra, para 2). The Heiho Auxiliary service scheme (see F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 6) is probably another sign of the same general Japanese security policy.

===== Page 102 =====
Small groups of people, all known to each other, are made collectively responsible for occurrences in their area and pay dearly in life and wealth if they fail to report suspicious individuals, movements, or happenings. We have not yet heard of "Railway-loving villages" in Malaya, a Japanese device in Manchuria for making railside villages responsible with their lives for the safety of sections of the track. But if guerrilla activities develop in Malaya, the Heiho and others will doubtless be coerced in like fashion to work for Japanese ends. Meanwhile, the Peace Preservation Corps' general function is probably the discouragement of gang robbery (see para 43), kidnapping, and extortion, crimes which increase rapidly amongst the Chinese of Malaya in times of depression and which must be a major administrative problem at the moment, especially in the tin mining State of Perak.

16. Political Propaganda: A film, "Harimau Melayu" ("Malay Tiger") depicting the exploits of the Japanese leader of an anti-British Secret Society will be sent to the Southern Regions by the Board of Information. (Domei in English, July 15th). Comment: The tiger, the beast himself and the human were-tiger, have an established place in Malay fables and the Malay imagination. Under British influence the tiger's status has perhaps been slightly raised. Be that as it may, the tiger and the keris have acquired heraldic significance and even in our Delhi broadcasts we habitually open with the words "Ini-lah harimau melayu yang mengaung dari-pada All India Radio, Delhi." ("This is the Malayan tiger roaring from A.I.R.") Malays will be unimpressed but greatly amused by a Japanese hero assuming the title Malay tiger.

17. Administrative Conference in Sumatra: On July 12th and 14th Provincial Governors reported on local conditions to the Military Administration. The Chief of the General Affairs Department, in a press interview, stressed (1) strengthening of defenses; (2) economic self-sufficiency; (3) securing resources of defense material; (4) maintenance of peace and order; (5) the need for the natives to learn Japanese, and employees of the Military Administration to study the local language. Departmental chiefs were present at the Conference. (Domei in English, July 15th, and Tokyo in Japanese, 0600 GMT, 25.37 m.)

18. Sumatra Leaders to Visit Japan: Thirteen men representing provinces in Sumatra have arrived in Singapore on their way to study the Japanese administrative system in Japan. (Domei in English, July 13th)

19. A Menangkabau chief, Sjafei, in this party, disclosed that leading officials in the Menangkabau district have decided to form a "Poetera" association in Sumatra on similar lines to the one already formed in Java. (Domei in English, July 16th). Comment: Poetera is a composite word derived from the initial syllables of Poesat = Navel or center, Tenaga = Strength, Rayat = People. Doubtless more will be heard of this association. See F.I.R: No.8, Netherlands Indies, para 1 and No.9, Netherlands Indies, para 12.

ECONOMIC

20. Five-Year Industrial Plan: The Japanese Administration has a draft five-year plan to encourage iron and aluminium industries in Malaya, by the utilization of local deposits of iron ore and bauxite. The project covers also fibrous industries. (Domei in English, July 14th). Discussing the plan, Domei in Japanese, June 19th, spoke of the inevitability of a long war, and the need for economy in shipping space. Plans and location of the factories are under consideration. Material from Japan will be necessary to start the plants. The intention is that once established the scheme will reduce

===== Page 103 =====
Japan's responsibility for production.

Comment: Singapore, with its natural advantages of sea transport, cheap and abundant fuel (electricity was less than 1d a unit), water and labour, had become industry-minded long before 1935. Steel furniture, office equipment, steel trunks, were amongst some of the articles manufactured. A Swiss firm was already manufacturing aluminium articles for the planting industry and domestic use in 1935.

21. Automobile Manufacture: The Kokusan Automobile Co., opened in the former plant of a certain Singapore factory, has turned out its first car. (Donei in English, July 5th).

Comment: Ford Motors' factory in Bukit Timah used to assemble cars from imported parts.

22. Rubber Road Paving: The East Coast Agricultural Experimental Station of Sumatra claims the discovery of a road paving material "equal, if not superior to asphalt" composed of palm oil, lime and chemically treated scrap-rubber. The possibility is seen of all roads in the Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere being paved with this material. (Donei in English, July 11th).

Comment: Research on rubber roadways has claimed the attention of government and commercial workers over many years in England and Malaya. As a result of pressure by the Malayan Rubber Growers' Association experimental strips were laid in London, Singapore and Kuala Lumpur, but they did not stand the test of heavy traffic over a long period. This Japanese process has obviously not yet been put to the same rigid test.

23. Food Control: Singapore city administration has established a food control office, and the grains, sugar and salt distribution associations have been abolished. (Donei in Japanese, July 5th). These distribution guilds were formed at the request of the Municipality which was unable to cope with the situation for lack of manpower. Furuyama is to be controller, with Kitagawa as his deputy. (Donei in Japanese, July 5th). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 13.

Comment: The first attempt at Food Control in Malaya was the institution of a Food Control Committee in May 1917, and there was rice control during the general rice shortage of 1919 to 1921. In changed circumstances and under the threat of war a Food Controller was appointed in the early summer of 1939 and by August rice dealers in Singapore had been licensed and obliged to submit monthly statements of their stocks and sales. This and other control measures were extended to the Peninsula and by August 1940 a Malayan "rice pool" had been formed, an arrangement by which all rice imported was held and issued under the Government's control and all retailers had to carry a month's supply, replenishable only against a permit issued by the Food Controller. In January 1941, the Government secured control over all rice imports and contracted with Siam, Burma and the Netherlands Indies to meet Malaya's needs of over 50,000 tons of polished rice monthly. Large quantities of padi (unpolished rice) were also bought from 1939 on and stored with the intention of creating a two years' emergency reserve to enable Malaya to withstand blockade, and milling facilities were extended accordingly. In March 1941, control was extended to other foods such as tinned and powdered milk, meat, flour, potatoes, tea etc. The principle throughout was the effective control of wholesalers and retailers and the enforced maintenance by them and the government of reserve stocks. Despite the fact that Malaya's population was only five and a half million, and that the Government was reputed fearless in enforcing the law, the Food Controller's task was a hard one, and it was generally appreciated that his was one of the more efficient and successful of the many wartime controls in

===== Page 104 =====
Malaya. With admirable intentions and energy from 1939, Malaya accumulated food and rushed construction of aerodromes throughout the country on a plan of peripheral defence. As it happened, lack of aircraft and the inability to stop the Japanese quickly rendered both these defence measures of great assistance to the enemy. As the British retired, the rice, canned milk, etc., from the Government warehouses throughout the country was freely given away, and the Japanese took over a country that could for a long time subsist without attention though normally it would depend upon imports for 60% of its rice. Japan's task was in fact lightened and she was saved the expenditure of much shipping.

24. Production Measures: The Military Administration at a conference in Singapore on July 11th urged the Governors of the various States to grow more food, and they were also given instructions regarding the rubber and copra industries. (Donei in English, July 12th).

25. Japanese Soldier Farmers: The Military Administration of Malaya has appointed soldier farmers, with a knowledge of the Malay language, to the various States to instruct the people in Japanese agricultural methods. These soldier farmers will commence their instruction in August when padi planting starts for a three months period. More soldiers will be mobilised for this purpose "if the plan is successful." (Donei in English, July 13th). Comment: This rather suggests that the Malay agriculturist has ignored Japanese wishes and advice and that he is now to receive military supervision. "If the plan is successful" may presumably be read as "if more pressure is needed."

26. Penang Botanical Gardens: The area of the Penang Botanical Gardens has been extended by 20 acres. K. Watanabe, Director of the Gardens, reports experiments with medicinal herbs, fibrous plants, fruits, and vegetables. (Donei in English, July 16th). See also F.I.R. No.7, Malaya and Sumatra, para 17.

27. Japanese Goods in Malaya: On July 15th, goods, mostly household commodities, were on sale to the public in Singapore at controlled prices. A second consignment has arrived for distribution, under similar conditions, in the Malay States. (Donei in English, July 15th). Consignments have arrived in Kuala Lumpur and Penang. (Donei in English; July 16th). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 35. Comment: The fact that it is considered worthwhile to record the arrival of Japanese goods proves that it is a most unusual phenomenon.

28. Sumatra Communications: The Palembang Railway Office, for some time past, has been running a bus service from Loebocklinggaw to Moearatebo (400 kms.), and the Northern Railway Office, on June 15th, began a bus service between Fort de Kock and Padang. (Donei in Japanese, June 26th). Comment: Under the Dutch, Sumatra had excellent bus services and many were run in conjunction with the railways.

29. Radio-Telegraphic Service: Singapore: "Donei News Agency Southern Regions General" started a service in English and Japanese on June 22nd.

===== Page 105 =====
1943, for transmitting early news of the Southern Regions to the East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere. The call sign being YZXL; kcs. 11,115, at intervals from 01.00 to 10.30 GMT. (Donei in Japanese, June 22nd).

30. Singapore Lottery: The second-term Government Lottery has been drawn with first, second, and third prizes drawing 50,000, 30,000, and 10,000 respectively. (Donei in English, July 10th). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, paras 37 and 38.

SOCIAL AND CULTURAL

31. Medical and Economic Research for Southern Regions: The Education Ministry has allocated 450,000 yen in 1943 to research groups of Tokyo Imperial University for work on malaria and yellow fever. In 1942 the Ministry appropriated 200,000 yen for research groups "for medical examinations and study of living conditions for Japanese in the Southern Regions." (Tokyo in German, June 13th, 0130 GMT)

32. Ethnological Research: The Military Administration has appointed a body of experts, assisted by native interpreters, to study the private and communal aspects of life of the various races in Sumatra. The workers, starting on the Menangkabau tribe, hope to complete their findings within 6 months. (Donei in English, July 14th and 15th). See also F.I.R. No.2, Malaya and Sumatra, para 2; No.3, para 3.

33. East Asia Development Training School: 160 students graduated at the 3rd graduation ceremony of the East Asia Development Training School in Singapore on July 8th, after which this school has closed temporarily. A school on similar lines is to be opened in Sumatra. (Donei in English, July 9th). Donei in Japanese, July 9th, says the school has closed and is to be transferred to Sumatra. Comment: This may be the "Axis Promotion Training Camp" for officials. See F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 48.

34. Education in Singapore: 21,000 pupils are now enrolled in Singapore, an increase of 60% over last year's figures. (Batavia in English, 1030 GMT, 16.6 n.) Comment: The average enrolment in English and Vernacular Schools in the Straits Settlements in 1937 was 93,145 and the 1941 figure would have been more. The figure for the Settlement of Singapore by itself is not known but it must have exceeded the total of the other Settlements viz. Penang and Province Wellesley, Malacca, Labuan, Christmas and Cocos Islands. The implication is that for practical purposes education ceased during 1942.

35. Education: A Japanese reader, the first of three volumes, has been published by the Society of International Cultural Relations for use by colleges and middle grade schools in the Southern Regions. (Donei in English, July 8th).

36. Teachers' Training Institute: Ipoh reports that Malay girls for the first time have passed the entrance examination for training as teachers of the Japanese language. (Donei in English, July 16th).

===== Page 106 =====
37. Training of local Military Interpreters: Donei in English, July 12th, mentioned the selection of 50 Singapore youths between the ages of 16 and 25 for training as Military Interpreters in the Koakun Renjo (Officers' Training Institute).

38. Seamen in Malaya: The Military Administration has announced that all seamen's ranks will in future be given Japanese designations. (Donei in English, July 12th).

39. Training Native Divers in Singapore: Medically fit native (Malay?) youths between 15 and 21 years of age can now train as divers in the Diving Operators' Institute opened last week. A four months' training will qualify them for salvage work. (Donei in English, July 5th).

40. Training of Sumatran Teachers of Japanese language: A school will be opened at Bukit Tinggi (Fort de Kock) by the middle of July, by the Japanese Military authorities for teaching native youths Japanese, so that they may teach it in their provinces when they have qualified. About 100 selected youths will be enrolled each term. (Donei in English, July 3rd). See also F.I.R. No.8, Malaya and Sumatra, para 53.

41. Sumatra Shrines: Work will commence in August on the Medan Jinja (Medan Shinto Shrine). It is to be built in the grounds of the former tobacco experimental station on Mangalan Road. (Donei in English, July 10th).

42. At Bukit Tinggi (Fort de Kock), a shrine was dedicated in the presence of local leaders. (Donei in Japanese, June 25th). See also F.I.R. No.6, Malaya and Sumatra, para 3. Comment: The Singapore Shinto Shrine was completed on February 15th, 1943, and was "expected to be the leading Shinto Shrine outside Japan Proper." Another shrine was under construction on the Peninsula. (Tokyo in English, February 6th, 1130 GMT, Tokyo in Japanese, February 15th, 300 GMT and February 23rd, 300 GMT).

43. Gang Robbery in Singapore: The ringleader, Lin Sey, of a gang which robbed a Chinese Merchant of 10,000 yen has been sentenced to death whilst one member has been given 12 years and four 10 years rigorous imprisonment. (Donei in English, July 14th). Comment: Crime in general and gang robbery in particular always increases in Malaya in times of slump. The position in the Chinese areas up-country e.g. Ipoh and Taiping is probably worse in this respect now than at any time since 1900.

44. Swimming Contests: A competition beginning July 19th, open to men and women, with gold and silver medals for the winners, is being sponsored by Donei News Agency's Medan branch. (Donei in English, July 14th).

===== Page 107 =====
Appendix I

Historical background of the British Protected Unfederated Malay States of Kedah, Perlis, Kelantan, and Trengganu ceded by Japan to Siam.

The position of these States before 1826 is not clear. Kedah (including Perlis) has sometimes been independent and sometimes forced to become a tributary to Burma or Siam. In 1771, Francis Light treated the Raja of Kedah as an independent ruler, and a treaty was signed between Great Britain and Kedah in 1791, ceding Penang to Great Britain. The Siamese were, however, allowed to overrun Kedah in 1821. Kedah and the other States had sent "Bunga Mas" (gold flowers) to Siam, but it was a matter of dispute whether this should be regarded as a token of respect or vassalage. In the treaty of 1826 between Great Britain and Siam, the supremacy of Siam over Kedah was acknowledged though she took no part in the government of the country. Perlis was then separated from Kedah by the Siamese and placed under a Raja. As regards Kelantan and Trengganu, the overlordship of Siam was always in dispute, and these two States were governed by their respective Rajas. Though they stood in an informally inferior position to Siam, they were entirely free from any effective control from Bangkok over either their internal or external affairs.

Owing to difficulties between the Raja of Kelantan and the British Duff Development Co., Mr. Graham was sent by Siam to act as Adviser in 1903, and in 1905 the Raja of Kedah accepted a small loan from Siam and agreed to receive an Adviser approved by the Siamese Government. Trengganu did not accept an Adviser.

Negotiations for a treaty with Great Britain were opened by Siam in 1907 as she ardently desired to see the last of British extraterritoriality. On the 10th March, 1909, a treaty was signed transferring the suzerainty of the four States to Great Britain, who treated them as Unfederated Malay States. By the same treaty, British Consular jurisdiction was practically abolished, and Siam obtained a loan to build the Southern Railway.

The population of the four States on December 31st, 1937 was officially estimated to be 1,133,644, of which 80% was Malay, 12% Chinese, 6% Indian, and others 2%.

Before 1909 the Siamese had no control over these States and derived no benefits from them. There were neither railways nor roads in the States, which could only be reached with difficulty from Bangkok. The population was predominantly Malay, and the Siamese had no regrets when handing over the States. Later, however, when the railway from Bangkok to Penang was constructed through Kedah and good roads made, the Siamese saw for themselves the country being opened up, land being cleared for rubber plantations and paddy fields, and general evidence of increasing wealth. Many then regretted that Siam had ceased to have any control over the States.

APPENDIX III.

Extract from Encyclopaedia Britannica, Eleventh Edition (1911).

MALAY STATES (Siamese) - The authority of Siam, which at one time covered the whole of the Malay Peninsula, now extends southward to an irregular line drawn across the peninsula at about 6° 30' N. Between that line and the Isthmus of Kra, usually accepted as the northernmost point of the Malay Peninsula, there lie some 20,000 square miles of territory inhabited by a mixed population of Siamese and Malays with here and there a few remnants of the aboriginal inhabitants clinging to the wilder districts, and with a few Chinese settlers engaged in commerce. Formerly this tract was divided into a number of states, each of which was ruled by a chief (Siamese, Chao Muang; Malay, raja), who held his title from the king of Siam, but, subject to a few restrictions, conducted the affairs of his state in accordance with his own desires; the office of chief, moreover, was hereditary, subject always to the approval of the suzerain. The states formed two groups: a northern, including Langsuan, Chaya, Nakhon Sri Tammarat, Songkla, Renawng, Takoapa, Pang Nga, Tongka and Trang, in which the Siamese element predominated and of which the chiefs were usually Siamese or Chinese; and a southern, including Palean, Satun (Setul), Patani, Raman, Jering, Sai (Teloban), Re Nge (Legeh), Yala (Jalor) and Nong Chik, in which the population was principally Malay and the ruler also Malay. Four other states of the southern group, Kelantan, Trengganu, Kedah and Perlis, of which the population is entirely Malay, passed from Siamese to British protection in 1909.

With the gradual consolidation of the Siamese kingdom all the states of the northern group have been incorporated as ordinary provinces of Siam (q.v.), the hereditary Chao Muang having died or been pensioned and replaced by officials of the Siamese Civil Service, while the states themselves now constitute provinces of the administrative divisions of Chumpon, Nakhon Sri Tammarat and Puket. The states of the southern group, however, retain their hereditary rulers, each of whom presides over a council and governs with the aid of a Siamese assistant commissioner and with a staff of Siamese district officials, subject to the general control of high commissioners under whom the states are grouped. This southern group, with a total area of about 7,000 square miles and a population of 375,000, constitutes the Siamese Malay States. A British consul with headquarters at Puket, and a vice-consul who resides at Songkla, watch over the interests of British subjects in the states of the west and east sides of the peninsula respectively. Other foreign powers are unrepresented.

(By W. A. Graham, Adviser to His Siamese Majesty's Minister for Lands and Agriculture).

===== Page 110 =====
APPENDIX IV.

British and Siamese influence on Peninsular Malays.

The Isthmus of Kra is usually regarded as the northern limit of the Malay Peninsula. The Siamese have long occupied the area north of the Isthmus. This is Buddhist Siam proper and does not concern us here. South of Kra to Singapore, there have been a host of states, most of them predominantly Malay in population and Mohamedan in religion. These states have been variously influenced by the Chinese, British and the Siamese, during the past two centuries, and particularly during the last 70 years.

Chinese influence has been commercial rather than directly political. The Chinese have emigrated to the Peninsula in large numbers but they have been largely a transient element in the population, and the Malays have hitherto been regarded as the political heirs to the Peninsula.

Nearly all the predominantly Malay territories of the Peninsula had come under British control or protection by 1910 and remained so in 1941. These territories were the Crown Colony of the Straits Settlements (Singapore Island, the Settlements of Malacca, Penang Island and Province Wellesley), the Federated Malay States (Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan and Pahang) and the Unfederated States (Johore, Kedah, Perlis, Kelantan and Trengganu), which, together with certain extra-Peninsular territories, viz., the settlement of Labuan, the Unfederated State of Brunei in Borneo, and Christmas and Cocos Keeling Islands, constituted what was collectively known as British Malaya.

In the area between the British Malaya border and the Kra Isthmus, Malay, Siamese and Chinese elements are mixed in varying proportions. The population of some districts in the south is almost purely Malay; further north the Siamese and Chinese elements are stronger. The entire area had been recognised as Siamese territory before 1910, and was shown on official maps as an integral part of Siam. But in parts of the area Siamese control remained very loose until comparatively recent years. Siamese interest in establishing a direct and effective administration in the areas of Malay or mixed population north of the Malay border grew only as communications and economic resources were developed.

Siamese interest in the states south of the British Malaya border rested on no firmer basis than the traditional payment of tribute by these states to the Siamese Crown, a practice which was discontinued finally under the 1909 Treaty with Britain. Even this interest was abandoned under the Treaty, and it has only been revived by an extreme nationalist minority in Siam since the 1933 Revolution. It is partly to coincide with this group that Japan has now transferred to Siam the four Unfederated Malay States of Perlis, Kedah, Kelantan and Trengganu.

The community of feeling amongst the Mohamedan Malays from the Kra Isthmus to Singapore must be borne in mind in assessing the implications of this transfer of Malay territory to Buddhist Siam. The move will affect the attitude not only of the Malay population of the ceded territories, but of Malays throughout the Peninsula. Malays on both sides of the British Malaya-Siam border have always resented Siamese interference with Malay communities and have readily appreciated the better standard of living, administration and justice prevailing south of the border.

Till the construction of the Peninsular Railway in 1922, Siamese interference with the Malays, even on the Siamese side of the border, was comparatively superficial, but after the Siamese Revolution of 1933, the Siamese government's policy towards the

===== Page 111 =====
Malays became actively "nationalistic." It was designed to extinguish the language, customs, and religion of the Malays, and make all the various elements in the area shown as Siam on the map think and act as one Siamese people. Malay men were forbidden to wear their characteristic sarong and songkok (Mahomedan headwear worn by all Malay men and boys of the Peninsula) and were advised to wear clothes of semi-European design. The women were denied their kebaya and selendang (scarf worn over the head by the Mohamedan Malay women and used as a veil when necessary) and told to wear hats. The use of Malay names was forbidden, and all official business, verbal or written, had to be conducted in the "national language," Siamese. The Mohamedan Friday holiday was forbidden. A law was passed forbidding religious instruction to anyone under 14 years old, though Buddhism was actively taught in the schools as "history." As very few Malay children indeed received education beyond the age of 14 years, it was inevitable that the Buddhist instruction thus received in childhood would in a generation or two modify the beliefs of these hereditary Mohamedans. Only Buddhists could hold official posts; all transactions and accounts had to be recorded in Siamese; signboards and advertisements were heavily taxed if not in Siamese.

The purpose of this policy was apparent and abhorrent to even the lowliest Malay. Malay resentment against the Siamese was strong in the years immediately before the Pacific War, but naturally the Siamese drew no attention to the problem, and it had long been expedient for Britain to treat the condition of Malays in Siamese territory as being no concern of hers. The matter, therefore, received little publicity, but it is a state of affairs which has exasperated many inarticulate Malays, and there is no doubt that the Japanese will now incur the hostility of the Sultans and peoples of the transferred states and the resentment of Malays throughout the Peninsula. By this act the Japanese and Siamese have seriously prejudiced their chance of conciliating the Malays.

===== Page 112 =====
FORTNIGHTLY INTELLIGENCE REPORT NO: 8.

Far Eastern Bureau,
British Ministry of Information,
New Delhi.

Period ended
July 3rd, 1943.

MALAYA & SUMATRA.

POLITICAL.

1. Foreigners in Malaya: In June 1942, approximately the following European neutrals remained in Singapore: 50 Danes; 150 White Russians; 60 Swiss and 40 of various nationalities, including Germans, Italians and Hungarians. All were known to the police of the former administration and most of them were freed from prisons or internment camps by the Japanese Army. The Japanese authorities were very anxious to reduce the number of these Europeans on Singapore Island. (Report of a French Official who left Malaya in July 1942).

2. Under-Nourishment: By July 1942 there were large numbers of persons in Singapore suffering from under-nourishment. (Same source as in para 1).

3. Japanese Treatment of Malayan Peoples: At least up to July 1942, the Japanese had treated the Malay population leniently and they were obviously trying to win the goodwill of Malay youth. It was the Chinese who suffered most and the Chinese population was deeply hostile towards the occupying forces. Eurasians had suffered greatly as it was not clear whether the Japanese would allow them to work for their living. Eurasian families had been broken up and many of the women were forced into prostitution. (Same source as in paragraph 1).

4. Masuzo Fujimura: Fujimura, Chief of the General Affairs Department of the Military Administration in Malaya, declared in Singapore on June 30th upon his return from Tokyo, that the extent to which the native inhabitants of Malaya would be allowed "political participation" would depend upon the "actual conditions in the various occupied areas". He added that so long as they co-operate with the Military Administration "their economic organizations and labour activities would be aided". Comment: The status of a Malayan Sultan has recently been no better than that of a "Wazir" to the Japanese Governor of his State. The Malays and Chinese are admitted to the administration only in so far as they serve the purpose of the Japanese. There is doubtless still an appearance of indirect rule but the sovereignty of the Sultans has been lost and this must be apparent by now even to the rayat.

5. Pro-British Feeling: The French source referred to in paragraph 1 above reported that at least up to July 1942, pro-British feeling had strengthened after the fall of Singapore and there was a general hope among the Malays and Chinese that the British would return. In general, Malayans had come to understand that Japanese occupation meant ruin,

===== Page 113 =====
Desolation, death, poverty, famine, unemployment, the return of malaria, the invasion of inhabited localities by the jungle, and economic disorganisation following organised pillage. They were certainly more Anglophile now than they were before, and it was not unusual to hear them confidently predict the return of the English. The belief was general that the return of the English would mean the resurrection of a fuller life and an assured standard of living. Some of the Indians were probably less eager for the return of the British.

6. Heiho: Auxiliary Service Recruits: The Sultan of Kelantan presented Heiho (Auxiliary Service) recruits with cigarettes, and several thousand well-wishers thronged the ferry depot of the Kelantan river to give them a rousing send-off. (Domei in English from Singapore, June 23rd). Comment: Formation of the Heiho may prove an important Japanese move. The ferry pier on the Kelantan river at Kota Bharu, about half a mile upstream from the railway terminus at Palekbang on the opposite shore, has been a scene of considerable pomp in the past. There is an open space from the ceremonial pier on the river bank for about 200 yards to His Highness' Astana and Balai (court and wall-enclosed council chamber). All outdoor ceremonies such as the official arrival or departure of the High Commissioner, the British Adviser, visiting Sultans, the Armistice Day celebrations, and the like took place in this open space.

7. Further Co-operation Requested: On June 30th, Naoaki Hattori, Chief of the Internal Affairs Department of the Military Administration of Sumatra, speaking at a conference of representative leaders from Atjeh, Palembang, and other Provinces, requested further co-operation from the people in view of Tojo's promise that the natives should be permitted to participate in the Government of their country. (Tokyo in Japanese, June 30th, 1200 GMT, on 25.37 m.; Tokyo in Burmese, June 1st, 1500 GMT, 21.01 m.; and Domei in English, July 1st).

8. Shigeo Odate: Shigeo Odate, till recently the Mayor of Singapore, has been appointed first Governor to Tokyo-to (Tokyo Metropolis). (Domei in English, June 23rd). Comment: Odate was formerly Home Minister and was appointed Chief Military Administrator and Mayor of Singapore last year.

9. Singapore Custodian of Enemy Property: On May 22nd, 1942, the custodian of enemy property in Singapore invited tenders for all the stock in trade, office furniture, and fittings of S.G. Parratt & Sons and D. Rendle, and issued notices with regard to Donaldson & Burkinshaw, a Singapore legal firm, and the British-Malay Trustee and Executor Co. Ltd. The Movable Property and Business Branch of the Custodian of Enemy Property, Singapore, was situated on the top floor of the Supreme Court building. (Syonan Times, May 22nd, 1942).

10. K.M. Engelmann: On May 21st, 1942, K.M. Engelmann was appointed Special Manager by the Custodian of Enemy Property for the Municipality of Singapore to

===== Page 114 =====
Wind up the Standard Engineering Company Ltd., the Singapore-British Engineering Corporation (Malaya) Ltd., and the Malayan Wire Mesh and Fencing Co. Ltd. (Syonan Times, May 22nd, 1942). Comment: Engelmann is a German.

11. Indian Independence League: Ras Behari Bose and Subhas Chandra Bose: Much attention was paid to the I.I.L. in Japanese propaganda during the fortnight. Ras Behari Bose, President of the I.I.L., and Subhas Chandra Bose, who since his "escape from Calcutta" has appeared in Berlin and Tokyo, both arrived in Singapore from Tokyo on July 2nd, "to plunge into battle for India's liberation". They were met at the airport by members of the Headquarters of the I.I.L., President of the Malayan branch of the League, and by prominent members of the Malayan and Sumatran branches of the League in Singapore, and thence proceeded on a round of calls on high Japanese officials. Subhas Chandra Bose is said to have "electrified" the Indian community, "from humble labourers to merchant princes," in his 40-minute broadcast from the Singapore Broadcasting Station which was picked up by "members of the I.I.L. throughout Malaya, gathered at their local headquarters". The gist of the propaganda is that "Japan will resort to every conceivable measure to oust the Anglo-Americans from India for India's complete independence". "Gratitude rallies in Malaya and other parts of East Asia" have been held by Indians to express appreciation "for Tojo's promise", his "momentous utterance", of June 16th, of "all-out assistance to India". At the Singapore rally, M.K. Chidambaram spoke in Tamil and "Major Singh" (Mohan Singh?) in Hindustani. As one of the propaganda preliminaries arranged for the coming of the Boses, there was a Military review of the Indian Volunteer Corps in Kuala Lumpur on June 22nd, and "dramas depicting the Indian determination to crush British imperialism were shown at the Nippon Gekijo on June 22nd and June 24th", at Singapore. (Domei in English, June 19th; Tokyo in Biamese, June 22nd, 1400 GMT, 25.42 m.; Seigon, "Free India Radio", in Hindustani, June 22nd, 13.45 GMT, 25.47 m.; Domei in English from Singapore, June 23rd; Domei in English, June 27th; Batavia in English, June 27th, 10:30 GMT, 16.6 m.; Domei in English, June 29th; Batavia in English, July 2nd, 10:30 GMT, 16.6 m.) Comment: M.K. Chidambaram, a Tamil lawyer's clerk about 45 years old, formerly of Sisson & Dalay, joined the legal firm of Laycock & Ong in 1940.

12. I.I.L.: Azad School: Fifty-eight students of the Azad School who had undergone military training for the previous two months "to enable them to serve the Motherland", demonstrated military arts in the presence of Japanese dignitaries at a graduation ceremony on June 29th. They were to be sent as "instructors to the Youth Movement recently started by the I.I.L." (Domei in English, Singapore Service, June 30th)

ECONOMIC

13. Rice Control in Singapore: On May 15th, 1942, the Singapore Rice and Paddy Distributing Association, 75 Boat Quay, Singapore, publicly issued a notice to rice retailers ordering them: (i) To report to the Association within 7 days, (ii) To submit their returns with the consumers' signatures thereon, (iii) Not to refuse to sell to anyone who wants to buy rice provided the sale is limited to one week's ration i.e. 5 katties to a person, (iv) To keep an accurate record of their rice sales, (v) To exhibit on conspicuous labels the controlled prices of the various kinds of rice in the shop. The notice ended with the words "any rice retailers contravening these orders shall be severely punished." On the same day separate public notice was

===== Page 115 =====
Given to rice consumers ordering them (i) to report the number of persons in each family to their nearest rice retailer; (ii) to get a receipt for their purchases from the rice retailer; (iii) to report immediately "any rice retailer contravening these orders." The notice continued, "Syonan people can buy rice without a permit at government control price, but consumers must not buy more than one week of their rice requirements." (Syonan Times, May 22nd, 1942).

Comment: The functions of the Food Controller under the British Administration with regard to rice and paddy appear to have been taken over by the Singapore Rice and Paddy Distributing Association.

14. Milk Unavailable: The French informant referred to in para 1 reported that milk was impossible to find after a few months of Japanese occupation.

15. Increasing rice lands: It is proposed "to develop approximately 900 square miles, hitherto undeveloped, into a fertile plain centering round the Pahang river." A survey corps is to be formed "with a view to constructing irrigation works" and 1,200,000 yen has been appropriated to subsidize families migrating to open up the land. (Domei in English, Singapore Service, June 30th).

Comment: There will be no shortage of urban Chinese willing to move on to the land but the Malays will not welcome them.

16. Malacca Chinese admitted to Agricultural land: "Extensive arable land" in the various districts of Malacca are to be issued under temporary occupation licences to Chinese through the Overseas Association "which will grant each applicant three acres of land and a loan of $100 to develop the plot." (Domei in English, June 19th)

Comment: There is very little potential paddy land left in the Settlement of Malacca, i.e., the Alor Gajah and Jasin districts, this territory being one of the oldest-developed and mostly thickly-populated Malay areas in the whole Peninsula. It is probable that the Chinese are being allotted land in jungle reserves along the Jasin-Merlimau road and in the direction of Nyalas for the production of tapioca, maize, etc. There is much open lalang grass country, the site of former soil-exhausting gambier production, on the lower slopes of Gunong Ledang (Mount Ophir), but the Chinese will probably secure the allotment to them of conveniently situated and less exhausted soil. The Malays of Malacca will not welcome this intrusion of urban and extra-Malacca Chinese.

17. Experimental Farms. Trengganu: One 224 acre experimental farm, and possibly others, is to be established 12 miles south of Kuala Trengganu. The farms are to encourage the production of foodstuffs and to teach young men "the superior Japanese agricultural technique and labour spirit" and make them "future leaders in agriculture." (Domei in Japanese, June 29th).

Comment: This may well be an attempt to hasten development of the newly opened lands to the south of the Kuala Trengganu-Kuala Breng Road at about the 12th mile. Dr. Scharff, Public Health Officer, Singapore, and noted malariologist, inspected this area in about September 1941, when much of it had been freshly cleared. He predicted that the project would be a failure on the ground that the nature of the soil, outcrops of rock and conditions of seepage on the slopes rendered adequate anti-malarial measures impracticable. In all other respects this area was attractive to the pioneer Malays, who were disinclined to accept advice.

===== Page 116 =====
18. Trengganu Irrigation Embankment Works: The Trengganu Government has decided upon "the construction of irrigation works with a view to obtaining two rice yields" per annum and also "to build embankments along sections of the coast to safeguard agricultural lands." (Donei in English from Singapore, June 30th) Comment: Though an agricultural Malay State, Trengganu imported some of its rice from Siam before the war. During the non-season from about October to March when ships cannot cross the bar at Kuala Trengganu, Trengganu is sometimes completely cut off from the rest of Malaya, as the route south to Kuantan in Pahang (see para 37) becomes impassable and sections of the road North to Kelantan are sometimes flooded for days on end. The Trengganu Government used to keep large stocks of Siamese rice against this risk.

19. Rewards to Rice-growers: Major-General Masakichi Itani, Governor of Penang and Province Wellesley, recently visited the Bukit Merah paddy area and rewarded an old Malay woman who "successfully grew a second crop of paddy with Taiwan rice." He presented "merit certificates" and "gifts" to nine others for their efforts. (Donei in English, Singapore Service June 30th). See F.I.R. No.4 Malaya and Sumatra, Appendix 1.

20. New Offices in Northern Perak: Naoksta Kawamura, Governor of Perak, visited the Krian rice area in Northern Perak on June 28th and inspected "the proposed new site for Government offices and quarters." (Donei in English, June 29th.) Comment: Such offices and quarters would presumably be at Parit Buntar, but might be at Bagan Serai, where there is a large Government rice mill. There would seem to be no activity in this area of N. Perak to justify offices and quarters on any extensive scale. Probably the importance of "revolutionising" Malay rice growing methods in this large rice area has called for the installation of a Japanese District Officer for when existing accommodation at Parit Buntar or Bagan Serai would be inadequate. See F.I.R. No.4 Malaya and Sumatra, para 39.

21. Copra: Inspectors sent in May by the Malaya-Sumatra Fats and Oils Council to all the States of Malaya reported on June 7th that the "sluggishness" in the collection of copra was due to the low price and to the increased domestic demand for coconut oil. They attributed this increased demand to the growing use of coconut oil for cooking, lighting, etc., instead of groundnut oil, bacon fat and kerosene, which were in short supply. There had also been an increase in the production of coconut sugar and coconut spirit brought about by the shortage of ordinary sugars and spirit. The use of copra in the preparation of rubber coagulants, acetic acid, etc., had also increased. Methods recommended to remedy this situation were (a) an increase in the price of copra and coconuts; (b) increased refining of substitutes for coconut oil, such as palm oil; (c) limitation of the domestic preparation of coconut oil and copra pulp. The price recommended for copra was S.S. $7 a picul, representing $3 per picul for the coconuts (one picul equals 300 fruit) and $4 for the cost of preparing copra. (Donei in Japanese, June 11th). Comment: Before the Japanese occupation, the average crop of copra for Malaya brought around $3 per picul. This price was so low that most of the coconut crop went unreaped. At that time $4.50 was thought the lowest reasonable working price. Palm oil was never popular as a substitute for coconut oil.

22. Horse Rearing: The Sumatra Military Administration is attempting to rear more horses and "to improve the breed." (Donei from Singapore, June 1st) Comment: Sumatran

===== Page 117 =====
Ponies are mostly stockily built, clean-legged beasts of eleven to twelve hands. They have been extensively used by the Sumatran planters, and the Netherlanders have done a certain amount of selective breeding. Horses are used in Java and Sumatra much as they are in India. In Malaya there was no horse traffic, the only horses and ponies being privately owned, luxury animals. Sembawang Island was another source of ponies for the Netherlands Indies.

28. Java Immigrants in Sumatra: The Military Administration in Solo (the Soerakarta region of Java) are sending fifteen local families comprising 75 members to the Soekadana District of Lampong, Sumatra, in order to ease population pressure in Java as well as to develop Sumatra. A further seven families will be sent from other provinces for the same purpose. (Donei in English from Singapore, June 23rd). Comment: This emigration appears from the figures given to be on a much more modest scale than might have been expected after Japanese announcements made last year when the figure of 300,000 migrants a year was suggested. The Dutch Administration had for some years carried out a long-term scheme of migration from over-populated, poor parts of Java to undeveloped regions of Sumatra. The number of immigrants increased yearly, and by 1939 was in the thousands. It was a most carefully evolved scheme, being backed by well-thought-out propaganda to the communities concerned. Every effort was made to move communities integrally, in order to preserve their social and cultural structure intact.

24. Self-Sufficiency: A 5-year Economic Development Plan has been announced which aims at "making the Southern Regions self-sufficient, and at the same time permitting the Japanese to concentrate all possible effort in defeating the Anglo-American enemies". The first step in Malaya will be to develop the iron mining and smelting industry and the production of aluminium from bauxite. "All necessary basic equipment will be provided from Japan, while the technical guidance will be by Japanese experts". "It is impossible to expect production to be carried out to a finished stage on the spot" so the products of Malayan industry "will be forwarded to industrial centres in Japan for final processing". The plan "will alleviate shipping trouble". (Donei in English, June 21st).

25. Negri Sembilan Board of Industries: It is proposed to grant subsidies "to deserving new local industries" in Negri Sembilan. A Board of Industries has been appointed to this end. (Donei in English, June 29th). Negri Sembilan is fast developing light industries. (Donei in English from Singapore, June 23rd). Comment: Negri Sembilan is an agricultural state producing rice and rubber. The light industries pictured by the Japanese are presumably soap, cherry and aerated waters manufacture, production of coconut oil for lighting, and the like.

26. Singapore Tropical Products Exhibition: This exhibition closed on June 30th "with a simple ceremony, when certificates and prizes were awarded to successful exhibitors". Another exhibition "on an even larger scale" is to be held in Singapore at the end of the year. (Donei in English from Singapore, June 30th). 325,000 people had visited the exhibition, according to Batavia in English, July 1st, (1030 GMT, 16.6 m.) and 35,000 according to Domei in English, July 1st. (See also F.I.R. No.4, Malaya and Sumatra, para 34 and No.6, para 19).

===== Page 118 =====
27. Motor Cars and Scrap Metal Shipped to Japan: According to the French source quoted in para 1, thousands of cars were loaded on Japanese ships, and thousands more, abandoned on the roads, were eventually cut up for scrap and sent to Japan.

28. Shipments from Japan: From October 1942 to February 1943, there were big shipments of miscellaneous goods from Japan to Singapore on three occasions. (Domei in English, June 14th). Forty-four registered retailers in Selangor have been licensed to sell "recently arrived goods from Japan," which range from bicycle spare parts to various Chinese medicines. (Domei in English, Singapore Service, June 24th). Comment: Only a very small quantity of manufactured goods has been shipped from Japan to Malaya. The potential demand is certainly not supplied. Doubtless licenses to sell and distribute much-wanted goods are eagerly sought. There must be considerable opportunity for corruption.

29. Southern Development Bank, Fort de Kock: "The local liaison office" of the Southern Development Bank is to be raised in status to a Branch Office in view of the "increased activities." (Domei in English, June 29th. See also F.I.R., Malaya and Sumatra, No.7, para 18; No.3, para 12; No.2, para 22; and No.7, Borneo, para 3)

30. Third Singapore Lottery: The sale of tickets for the third official Singapore lottery began on June 1st. The drawing is to take place at the Shonan Kokaido on August 10th. (Domei in English from Singapore, June 1st). See also F.I.R., Malaya and Sumatra, No.3, para 15; No.2, para 11. Comment: The Kokaido is presumably the Victoria Memorial theatre.

31. Sumatra Lottery and "Absorption of Surplus Funds": "With a view to absorbing the surplus purchasing power as well as to ensure revenue, the Military Administration of Sumatra will shortly sell 800,000 guilders worth of lottery tickets, issued at the rate of 100,000 guilders per month. The tickets will be one guilder each, and the first prize will be 10,000 guilders ..." (Domei in English from Singapore, June 23rd; Domei in English, June 24th, and Tokyo in Japanese, June 23rd, 800 GMT, 25.37 m.)

32. "Donations": The Overseas Chinese Association of Malaya have donated 17,709 dollars to the Bukit Timah memorial and the Shonan Shrine. (Domei in English, July 1st). See also F.I.R. No.6, Malaya and Sumatra, para 3.

33. "Fishery Consultation Offices": Kelantan Coast is to be divided into three zones controlled by "Fishery Consultation Offices" to give guidance and advice to the 6000 fishermen there and to improve their primitive method of fishing. (Domei in English, June 29th). Comment: The three coastal sectors will almost certainly be controlled from Sabak, Bachok and Cherang Ruku, which were the three main fishing villages before the invasion. Fishing has always been one of the major Kelantan industries. The Kelantan coast has long been divided into sectors to control the collection of turtles' eggs under licence. Given vessels with refrigeration plant, a considerable increase in catch with export to Singapore will be possible. The S.S. Fisheries Department were constructing two boats for this purpose in 1941.

===== Page 119 =====
Increase in catch with export to Singapore will be possible. The S.S. Fisheries Department was constructing two boats for this purpose in 1941.

34. Lumber: The Mitsubishi Trading Company is to be allowed to exploit the lumber reserves of Pahang State, which are estimated at about 300,000,000 tons. (Donei in English from Singapore, June 1st). See also F.I.R. No.7, Malaya and Sumatra, para 15.

Comment: Timber extraction has hitherto been in the hands of Chinese licensees under the direction of the Forest Department. Although reckless deforestation is not a feature of Japanese policy in normal times, at least in Japan and Formosa there is evidence to show that the Japanese in their haste to secure Malayan self-sufficiency in foodstuffs and timber for wooden ships, are exploiting Malaya's soil and forests with little regard for the country's long-term needs. As Major Colin Marshall, Malayan Forest Service, remarks in his note on British Forest Policy in Malaya (see Appendix I), "it is very easy for the Japanese to produce large quantities of timber by cutting without consideration the future crop but the people will pay dearly for this in the years to come".

35. Alienation of Forest Reserves: 1,208 acres of forest reserve in Perak has been alienated "for the cultivation of foodstuffs", (Donei in English, June 29th), and "a further 500 acres of forest reserve are now under survey for cultivation purposes". (Donei in English, June 30th).

36. "The Perak River Hydro-Electric Power Company's dam and plant on the Perak River at Chenderoh were restored to service" a year ago. (Donei in English from Singapore, June 24th).

Comment: The Perak River Hydro-Electric Power Company was much the largest of the few privately operated electrical undertakings in Malaya, and Perak was exceptional among the States of Malaya in that it was the only one in which a private company had been encouraged to overshadow the Government-provided electrical power installation system. By harnessing the Perak River at Chenderoh, and running certain steam power stations, the Perak River Hydro-Electric Power Company, with its subsidiary the Kinta Electrical Distribution Company, had long provided current to the Kinta tin fields and approximately 75% of the total electricity consumed in the State of Perak. Until 1935 the remainder of the State's electricity was provided by small Government-run power stations.

The Perak River Hydro-Electric Power Company was London-promoted and had received great initial, financial and other assistance from the F.M.S. Government and had proved a source of anxiety and financial embarrassment in return. In 1935 "a scheme of reorganisation of capital ... a difficult operation" involving the writing off of the F.M.S. Government's investment of some $1,800,000 was effected, the Company being allowed "well in advance of its immediate needs" (as its Chairman put it at the Fifteenth Annual General Meeting on December 9th, 1941), to acquire certain of the small excellently run electrical undertakings of the Government of Perak, and to develop towards the ultimate monopoly of a public utility which had hitherto been largely State-run throughout Malaya. This acquisition was not possible without official sanction and the official sanction was secured despite the Company's history of cost to the Malayan community and the general public's satisfaction at the cheapness of the current supplied efficiently and economically by the small electrical undertakings of the Perak Government. The ultimate political issue at stake was whether the Malayan Government or a London company should develop the proposed "Malayan grid system" of electrical distribution and the Malayan public had no say in the matter. The Chenderoh Dan [POSSIBLY MISSING].

===== Page 120 =====
Had a valuable incidental function in flood control. If the dam and plant were completely repaired a year ago, they could not have been effectively damaged.

37. Kota Bharu-Singapore Road: "A through highway connecting Kota Bharu and Endau is to be completed by the end of this year ... thus contributing much to the economic development of eastern Malaya." The new highway will run southward from Kota Bharu in Kelantan to Endau via Kuala Trengganu, Dungun, Kemaman, Kuantan, and Pekan. "Bridges are being constructed over the Kelantan River, Dungun River, Trengganu River, Pahang River, and others. These will be completed by the end of this year." (Domei in English, June 30th, and Domei in English Singapore Service, June 30th).

Comment: Since 1939 it has been possible to motor in dry weather from Kota Bharu in Kelantan to Pekan in Pahang. Portions of this road are along the seashore, the most precarious section on sand being near Tanjong Cherating and the worst through swamp inland from just north of Tanjong Gelang to Kuala Balok. A good road has long existed from Singapore up to Mersing and thence it was possible to take a motor vehicle north to Endau. Since 1938 the section from Pekan to Nenasik had light bridges and was fit for Austin Sevens only. The road trace from Nenasik to Endau was a mere track and there were no bridges or ferries at Kuala Rompin and Kuala Pontian in 1941. It is quite conceivable that the Pekan-Endau section of the E. Coast Road, the only few miles still unnegotiable to motors before the invasion, can be opened to traffic "by the end of this year." It could have been done in 1941 for about $20,000 but it is almost inconceivable that within one year the road throughout from Kota Bharu to Singapore will be made "24 ft wide" or that the major ferries of the existing system will give way to bridges. There is comparatively little potential traffic over this route and the obstacles are great. Much of the road is over loose unprepared sand, through mangrove swamps and over hills of slippery clay. In wet weather the route often becomes impassable. Bridges capable of resisting the annual floods during the east coast monsoons would cost many millions of dollars. Unless the Japanese attach strategic importance to this route, it may be taken that bridges will not be constructed across the Trengganu and Pahang rivers and that a bridge across the Kelantan River near Kota Bharu is possible but unlikely. The great Peel and Guillemand Bridges of the F.M.S.R. in Kelantan at Kemubu and downstream from Temangan, on the Kuala Lipis-Kota Bharu section of the F.M.S.R., were destroyed as the British retreated, as were the tunnels on this line. The line has not been reported open. It would seem probable that they would restore the railway before the road. See also Perak-Kelantan road construction in F.I.R., Malaya and Sumatra, No. 3, para 35. It is however readily appreciated that no totalitarian state holds up strategic road or bridge building on financial grounds. Also possibly the Japanese are creating work for the unemployed estate coolies of Pahang and elsewhere in the construction of these otherwise uneconomic roads. But the most probable explanation is that the Japanese for propaganda purposes are making rash claims and boasts about a little-visited area and that the E. Coast route is very much what it was in 1941. (Fuller information on the E. Coast route will be given on request).

38. Medan-Port de Kock Bus Services: A Government-operated bus service has been opened between Medan and Bukit Tinggi (Fort de Kock). (Domei in Japanese, June 19th).

===== Page 121 =====
39. Kampar River Bridge: A large wooden bridge 168 metres long and 4 metres wide is reported to have been built across the Kampar River in Riouw Province, Sumatra in 30 days by 48 Japanese soldiers with the assistance of over 1,000 workmen. The bridge has been named Sanrokubashi and stands at a point west of Bangkirang. (Donei in English, from Singapore, June 25th, and Donei in English, June 26th). Comment: This must be the bridge to replace the ferry over the Kampar River where it is crossed by the main Pakan Bharu - Fort de Kock road. The rivers on this route to the East Coast are swift, and it will be interesting to see whether flood risks have been anticipated. The Kampar River flows into the sea opposite Singapore and the Riouw Archipelago.

40. The Japanese claim to have opened a newly built highway across Central Djambi Province, from Fort de Kock to Muarotebo. Government buses make a round-trip daily on this route. (Tokyo in English, June 27th, 905 GMT, 19.86 m.)

41. Singapore-Philippines Telegraph Service: Public telegraph service in Japanese and two European languages was opened between Singapore and the Philippines on June 10th. (Donei in Japanese, June 10th)

SOCIAL AND CULTURAL

42. Courts: According to the French source referred to in para 1, the former British laws were still provisionally in force up to July 1942.

43. Vaccination and Inoculation: Singapore: The Japanese claim that 800,000 people of Singapore were vaccinated and injected for typhoid last year. (Donei in English, June 26th). Comment: 800,000 is probably a Japanese overestimate of the population of Singapore which was just under half a million at the 1931 census and was probably approaching three quarters of a million at the time of the Japanese invasion.

44. Training Institute for Malayan Officials: On May 18th, 1942, an official training institute for 80 Malayan government officials ... Malays, Indians and Chinese, known as the Syonan Ko-ua (sic. trans. Singapore Asia Promotion ) was opened near the former Japanese cemetery in Yeo Chu Kang Road. Wataru Watanabe, President of the Gunsei-bu (Military Administration), addressing selected officers from many parts of the Malay Peninsula told them that they would receive spiritual training, obey his instructions and those of the training officers and be willing to endure many hardships. He said that present hardships endured at the Institute would later instil obedience in the Malays. The course at this Axis Promotion Training Camp was to be three months, great emphasis being laid on the teaching of the Japanese language. (Syonan Times, May 22nd, 1942)

45. Kuala Lumpur Library: The Kuala Lumpur Library has established a Japanese literature section containing a wide range of books in Nippongo literature and language as well as periodicals and magazines. (Donei in English, June 27th, and Tokyo in English, June 27th, 905 GMT, 19.86 m.) Comment: The Library mentioned is probably the Kuala Lumpur Book Club which was the best library of contemporary literature in Malaya, and had moved into a new building behind the Selangor Club in 1940.

===== Page 122 =====
It was not a Government library, but the Government contributed towards its new building. This library was slightly damaged in the bombing of the Government offices in January 1943, but no books were lost.

46. Penang Swimming Club: The swimming pool of the Penang Swimming Club at Tanjong Bunge just outside Penang has been opened to the public by the authorities of the Japanese Administration. (Donei in English from Singapore, June 23rd). Comment: Donei remarks that the Penang Swimming Club was formerly an exclusive European rendezvous, and it is clear here and from other sources that the Japanese are making what little propaganda capital they can from the charge that the Europeans did not mix freely with the other races. This charge was becoming less and less cogent as Malaya's Asiatics steadily waxed in wealth. There was fast-increasing spontaneous and willing social intercourse between many of the better-off Chinese and Europeans in Singapore and Penang, and between the sophisticated Malays, Chinese, and Europeans in the more outlying parts of the country such as Kota Bharu.

47. Malay Students: 16 selected Malay students from those who have finished the prescribed course of the Military Administration in Singapore and are fluent in Japanese are to be sent to Japan to continue study in their chosen fields of agriculture, navigation, and technical science. (Donei in English, June 20th)

48. Higher Maritime Training School: A Higher Maritime Training School is to be established in addition to the Singapore Nautical School opened for youths up to 22 years of age. The Singapore Nautical School has trained a number of apprentice telephone operators. (Donei in English, July 2nd)

49. Japanese Language Schools in Johore: Donei in English, June 23rd, claimed that there are now more than 2,500 children attending 14 Japanese language schools in Johore. The education is free.

50. Ipoh Teacher's Training Institute: 139 teachers of the Ipoh Kyoin Yoseijo (Teachers Training Institute) are reported to have received graduation certificates from the Principal, Shichiro Banno. (Donei in English, Singapore Service, June 1st).

Higher Normal Schools in Malaya & Sumatra: It was announced on June 30th that the Military Administration office would shortly open higher normal schools in Padang and Kuala Lumpur to train native teachers to instruct the natives in the Japanese language and general subjects. 52. Medan Training Institute: 30 selected young men from the four sub-provinces under the jurisdiction of the East Coast Government have begun a three months' course at the Medan Training Institute. The course includes Japanese language and military drill.
