===== Page 154 =====
1. NAVAL AND SHIPPING.

(a) Movement of Vessels. Very few ocean-going steamers call at Singapore. At first, large gangs of Chinese and some Indians were employed in repairing the Naval Base and Keppel Harbour but the bulk were discharged after completion of this work. The wharves and godowns have now been repaired and all war damage has been cleared up. The Naval Base is said to be in use for warship repairs.

Of native craft, Chinese junks have been seen at Penang but no Japanese fishing boats, Sumatran prahus or Indian Ocean craft such as Arab dhows. No lighters are to be seen in Penang Harbour, but there are present on occasions small naval patrol vessels and small merchant ships.

The Japanese are encouraging passenger and freight traffic in small vessels and junks. The regulations for the control of junk traffic are strict but not always enforced. All junks and junk crews must be registered and also the journeys to be performed, although one registration is sufficient if the vessel trades regularly in the same area.

The main shipping movements are as follows:

(a) Regular services between Penang and Medan.
(b) Regular services between Penang and Klang.
(c) Regular services between Penang and ports on west coast of Siam, such as Kantang.

(d) Passenger service between Singapore and Kemaman, possibly continuing up the east coast of Malaya to Siam.

(e) Some trading between Singapore and South Sumatran ports.

One or two small steamships are employed on the Penang/Medan and Singapore/Kemaman runs but the greater part, especially of the coastal traffic, is carried by Chinese sailing junks and tongkans.

(b) Shipbuilding and Salvage. Although the Japanese have been doing their best to encourage shipbuilding, it is reported that there are actually few signs of it in either Singapore or Penang.

A certain number of small vessels which were sunk during the Malayan campaign have been salvaged inside the Singapore Breakwater. These salvaged vessels were broken up for scrap and a great deal of scrap iron has been shipped to Japan from this and other sources.

2. AIR.

10,000 men were employed last October in enlarging the former Singapore Civil Airport at Kallang.

3. MILITARY.

(a) Japanese Troops. It is reported that there are now very few Japanese troops in Malaya.

In Singapore the Japanese are billeted in the Chinese residential areas. The C-in-C of the Japanese Forces is living in Government House, Singapore.

(b) Guerilla Activity. The activities of the guerilla bands, which include some Australians, are directed mainly against those Chinese who are working for the Japanese. The guerillas have been particularly active on the Parak/Pahang Border, where they have been reinforced by volunteers raised by the Chinese Mobilisation Council who were trained in Singapore during the Malayan campaign. The guerillas have had encounters with the enemy in Kuala Lumpur, Ipoh and in the Betong-Grik-Kroh area, where they raided the police station at Koboh on the frontier. The Japanese sent troops to mop them up but the guerillas escaped into the Yala area of South Siam. Japanese reprisal methods are very thorough and villagers dislike guerilla activity in their area as a result.

The guerillas use bicycles; consequently there is a very strict control exercised over bicycles which now have to be registered and licensed.

There is no guerilla activity on the coast.

===== Page 155 =====
4. COMMUNICATIONS.

(a) Road. In Singapore, there are very few cars and they are used only by officials. The buses and trolley buses are run by the authorities, but the numbers available are only about half the pre-war figure.

There is no petrol issue to the public and consequently no rationing.

A travel permit is required for all journeys up country. The trip usually has to be made by charcoal-burning lorry, as there are few buses and no taxis. The bridge over the Perak River at Kuala Kangsar has been replaced by a wooden structure.

(b) Rail. Normal running conditions have been resumed on the main railway lines.

5. POPULATION.

(a) The Malays. Generally speaking, the Malays are inclined to be pro-Japanese and are largely employed in the police force, which has been enlarged, especially in the detective branch. However, there has been at least one case of harsh treatment of Malays. In July 1942, nine Malays were publicly beheaded because they retaliated when the Japanese entered their kampongs and interfered with their women. (Note: The observer was not a Malayan and his views on the attitude of Malays should be accepted as based on outward observation only. It is not likely that in present circumstances Malays would give any open indications of hostility to the Japanese.)

The Sultans enjoy much the same position as they did under British rule, and the friendly relations which are said to exist between the Sultan of Jehore and the Japanese have been made the subject of much local publicity.

(b) The Chinese. The Chinese are subjected to a number of restrictions and must hold occupation certificates and household certificates. The occupation certificate is merely an identity card and the household certificate gives information regarding the number of inmates in each dwelling place, being used as a basis for the issue of rationed articles.

All new arrivals must be registered, and when they reach Singapore from outside, a travelling pass must be produced together with an occupation certificate.

There is a well-developed "go slow" policy amongst labourers of the Chinese labour force, who do not hesitate to idle when their Japanese supervisors are not looking. There is talk amongst them of organized anti-Japanese riots.

In every big town there is an Overseas Chinese Association which raises funds for the Japanese and assists them to maintain law and order. Although the members of these associations are elected by the Chinese they must be finally approved by the authorities.

The former Secretariat for Chinese affairs has been abolished and Chinese affairs are now controlled by a State Secretariat through the Overseas Chinese Associations.

Dr. Lim Boon Keng is the President of the Chinese Association, which compelled the Malayan Chinese to take oaths of allegiance to the new regime, and contribute over 50 million dollars to its funds before June 1942.

Immediately after the capitulation, severe punishments were meted out all over the Peninsula to those Chinese who had helped in the war against Japan.

(c) Indian Population. The Indian police are functioning as before. There appears to be little I.N.A. propaganda.

Household certificates must be held by members of the Indian community but occupation certificates are not required.

From time to time the Indians hold meetings and processions, but it is difficult to see what useful purpose these serve as the demonstrations are usually broken up by the participants quarrelling amongst themselves.

The Indian community in general is reported to be anti-Japanese.

6. LIVING CONDITIONS.

(a) Rationing. The following foodstuffs are rationed:

- Rice - five catties (1 catty = 1 1/4 lb) per week.
- Sugar - one cattie per month.
- Salt - half a cattie per month.

It is believed that the rice ration was reduced to three catties per week as from October last. Ration cards are not required in restaurants and there are no restrictions on unrationed foods. Rations are the same for men, women, and children.

===== Page 156 =====
(b) Food Prices. Vegetables cost much the same as before the war but fish is expensive as the supply is limited because only a restricted number of boats are allowed to put out to sea.

Prices of rationed foodstuffs are controlled but there is an extensive black market. Prices are as follows:

Controlled Price. Black Market Price.

Rice - 8 cents per cattie 50 cents per cattie
Salt - 60 cents per cattie 1.50 cents per cattie
Sugar - 60 cents per cattie 2.00 cents per cattie
Pork - 60 cents per cattie 1.70 cents per cattie

(c) Commodities. There is a great shortage of imported goods. The effect of price control has been to encourage the hoarding of such commodities as matches, clothes and soap, which are difficult to obtain. Drugs are in great demand.

7. ECONOMIC.

(a) Rubber. The export of this commodity is in the hands of a monopoly who have set up an "Exporting Establishment" for the purpose.

Established firms are permitted to trade internally but have to ship through the monopoly. There are large rubber stocks in Singapore, Kuala Lumpur, and Penang. On the plantations, tapping is still going on but on a much reduced scale.

Three rubber shoe factories are working in Singapore.

The current price of rubber is 15 cents a pound.

(b) Trading and Smuggling. There is a considerable amount of smuggling from Malaya into Siam and the Japanese are prepared to assist in the practice even to the extent of lending military trucks. In return they expect introductions to the "houses of entertainment" and monetary gifts.

A large quantity of gold is smuggled into Siam where it commands a higher price than in Malaya. The smuggling is done by Chinese using jungle paths over the frontier.

A good deal of opium smuggling goes on inside Malaya in Japanese cars and trucks.

(c) Currency. Straits Dollar notes and Japanese military yen are equal in value and are both legal currency. Japanese notes are issued in the following denominations:

10 yen
5 yen
1 yen
50 cents

The Straits dollar notes are gradually being replaced by military yen but there has been no official announcement of their withdrawal. A number of the old British notes are being hoarded.

There is a black market for sterling notes, Chungking National currency and U.S. paper dollars.

The old coins are still in use but there is quite a shortage due to hoarding.

8. Hygiene.

All government hospitals have been taken over by the military authorities and medicines, particularly European medicines, are hard to obtain. Even quinine is scarce in civilian circles, although the military are apparently well supplied.

Vaccination and inoculation against cholera (typhoid?) is compulsory but it appears that no anti-malarial work is being carried out. The General Hospital, Singapore, is under an armed guard and apart from the facilities provided for vaccination and inoculation there are no Government medical services, free or otherwise.

9. DISSEMINATION OF NEWS.

(a) General. Since 15th August 1942, when the short wave wireless sets were confiscated, little news has come in except from Japanese sources. A certain amount of Allied news, however, has reached the public from Indian sources.

===== Page 157 =====
(b) Newspapers. The "Shonan Times", the successor of the "Straits Times", is published in English, and there is also the Chinese "Shonan Pao" in addition to an Indian publication and the Malay papers. Other Chinese papers are published in Penang and Kuala Lumpur.

All the papers are controlled by the propaganda department, the source of news being the Domei Agency. There is an acute shortage of paper, the "Shonan Times" consisting of one small sheet costing five cents.

10. JAPANESE PROPAGANDA AND POLICY.

In Singapore, the Japanese have a few trucks decorated with caricatures of prominent persons such as Mr. Churchill and Chiang Kai Shek. Loudspeakers operate in various languages from these trucks which tour the city.

Japanese newsreels, shown at all the cinemas, contain mainly propaganda. All the schools are under strict government control, separate schools having been set up for the different races. Students are compelled to study Japanese for two hours daily.

In Singapore, the "Hin Ah" training school trains young men for junior Government posts.

11. ALLIED P.O.W.

About 30,000 (?) British prisoners of war are interned at Changi and are strictly controlled by Japanese officers. Subordinate posts at the camp are held by Indians and Chinese who are not allowed to come into direct contact with the prisoners.

The Australian prisoners in Singapore are employed in manual work under Japanese supervision. They are not allowed to approach the public.

Malay soldiers have all been set free and some have been sent to Siam to work on the Siam-Burma Railway.

Indian troops have been transferred to the Indian National Army and the Indian Labour Corps.

Shonan (Domei): The representatives of 10,000,000 Moslems in Malaya and Sumatra will hold a great meeting on 5th and 6th April. Since the war, the Moslems have collaborated with Japanese military operations and the policy of the military authorities, carrying out the work of reconstruction of Asia for Asiatics. Their meeting will certainly establish the collaboration of all Moslems in Asia and will have a large repercussion among all Moslems in the world.

09:40 MALAYAN RECONSTRUCTION PLAN
Serenban: Statistics recently compiled by the Government of Negri Sembilan revealed a policy designed to create a strong middle class to ensure post-war Malaya against Communism. The same report disclosed that the control of movement of goods resulted in the growth of numerous light industries and small businesses. Registered businesses in the State today are 12 times that under the former regime, while the farming population has also greatly increased. Today, 3,000 new farmers were set up by the local government which gave three acres of land for cultivation to each farmer who previously had no land. Official figures show that at the end of a three-year plan in 1944, 10,000 such families will have been rehabilitated.

DOMEI (JUP) IN ENGLISH FOR EUROPE 4.4.43

10:57 SELANGOR'S INDUSTRIAL REVIVAL
Kuala Lumpur: November last year marked an industrial revival in Selangor under Hippon administration, according to the business registry of factories here. There has been a steady influx in the registration of factories for vegetable oil and tobacco products. Among the manufacturers catering for secondary necessities were those engaged in turning out buttons, toilet requisites, cosmetics, toothpicks, cowhide, leather, toothbrushes, soap and paper. Registration of companies, which grows monthly, promises to reveal more and more novel industrial enterprises.

14:45 MALAYA, SUMATRA: DEVELOPMENT OF ELECTRICAL INDUSTRY
Shonan: Fully realising the fundamental importance of electric enterprises in the development of industries in Malaya and Sumatra, the Japanese Military Administration in a recent announcement on its fundamental policy declared that electric enterprises will be placed under controlled management, and power will be supplied abundantly and cheaply under a controlled distribution plan.

According to competent authorities, existing electricity enterprises in both Malaya and Sumatra have already been fully reconstructed and are operating at an efficiency rate comparable to the pre-war rate, if not better, due to the untiring efforts of Japanese Army and civilian experts as well as the wholehearted cooperation of the native populace. New industrial development everywhere, however, demands greater power supply.

Existing facilities in both generation and transmission are regarded as inadequate and thus, to foster the development of the electricity industry in coordination with developments in other fields, the competent authorities have decided to institute a control plan. According to a draft plan for the rationalisation of the electricity industry, two supervisory offices will be established: one in Shonan and the other in Sumatra. Both will be manned by expert technicians whose task will be not only to conduct exhaustive research into power resources but also to supervise the development of such resources. Research will include the problem of harnessing rivers for the establishment of coal power plants. Supervision will be exercised not only over future development but also over expansion or removal of existing facilities. It is pointed out that both Malaya and Sumatra are fortunately favoured with great possibilities for developing hydro-electric generating plants, and there is no reason why these resources should not be fully developed and utilized side by side with the development of other natural resources.

===== Page 160 =====
14.34 CHEAP QUININE FOR SINGAPORE INHABITANTS
Shonan: The large-scale distribution of cheap quinine tablets to residents of Shonan Special Municipality has started through the police and hospitals. Quinine tablets will be sold at five sen each to enable all classes to obtain the benefits of the Municipality's present antimalaria drive.

Shonan: The representatives of 13,000,000 Moslems of Malaya and Sumatra yesterday held a meeting aiming at intensifying their collaboration with Japan in the prosecution of the war. A resolution was approved, in which those present swore to do everything possible for a happy conclusion of the present war, in gratitude for the generosity shown by Japan.

09.15 SUMATRA RECONSTRUCTION: MOSLEM SPOKESMAN
Shonan: Contrary to superstitious belief, Friday 13th March 1942 proved a most fortunate day for the people of Medan and the East Coast of Sumatra, for on this day Japanese Imperial troops entered Medan, thus sounding the death knell to Dutch oppression and tyranny which had ground down the country for centuries past, declared the spokesman of the Sumatran delegation at the Muslim religious conference in Shonan. (Sumatra's welcome to victorious Japanese troops: details of reconstruction).

===== Page 161 =====
08.07 SINGAPORE: "INDIAN NATIONAL WEEK": AMRITSAR RECALLED Shonan: For the first time in Malaya, Indians here will be given an opportunity to celebrate Indian National Week, commemorating the blackest week in the history of British oppression in India, the mass murder of thousands of innocent men, women, and children by the British authorities on 10th and 11th April, 1919, in the city of Amritsar. Shonan Indians are planning to hold rallies and mass meetings from 9th to 13th April inclusive. In the second week of April 1919, Gandhi burst on the Indian political firmament by leading the movement nullifying the hated Rowlatt Act, with which the British sought to cripple and kill Indian nationalism by martial law in the Punjab. At that time, Gen. Dyer, misconstruing peaceful demonstrations as a repetition of revolt, trapped thousands of Indians who were in a virtual trap. This marked the beginning of the unarmed war of 400,000,000 Indians against British tyranny.

During Indian National Week, Indians at home and abroad will pause to pay homage to those who died as martyrs in that grim week and will renew their determination to crush British imperialism, winning India for the Indians.

===== Page 162 =====
10.37 MALAYAN, SUMATRAN ESTIMATES APPROVED

Shonan: It is officially announced that with the approval of the Japanese Government, all budgetary estimates for the current fiscal year for Malaya and Sumatra have been put in effect since 1st April. Budgetary estimates for regions were based on the annual budget system, which was introduced for the first time this fiscal year. In compiling figures of both debit and credit items, special attention was placed on attainment of self-sufficiency in commodities, maintenance of peace and order, speedy development of natural resources, reconstruction of industrial facilities as well as the communication system.

Officials said ample appropriations had been provided for the development of maritime industry, engineering, agricultural and other important industries. Every possible effort will be made both in Malaya and Sumatra towards self-sufficiency in vital commodities such as foodstuffs and other daily necessities.

The military administration paid as much attention to debit accounts as to credit accounts in compilation of the budget. According to officials, of the total revenue anticipated for the current year, approximately 40 per cent will be realised through taxation, whereas the balance of funds required will be covered by sales of public property and income from government monopolies such as tobacco, railways and postage stamps.

6. MALAYA, SUMATRA, PHILIPPINES: MONEY TRANSFERS

Manila: To improve even more the trade in our occupied Southern Territories, the military authorities of Shonan, on 15th March, allowed the inhabitants of Malaya and Sumatra to transfer money to Burma, Java and North Borneo. On 12th April, transfer of money was allowed to the Philippines also.

23.44 MALAYAN BANK DEPOSITS INCREASE
Shonan: Bank deposits and loans throughout the Peninsula have increased tremendously. According to figures at the end of March, the deposits in Shonan Banks had reached 56,020,000 Straits Settlements dollars, an increase of 6,295,000 dollars over the previous month. Three Japanese Banks account for 33,913,000 dollars; six Chinese Banks rank next with deposits of 18,946,000 dollars; the remaining 3,261,000 dollars were deposited by two Indian Banks. In other regions of the Peninsula, bank deposits also show a steady increase. At the end of March the total reached 34,709,000 dollars. Of this amount, 19,560,000 dollars are held by 11 Japanese Banks, 13,180,000 dollars by 15 Chinese Banks and 1,954,000 dollars by eight Indian Banks. The figures indicate a remarkable increase over the preceding two months and is due to the development and rehabilitation of the Peninsula. Loans made by the Southern Development Bank to Japanese, Chinese and Indian Banks reached a total of over 100,000,000 dollars.

Shonan: Following a decision by the Military Administration, an Export and Import Control Association has been formed here for the control, collection and distribution of commodities in Malaya and Sumatra.

===== Page 165 =====
10.50 SINGAPORE RECONSTRUCTION (Domei correspondent). Phonan: Most striking progress has been made by Malaya in self-sufficiency in foodstuffs. Rice areas in the north, particularly Kedah, Sungei Nanik Valley, and the coastal regions of Selangor, have been expanded considerably. With the introduction of rice with a high yielding rate from Taiwan, rice hitherto grown once a year is now being harvested twice yearly. The total output of rice is more than sufficient to meet the demand of the 5,000,000 local inhabitants. At the present rate of production increase, Malaya need have no anxiety regarding food supplies. New ways of using excess rubber are being discovered. Gasoline is now being taken from rubber for use in automobiles and trucks. Crude rubber oil is used in anti-malarial work; poured over stagnant pools, it prevents mosquito breeding. Rubber crepe is used extensively as soles of shoes, replacing leather. Meanwhile, liquor, tobacco, condensed milk and other foodstuffs which formed a sizable proportion of Malaya's imports before the war, have been effectively replaced by local products. Local distilleries and breweries are today doing a flourishing business. Sales of tobacco and cigarettes, produced in Malacca and Penang, have increased greatly. Japanese and Thai tobacco products are also in keen demand.

DOMEI (AU2) IN ENGLISH FOR EUROPE 6.4.43

18.40 MALAY FINANCIAL RECONSTRUCTION (By Domei Staff Correspondent in Shonan) Since the liberation of Malaya from the oppression of Britain and U.S.A. by the Japanese, it has undergone a rapid economic change. Before the outbreak of war Malaya depended wholly upon outside sources for food and other commodities. Its imports were nearly one billion dollars, considered to be the highest in the East. Consequently the wage level and cost of living in Malaya is among the highest in the world. The ratio of notes in circulation to the people was so high as to encourage wasteful and luxurious habits to an alarming degree. Another inflationary factor was the unrestricted expenditure in 1940 and 1941 on defence works, which the imminence of war necessitated. It is known that the British spent vast sums of money on last minute improvements or alterations to the various defence works. The Japanese military authorities sterilised or absorbed excess money in the country under a planned economy. By encouraging interchange of goods among the Japanese occupied areas and distributing essential commodities, such as rice, sugar, salt and matches, business was gradually revived, facilitating note circulation through the hands of the authorities. This enabled them to impound and then release as much money as was required to finance the country's essential needs. At present in Malaya essential goods are rationed such as rice, sugar, salt, flour, matches, soap and other goods which are exceptionally cheap and available in sufficient quantities to meet normal requirements.

Meanwhile the people have done away with luxurious ways of living. Through local initiative and Japanese guidance almost every variety of imported goods has been replaced by adequate substitutes. The use of tooth powder made from local medicinal herbs in place of imported toothpaste proved such a benefit that dentists are said to be having a lean time. Women are getting along without using Max Factor cosmetics.

===== Page 166 =====
From: Major W.D. Shaw, M.I.2.a.

SECRET.

D.C./6388/M.I.2.a. 25th February, 1943.

Dear Colonel,

As promised, I enclose one copy of the report on conditions in MALAYA up to May 1942, which MONSON was interested in. The report was written by an Indian.

Yours,

Lieutenant-Colonel W.L. Rolleston,
Colonial Office,
S.W.1.

===== Page 167 =====
ATTITUDE OF LOCAL POPULATION.

(Note: Local refers to Singapore in particular and Malaya in general).

1. BEFORE 3rd SEPTEMBER, 1939.

A. General.

The local population is mainly Chinese, Indians, Malays, Eurasians, Europeans, other nationalities and, of course, Japanese. Obviously the Chinese have been very anti-Japanese, particularly after the infamous Mukden incident and after Chiang Kai Shek's dynamic leadership following the Sian capture by the "young" Marshal.

There had been a systematic boycotting of Japanese goods, secret underground activities, but as all these patriotic activities had always had their inspiration from the Chinese communists, the local Government was always in a very awkward position. Even before 1939 these demonstrations against the Japanese were also in a way connected with communism. The British and local police had a very difficult time with these agitators. The local Government could not openly allow hostile Japanese activities, as Japan was then not our enemy but our Ally; the primary concern of the local Government was to enforce law and order and this always was a very thankless task, especially in connection with the Chinese communists. Perhaps the best man to give authentic information on this subject is Mr. MEHTA PRITHVI CHAND, who is fortunately in India having left Malaya about a fortnight before the surrender.

B. Chinese.

This community can be divided into regional groups linguistically as they always call themselves according to the dialect they speak. Thus we have the Cantonese who come from the south of China in or around Canton. They are generally skilled workmen, and in fact almost all the skilled labour, with particular reference to the mechanical engineering trades, were Cantonese. The Harbour Board, the Naval Base, are staffed with Chinese. Thus this section can dictate terms in a crisis by holding up work and asking for impossible things. This they did in the Harbour Board and United Engineers only a few months before the Japanese war. This community is full of "bad hats", communists, terrorists, counterfeiters, burglars, secret society men, procurers, prostitutes, etc., and more Cantonese came into Malaya each year through the quota system than any other section of this community. But this section has its intellects too. Many of the brains of China come from here. However, this community has a more respected section - the Teochews - who are supposed to be the equivalent of our idea of the upper class of China. The usual surnames are Chiang, Cheang, Cheong, Chong, Wong, Wang, etc.

Then there are the Hokkiens who hold the monopoly of the eating and tea shop trade in Singapore. They are slightly taller on the average than the Cantonese and they come in order of demerit to the Cantonese.

The Hylams are usually cooks and boys whilst their women act as amahs, cooks, sewing women and keechile, little servant girls. They often act as informers to burglars and cases occurred of valuables being stolen from European houses in which they were employed. They are very good locksmiths and this helped them in their nefarious trade.

There is also a sprinkling of Formosans, Koreans, Manchurians, Mongolians, Hainanese and Khehs (who generally own pawn shops). It is mainly from this group that the Japanese recruited their 5th columnists.

2. AFTER THE DECLARATION OF WAR ON GERMANY AND BEFORE THE JAPANESE HOSTILITIES.

(a) After 3rd September 1939, the Chinese leaders were willing to cooperate in our war effort and did a lot in that way. But I (Radharkrishnan) have my doubts whether the Chinese community as such was very warm. I have heard much

===== Page 168 =====
Talk of Germany's brains, her scientific genius, capacity for organisation, her power of hero worship and regimentation, and then her invincibility - especially after the fall of France. Of course, this was all said in a very innocuous way without any chance of being charged for sedition.

(b) Then came the mating of two strange bedfellows in politics - Stalin and Hitler. This put the community on the edge of a dilemma, as it did everyone else, except perhaps deep students of politics who saw the bluff behind the facade. This led to a number of strikes by the communists and there were grave dangers of impeding the war effort in Malaya, which is known as the Dollar Arsenal of the Empire.

Almost all the labour in the tin mines was Chinese, and even though the preponderance of Indian labour in the rubber plantations gave a steady stream of rubber, the supply of tin was in fact interrupted by strikes.

The 22 months of this unholy pact was a trying time for Malayan labour, and I have a strong feeling that the fifth column racket matured and flowered during this period. I call it the formative period of the fifth column movement.

(c) After the declaration of war on Russia by Germany, the communists were forced to change their tactics and had no other alternative but to join with us. The Governor, with his usual wisdom, met the communist leaders. The Chinese community was now at last throwing its full weight on our side both officially and unofficially.

(d) The closing of the Burma Road gave a tremendous impetus to the fifth columnists, even civilians questioned our good intentions and the Governor was in a very unenviable position at that time. The interval between the closing and re-opening of the Burma Road brought the fifth column movement to its culminating point.

(e) The re-opening of the Burma Road was a palliative to tide over the fifth column activities. It did not, however, have the full benefits that might have accrued after such a decision, as there was civilian talk that we were playing hide and seek with China.

(f) The occupation of Indo-China and Hainan and the Spratley Islands by the Japanese, and the threat which accompanied the negotiations for oil in the Dutch East Indies, made the Chinese realise that hostilities in the Far East were imminent.

(g) The freezing of Japanese assets gave a fillip to the Chinese help towards winning the war and the communists were drawing nearer to us. It is a fact that the communists were and are a very serious factor to reckon with for good or for bad in the Chinese body politic. Leading Chinese who were definitely pro-Japanese were trying to get out of Malaya to Australia, India, Rangoon, Hong Kong and China itself.

It is astonishing for its accuracy, as well as for its forecast in that people were talking of a war in January 1942 and that Japan would strike from the north, that Siam would give right of passage in a slippery way and doublecross us, that Japan would strike in many directions and that Singapore would be taken in three months. Even coolies talked like this and asked educated folks whether it was at all possible.

(h) During the last week of November and the first week of December 1941 a subtle exodus of the Japanese began which was mainly by rail overland to Bangkok. They may never have gone as far as Japan, and in actual fact many came back with their armies into Malaya as administrators. There were wild rumours about this in these two critical weeks, but few people believed in them seriously, even though they all heard it. Such was the fifth column giving out facts, but in an unbelievable form; a subtle method of propaganda.

The Manager of the N.Y.K. Co. and the Bank of Taiwan were reported to have paid their staffs three months' salaries and confided in some of them that after three months they would be back under the Japanese flag. The proprietor of K.Baba & Co. was reported to have paid two of his Eurasian salesgirls six months' salaries.

===== Page 169 =====
and asked them to have a good time for three months, after which time they could work again in a bigger Baba & Co. and at salaries almost approximating to Europeans' salaries.

3. ON AND AFTER THE DECLARATION OF WAR BY JAPAN.

(i) The bombing of Singapore created some panic and an exodus of the poor classes commenced. The city presented almost a sinister appearance; it looked as if everything was in mourning. There was already talk, without any traces of origin, of the invincibility of the Japanese air force, their unconquerable army, their fighting qualities, their courage, and of their being a premier Asiatic race, championing the other Asiatic races against the whites, and above all the subtle colour bar against the Asiatics in Malaya. This kind of talk one could hear, but it was in such a novel way that it was hard to trace the source, and then there was a continual dinning into the ears of the uneducated coolies rumours of high wages under the Japanese, equal treatment, etc.

Even many Chinese were bewildered by the sinking of the Prince of Wales and the Repulse, and the lightning attacks by the Japanese at many points. This helped the fifth columnists tremendously. Then came the fall of Penang which more or less disheartened everybody, but in particular the Chinese. It was at this stage that the Government repatriated a very large number of Europeans, and few Asiatics, which resulted in the motives of the Government being questioned. The Penang radio under the Japanese made political capital of it telling the Chinese in particular what the "Whites" thought of Asiatic lives.

(j) It is my considered opinion that the fall of Penang, our repatriation of a large number of Europeans with few Asiatics, and above all the operation of the Penang radio under Japanese auspices, did us more harm than anything else on the civil side up to the time of the surrender. The Japanese exploited all the possibilities of propaganda even asking or forcing the Sultan of Perak, an unnamed Indian woman, a renegade European, an anonymous Indian leader and an anonymous Malay to broadcast. They harped on the hackneyed and age-old theme of the injustices committed by the Whites against the Asiatics, particularly the astute and intelligent Indians, the practical, yet misguided Chinese in the pay of the arch traitor to Asia - Chiang Kai Shek - the simple, docile and sophisticated Malays, who have been exploited and drenched on their own soil. Then GOD SENT THE JAPANESE FOR THE LIBERATION OF ASIA FROM THE WHITE SCOURGE. Our reply, particularly from Singapore, was very feeble.

(k) After the fall of Kuala Lumpur, there were mysterious rumours of milk tins selling at 6 cents (as against our 28 cents), bags of rice at 6 (against our 12) in the Japanese occupied territories, and the fatherly love of the Japanese for the Asiatic peoples in those regions.

(l) After the battle of Batu Pahat, there was a regular influx of up-country people, particularly Chinese, into the Island, and it would not be surprising if fifth columnists and real Japanese spies also filtered through. It was at this time that I heard from many sources of two secret Japanese transmitters operating on the Island and when I told Capt. F.B. Goude (the Principal of the Government Technical School, Singapore, and my boss) of this news he replied that he too had heard it and in addition that two European officers had been shot as spies.

From the time of the retreat from Batu Pahat till the battle of Johore, prominent Chinese still in Singapore began clearing out of Malaya, and I understood that Mr. Tan Kah Kee, leader of the China Relief Group, left about this time.

It was at this time that the communists were released from the jails by the Governor and within a few days the Chinese communists put about 2,000 semi-trained men in the field. They fought on the Singapore front and were called the Dalcos. It was reported that they fought extremely well.

(m) When Singapore surrendered on Sunday, 15th February, the Chinese public was dismayed. None in his wildest dreams had ever imagined a British surrender. I think even the fifth columnist was no exception. When the event became a fatal reality bewilderment gave place to a resolve to manage as best as possible under the new dispensation, which to the Chinese must have been an Inquisition. Slowly the Chinese community tried to steel itself against the catastrophe, and during and after the registration of all Chinese took all the ill-treatment very philosophically. But the mass executions of the Chinese, the beheading and the bayonetting, the forced

===== Page 170 =====
Prostitution that descended on their girls, the heavy contributions that they had to make in money to the Japanese War Fund, the subtle and devious ways by which they were dispossessed of their properties and estate, all these, instead of striking terror into their hearts, hardened them, and at the time of my leaving the Island had become a definite revulsion and a deep and a bitter hate. They were all simply waiting for better days.

(n) After the first few days of victory, the Japanese were all out for the Chinese in their newspapers. Despite their inquisitions, they started the old refrain of the Japanese and Chinese being brothers and the help of the Malayan and Dutch Chinese in building up the "New Order of East Asia", in carrying the banner of "Asia for Asiatics", and in the great "Cosmocracy under the aegis of the Imperial Sword" and the inexorable will of "His Imperial Majesty" the Japanese Emperor. The Japanese devoted a certain part of the newspapers for cultural propaganda about the affinity of the Japanese and the Chinese which the "Whites" were trying to undermine. They even tried to placate the Chinese by writing articles in a philosophical strain about the unnecessary bloodshed in China.

(o) During the celebration of the Japanese Emperor's birthday on 28th April, 1942, at Bras Basah Road, a couple of hand grenades were thrown. For this, the Japanese beheaded and tortured a large number of Chinese. Yet their newspapers spoke of the hearty celebration of the Emperor's birthday amongst all communities - particularly the Chinese.

(p) Reliable reports exist of a factory manufacturing hand grenades for the Chinese; the Japanese have not been able to locate it.

(q) Chinese guerrillas who have taken to the jungle near Kuala Lumpur are secretly rationed by civilian Chinese and shoot any Japanese stragglers who come their way.

(r) The Chinese have discovered a method of approach to the Japanese in the form of a Pacification Committee known as the Red Swastika Peace Committee. Dr. Lim Boon Keng is the leading light in this appeasement policy which is, in actual fact, a policy of marking time, and is trying to reorganise the community.

(s) The Chinese have recaptured the sugar business which had been their monopoly; they have in fact got back into their previous premier position as businessmen; even the Chinese auctioneering firms have reopened.

(t) The Chinese do a tremendous amount of our propaganda by listening secretly to our broadcasts and passing the news around. There is a great whispering campaign going on in the whole town. Thus the news of the bombing of Tokyo by us about the 20th April was known to the majority in Singapore, including even the coolies.

(u) The Japanese have taken away the money from the Chinese; they have dispossessed them of their money; they have raped many of the Chinese women; still they have not struck terror into the hearts of the Chinese. The Chinese never can, and never will, forgive the Japanese. It is in their blood - this hatred of the Japanese. It is paradoxical, but nonetheless true, that the Chinese can hate the Japanese and yet wait patiently for their revenge whilst still working under them, and even apparently for them, and yet upset the apple cart. It is difficult to understand this mentality - but it is there.

C. Indians.

BEFORE 3rd SEPTEMBER 1939.

(a) The Indian community in Malaya is mainly government-aided labour and is almost 80% of this community. This community is voiceless and illiterate though not uncommonly political capital used to be made of this material both in Malaya and in India in political agitations.

(b) This community is mainly from the Tamil parts of South India and is better off in comparison with similar labour in Southern India itself. It at least gets continuous labour and wages, whilst a certain amount of discipline is

===== Page 171 =====
Maintained with some medical attention, tolerable housing, and fairly hygienic living under almost modern conditions. This labour force is mainly in the rubber plantations, where there was a clamour for higher wages intensified by the coming into existence of a Congress ministry in the province of Madras, as well as the ban by that ministry on the emigration of labour from South India into Malaya. This topic was under discussion between representatives of the Malayan and Indian Governments. About this time a Congress agitator did some successful propaganda amongst these men. He was found, however, and deported by the Malayan Government. This was followed by labour strikes for higher wages, and the Malayan Government was compelled to open fire on the unruly mobs. This was one of the causes of discontent in this section of the Indian community. The other was the comparatively higher wages of Chinese labour and the displacing of Indian labour by Javanese and Boyanese. All this, in so far as my information goes, gave material for fifth column activities. It was quite easy to do successful fifth column work in the estates with impunity.

The evils amongst this community were the appalling dearth of women and the running of today shops on estates where the coolies used to spend more than a reasonable percentage of their wages. In fact, no coolie was free from debt.

There were very reliable reports of these coolies showing the way for the Japanese. Probably the rank and file might have been indifferent, an indifference born of ignorance, or they might have aided our own men who might have lost their way. But there were a good many who helped the Japanese, and it is just probable that these might have been fifth columnists.

(c) The urban labour force contained many bad hats and fellows with a smattering of political catchwords. This labour, especially in Singapore and Penang, was literate and could have held its own but for its lack of discipline. This force, without leadership and discipline, successfully went on strike from the municipal services for over a week. It was very unexpected from this section as even the well-informed dismissed it as very backward. Propaganda, radio, the German war, contacts with Chinese secret society men, communism, the press, particularly the leftist periodicals in Tamil from South India, books published with a Congress leaning, disappointment that nothing came from the strikes - all these added to the disgruntlement of this section with the result that the Japanese found excellent fifth column material. The Japanese replaced all the Chinese who struck work in the iron mines with Indian labour. This section used to talk in praise of Japan. No doubt their knowledge was very meagre, but then the Axis propaganda had its effect on this strata.

(d) The Malabar semi-skilled labour in constructional jobs, particularly the Naval and Air bases were far more literate than the Tamil urban labour section, and the Hindus, in particular, were better educated. These were all Congress-minded. The Mohamedans were in the main league-minded and not anti-British.

(e) A small and narrow wedge of casual labour harboured the rowdies, bad hats, newsmongers, rumour mongers, Japanese spies, counterfeiters, men belonging to Chinese secret societies and ex-convicts who had escaped from India. Perhaps the Japanese found very fine material in this section.

It is remarkable that few Indian labourers had their real names, and if asked they never gave the name of their village or town of origin but gave evasive replies.

(f) The Middle class were mainly wage earners as clerks, technical assistants, assistants in commercial engineering and private firms, etc. These men were not paid well. It may be because of competition, or they were not qualified for higher pay, but their wages were low. These people were generally anti-British though there were exceptions.

The Government servants, who also came from this class, were well-off and invariably they were pro-British. They were more educated and were better disciplined. Many of the Government servants were teachers.

===== Page 172 =====
(g) Then there were the high class Indians. There were many Government doctors who were paid well and were loyal. The lawyer class can be grouped under this section, and perhaps the so-called political leaders came from this group. This group did more harm than any other body of opinion in the community. I do not know why, but lawyers have a very great affinity for politics, and the results are often sinister. A full list of the lawyers who had organised the Indian Independence movement on the civil side is given in a separate appendix.

(h) The South Indian Mohamedan predominates in the merchant section of the community. These are men of moderate incomes and had business dealings with the Japanese but were invariably loyal. The richer merchants were Mohamedans from Bombay like the Suratees and they too were loyal.

The South Indian Hindu merchants did not concern themselves with politics, except for a lukewarm sympathy for the Congress which will evaporate if a subscription list is sent round.

The North Indian Hindu merchants were mainly Bombay men and Punjabis. The Gujaraties had Congress sympathies but their businesses ran their heads and their hearts never ran their heads.

There were few Sikh merchants, but Messrs. Gyan Singh of Singapore, a very big retail store of drapers, were involved in a case of underhand transaction in Japanese currency a few months before the Japanese hostilities.

5. AFTER THE OUTBREAK OF JAPANESE HOSTILITIES AND UP TO THE SURRENDER.

(i) Our setbacks and Japanese propaganda created wild rumours, the origins of which could not be traced, amongst the Indian community. These rumours took the form of the efficiency of Japanese rule, the absence of colour bar and perfect equality between all Asiatic races. This used to be the topic in hotels which were places of dissemination of news. The coolies were all talking of better wages and conditions under the Japanese. They were sure of a Japanese victory. Some even talked of glorious days ahead.

AS RELATIONS BETWEEN CHINESE AND INDIAN COOLIES WERE BY NO MEANS FRIENDLY, THE INDIANS HOPED THAT THE JAPANESE WOULD WIN; AS THIS WOULD RESULT IN HARM TO THE CHINESE.

(j) The Middle Class, whose mouthpiece was the Youth League (a radical body) did very good service to the community by running an efficient Indian Passive Defence League. This earned high praise even from the Chinese leaders. But there was fifth column activity amongst this class too.

(k) In general, the merchants were bewildered at our setbacks.

6. AFTER THE SURRENDER AND UP TO 7/5/42.

(l) At first most of the Indian community were contented. The apparent favoured treatment which the Japanese meted out to the Indians with studied cunning had its effect. Indians had fallen for the Japanese. But sentiment gave way to hard realities, for almost every Indian in Singapore was a wage earner. But the Japanese were not in a mood to provide jobs for everybody. This resulted in serious unemployment, for there was no other chance of getting a job except under the Japanese.

(m) The stock of food was dwindling, which resulted in the soaring of food prices. An unemployed man cannot afford to buy foodstuffs at three or four times the normal price. In other words, they were faced with the prospect of starvation which resulted in a growth of discontent.

(n) Even those who managed to get jobs under the Japanese were paid 30 to 40 per cent lower than previously, and the higher paid ones had cuts of as much as 60 per cent. But as the price of essential foodstuffs had risen to as much as 500 per cent in some cases, it was impossible to make both ends meet. The old recourse of borrowing could not be resorted to as no one had any money.

===== Page 173 =====
Appendix "F".

(o) The men in the municipal essential services all came back to work. The men in the non-essential services were retrenched to about 30 per cent. The non-technical staff in the P.W.D. (mainly Asiatic before) were not promised any jobs but were allowed to carry on; in return they were given about 100 lbs. of rice, 2 sardine tins, 3 tins of milk and 1 lb. of sugar, but no salary was given them. The hospital staff were retrenched. The departments of Fisheries and Monopolies were not functioning by the time of our departure. The police force was carrying on and there were a lot of new entrants, but the Chinese detectives had been thrown out. Under the British rule the G.P.O. employed 900, the Japanese, however, only needed 120.

(p) This discontentment was unconsciously fostered by the rude behaviour of the Japanese themselves, their arrogance, smacking of faces, pinching and screwing of noses and cheeks, and also by kicking. These punishments were not necessarily for any mistakes in work, but through difficulties caused by language difficulties.

(q) Some English writer said that Malaya is a land of clerks. This was true to a great extent. The clerks had a great deal to say about insufficient pay, bad treatment, and unpleasant conditions. Under the new dispensation the number of clerks has shrunk to a quarter of their original number, their salary is less and they cannot air their grievances. It is a sad plight but the growth of discontent is there and momentum is being added to it.

(r) It is the same with the property owners, the business people and the Chetties or money lenders. The property owners have to pay rates and taxes, but the rents are reduced and few people can afford to rent houses. The business people have to pay extra taxes such as war tax, business registration tax, advertisement tax, etc. and yet sell at the old controlled prices. They never do it but if caught they know it is their end. The chetties have to pay the same taxes as the merchants and in addition have to register as money lenders and show monthly returns. This section which has always been loyal is the most discontented.

(s) Indian women must also be reckoned with. Often the woman in the house is loyal even though her husband may be Congress-minded. At all times they are shy and stay-at-home people. Now they are afraid to go out as well as afraid to stay at home alone. Their children cannot go to school as they cannot get conveyances and in many cases their husbands are unemployed. They are without medicine and food is hard to obtain. They are the most unhappy of all.

(t) The tradesmen, mostly silversmiths and goldsmiths, have no business at all. The blacksmiths are idle. The small man with a small holding is helpless. The milkmen have had most of their cows slaughtered by the Japanese. The eating and coffee shop proprietors can make a really good profit now but they are sorely disappointed as they cannot get the foodstuff or the money to buy it. The tailors have no business as their stock of clothes has mostly been looted. Even the flower seller complains.

D. Malays.

7. BEFORE HOSTILITIES.

(a) The Malay is a pleasure loving man and he was in the backyard in the struggle for living against an industrious Chinese or a working Indian. Perhaps that was his grouse. The Government had a pro-Malay policy which though admirable had one drawback in that the Malays became pampered and expected rights as a matter of course.

(b) The Japanese were very cordial with the Malays, many keeping Malay women. The Japanese could speak Malay well and knew the Malays better than any other community there, including the average British Government officer. The Japanese could get to the heart of the Asiatics by sitting down and talking with them on terms of perfect equality, with a studied cunning. It would have been a miracle if any European had condescended to do that.

===== Page 174 =====
Appendix "F".

(c) There was much fifth column work in favour of the Japanese amongst the Malayan community. They had nothing against the Japanese and in all Japanese retail shops in Singapore the salesmen were Malays or Indians. Their syces were also Malays.

(d) Relations between the Malay rulers and the Japanese were cordial. The Sultan of Johore had given concessions to the Japanese in his state to work the iron ore mines and the Japanese owned many rubber estates in the states. Many Japanese had become Mohamedans and this flattered the Malays a good deal.

(e) The Malays as a seafaring race were able to give the Japanese a great deal of information about the east coast of Malaya particularly.

8. AFTER THE OUTBREAK OF HOSTILITIES.

(f) There were wild rumours of the Malay Regiment deserting to the Japanese at Kota Bharu. This proved to have been a rumour. Great activity existed amongst the Malay secret societies who were under close observation by the police. There were even rumours of Malays in the rural areas outside the municipal limits of Singapore hiding some Japanese. A few days before the outbreak of hostilities many Japanese shopkeepers sold their goods to the Chinese through Malay intermediaries. Malays and Malay organisations were spreading rumours about a Japanese victory and liberation of Malaya.

9. AFTER THE SURRENDER.

(g) Singapore town has not a large Malay population. In fact they are in a minority and not pure Malays. When the Japanese entered the town many Malays came with them some of whom wore the same uniform as the Japanese soldiers. I myself saw two Malays dressed in this way.

(h) The Malays were allowed a large measure of freedom. They even went so far as not paying their hotel bills, or for goods they bought, and the shopkeepers were nervous of reporting them to the Japanese authorities. However, in two cases which were brought to the notice of the Japanese they were immediately belaboured. All this happened in the first few weeks following the surrender.

(i) About the first week of April, I heard from one Kadir, a Malay who is a notorious bad hat and is rumoured to be a President of a Malay secret society, that the Japanese had rounded up all the Malay secret societies and beheaded those members they could get hold of and even some of the Malay fifth columnists who had helped them. Kadir added that he himself was nervous of his safety and wanted to go up country.

(j) It is reliably reported that the Sultan of Perak rebuked a Japanese officer (who held a position similar to that of British Resident) for not getting up in his presence. The latter drew his sword and threatened to put another Sultan on the throne.

(k) At a meeting of the nine rulers of the states which was held at the Adelphi Hotel, the Sultan of Johore was reported to have stated that there was Japanese blood in the veins of some of the Malay Sultans. This was reported in the Japanese daily newspaper Syonan Times.

(l) Japanese soldiers were reported to have forced Malays to slaughter pigs in the precincts of the Kuala Kangsar mosque.

E. Eurasians.

10. BEFORE HOSTILITIES.

(a) The Eurasians are equivalent to the Anglo-Indians of India. They are mainly half-breeds of Portuguese mixture, some Ceylon Burghers, some Anglo-Burmans, Anglo-Siamese, Dutch-Javanese and Anglo-Indians. The fairer of the Syrian christians from Malabar used to pass off as Anglo-Indians.

===== Page 175 =====
Appendix "F".

(b) Some Anglo-Indian or Eurasian girls were employed by the Japanese as steno-typists, telephone operators, and as sales girls.

(c) The Eurasian community as a whole remained loyal. Whilst their standard of living was higher than that of any Asiatic community, it is doubtful whether their earnings warranted that high standard. The result was debts, and sometimes "gentel prostitution". It was quite easy for a Eurasian girl with Japanese leanings to get information by various methods, as a prostitute, dance hostess, or cabaret girl.

(d) Almost all the Eurasians joined the Volunteers and some were particularly conscious of their duties. Others, however, joined merely as a matter of form.

11. AFTER THE SURRENDER.

(e) The Eurasian community has suffered much at the hands of the Japanese. This applies particularly to Eurasian teachers. The Japanese must have had a secret 'black list' and those men who were known to have been pro-British were not employed. I have met teachers who have taken it with a philosophical attitude and with a full hope of our return. Others are pessimistic. The rank and file of Government teachers are not yet corrupted.

(f) No Volunteers can get jobs and none of the M.A.S. or A.R.P. (mobile canteen) women are employed. The Eurasian nurses have a tough time with the Japanese soldier patients in the hospitals.

(g) There was an advertisement in the Syonan Times asking for Eurasian girls as salesgirls in Whiteaways which the Japanese proposed to run as a clothing and general stores for their officers. When some of the girls went for an interview they were interrogated in a silly manner and asked to go to the bathrooms where to their horror they were told to undress for Japanese officers who were already there. Those who refused did not get a job.

(h) Immediately after the surrender, the Japanese ordered the Eurasians to march to the Padang (Eurasian recreation grounds in front of the Municipal Building) where, with the aid of loudspeakers, etc., they were told not to be pro-Ally on pain of severe punishment and were asked to take the oath of allegiance to the Japanese Emperor.

F. JEWS.

(a) There are not many Jews in Singapore, but there are some among these few who are very rich. Meyer is a very rich real property owner. Other wealthy Jews are Frankel, David, Mannesh, Julian Israel, etc. The rest are petty or itinerant merchants, peddlars and commission agents, whilst some held subordinate positions in the local Government.

(b) Since the visit of the German General with his Italian satellites in April 1942, who inspected P.O.W., booty and the battlefield, they are very anxious about their future. They are very dissatisfied with the present situation and pray for victory. They have a definite stake in the country. Anti-Semitism was not in evidence at the time, May 1942, but there was a case of an Italian doctor inoculating at the Doh Jin clinic in Middle Road. He was asking light-skinned patients if they were Jews and on one man replying in the affirmative, he manhandled him brutally and kicked him out.

G. ARABS.

There are a few Arabs some of whom are very wealthy. Alsogoff and Alkaff are big real property owners and this confiscatory measure of the Japanese must be hitting them very hard. At the time of our departure they were trying to wangle their way out as neutral or non-belligerent nationals, but we do not know what success they met with in this direction. On the whole the Arabs are not impressed by the Japanese as they consider them their religious enemies. They are certain of an Allied victory.

An old Arab that I know said that in their religious books the Japanese are Satan who will disrupt things and rule for a few months!

===== Page 176 =====
H. SUMMARY.

(i) The Chinese are very hostile to the Japanese.

(ii) The Indian middle class is pro-Japanese, for the most part, with the poor class anti-Japanese, but even the middle class was shedding its apparent love for the Japanese in May 1942. The Japanese are doing their best to enlist Indians on their side, with a view to carrying on the local Government with some trustworthy men and to try to have a dig at us in British India. Other Indians are being used as a pawn in the game.

(iii) The Malays were in the main pro-Japanese on account of Japanese pampering - the Malays were prostituting their country for money. There was, however, growing revulsion in May 1942.

(iv) The Eurasians were anti-Japanese although some adopted a pro-Japanese attitude after the surrender.

(v) The Jews were passively anti-Japanese.

(vi) The Arabs are anti-Japanese mainly on account of their religion.

(vii) The rest, an insignificant lot, were not anti-British.

===== Page 177 =====
TREATMENT OF CIVILIANS (ATROCITIES ETC.), PERSONALLY WITNESSED BY LT. V. RADHARKRISHNAN UNLESS OTHERWISE STATED.

(Report by Lt. M.M. Pillai and Lt. V. Radharkrishnan who escaped 7/5/42).

A. CHINESE.

1. Mass executions.

After the fall of Singapore, numbers of Chinese were rounded up, forced to dig their own graves, and then compelled to kneel in them. The Japanese officer present beheaded the first man in the line, after which the sword was wiped with a handkerchief and with the tip of his tongue he would lick the blood. A subaltern carried on beheading the rest of the victims. A second line of victims were then compelled to bury the first lot and prepare their own graves. These executions lasted for a period of about ten days.

2. Mass prostitutions.

The worst misfortune that befell the Chinese community happened during the registration of this community. Girls between the ages of 13 and 25 simply did not return to their homes. When this matter was reported to the Japanese, no satisfactory replies were obtained. The girls were eventually located living in houses in the former residential areas. There were at least 9,000 of them being given free food and lodging. The Japanese soldiers were allowed to visit these girls at the rate of half a dollar a visit.

3. Ill treatment of Chinese nurses and Medical Auxiliary Service personnel.

There were many reliable reports of brutalities being committed on the hospital personnel by the Japanese patients.

4. General.

The Chinese suffered from unprovoked assaults by stray Japanese soldiers, their homes were pillaged, their women raped, their businesses looted, and there were many cases of torturing, especially at the Y.M.C.A. School of Commerce which was the centre of these practices. These tortures took the form of burning oil being thrown on the victim who was then flayed alive.

B. INDIANS.

1. In comparison to the Chinese, the Indian community suffered very little, although there were individual cases of beheading, rape, looting and assaults.

C. MALAYS.

Even with this section of the population there were cases of rape and maltreatment. Many prominent Malays received particularly brutal treatment on account of their pro-British feelings.

D. EURASIANS.

No atrocities were heard of beyond a few cases of individual ill-treatment, manhandling, and rape.

===== Page 178 =====
With the Compliments of the British Consulate General, Kunming, China,

Under Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs 31 MAR 1943

P.L. 228A of 4.3.43 from Embassy Chungking to Foreign Office.

Sir,

With reference to Your Excellency's Circular Savingsgram to Consuls No. 1, I have the honour to enclose herewith copies of an interesting report on conditions in Malaya which has come to my notice.

2. My informant is Colonel Leong Yew Koh, who was the subject of my predecessor's despatch No. 120 of the 10th October 1942 and who has since then manifested frequent signs of wishing to be on good terms with this office and one of our Intelligence organisations, possibly in consequence of the somewhat delicate state of relations that exists between himself and the local authorities.

3. In the course of conversation he happened to mention that a Chinese who had escaped from Malaya had been staying with him and when I expressed a desire to meet this man and hear what he had to say Colonel Leong said he would try to arrange a meeting. He has now informed me that the man in question has left Kunming but has sent me a copy of some notes of their conversation which he had made at the time. The story is, unfortunately, some months old and may have reached the authorities concerned through some other source, but in case this has not happened and in any case for its general interest and propaganda value it may be thought worth having on record.

4. I should add that, as stated in my predecessor's despatch under reference, Colonel Leong is of dual nationality and while he has served for some years as a Chinese official in different capacities he was born in Malaya, considers himself a good British subject and has recently offered his services to the Colonial Office in any capacity and still owns some property in Malaya.

I have, etc.,

Sir Horace J. Seymour, K.C.M.G., Consul General.

His Majesty's Ambassador to China, British Embassy, Chungking, China.

===== Page 180 =====
The following miscellaneous information has been gathered by the writer from a man who left Malaya about two months after the fall of Singapore. It gives a glimpse of the then existing conditions.

MILITARY

The Japanese soldiers arriving in Malaya were badly armed with antiquated rifles and poorly clad. Their physique, however, was generally strong and healthy. The rifles with which the Malayan police were armed were all seized by the Japanese and replaced by shotguns confiscated from the people.

The main bodies of the Japanese soldiers were stationed in large towns along the railway, e.g., in Taiping at the Christian Brothers' School buildings near the railway station with H.Q. at the Fukien Association Hall; in Ipoh in the Chinese Town Hall with H.Q. at the Hongkong Shanghai Bank Building near the railway station.

When the Japanese arrived, they were very well informed of everything in Malaya. For instance, they possessed full records of all car owners and within 24 hours they were able to lay hold of every serviceable motor vehicle. They had complete lists of all individuals formerly engaged in anti-Japanese activities. They could lay hold of all available stocks of rubber without difficulty.

The war prisoners were kept at Ipoh, Taiping, and Butterworth opposite Penang. They were obliged to do hard labour such as carting rubber from stores to factory and sweeping the streets, etc. European and Eurasian civilians of both sexes were all interned and seemed to be very inadequately fed.

During hostilities, the most severely bombed place was Penang. Altogether about 600 buildings were completely destroyed, mostly along Beach Street and Campbell Street. Most of the people in the bombed area did not evacuate, so there were very heavy casualties. One estimate gave about 8,000 people killed.

European and Chinese guerillas were quite active. Trains were frequently derailed and numerous Japanese soldiers killed. The guerillas seemed to be well supplied with ammunition.

POLITICAL AND SOCIAL

All Chinese were being registered and were forbidden to move from place to place without written permission from the military authorities. All towns and villages within a distance of a mile from the roads were being visited by small detachments of Japanese soldiers and Peace Maintenance Committees appointed for them. Descent is suddenly made upon a village or town very early in the morning. All Chinese old and young, male and female, educated and uneducated, rich and poor, all alike are summoned to assemble in an open space and made to squat for two days in the blazing sun and a night without food or shelter. None are allowed to leave or stand up. Names, addresses, and other particulars are leisurely taken down. Some men who have been reported against for anti-Japanese activities in the past are removed to be summarily bayoneted to death in the Malay Mosque nearby. Most of the men before being allowed to leave are given a severe beating without reason or cause simply to satisfy their sadistic lusts. A careful and minute search is then made of every house. All valuables are forcibly taken.

===== Page 181 =====
Away. If, unfortunately, a British or Chinese flag, a Sun Yat Sen, Lin Sen, or Chiang Kai Shek photograph is found, the male members of the house are forthwith marched off to be summarily murdered.

At first, the Malay inclined to hope for great things from the Japanese. Influenced by intense Japanese propaganda, they thought they would get better benefits under Japanese rule than under British. But they were sadly disappointed. The Japanese ruthlessly outraged their keen religious susceptibilities by turning their Mohamedan Mosques into execution grounds. The water pools appurtenant to every Mosque for religious ablution were made into swimming baths for the soldiers and abattoirs for slaughtering pigs. Chinese temples and joss-houses were, however, scrupulously respected. The Japanese soldiers worshipped superstitiously every form of Chinese joss.

During the British administration, all the various communities lived together in great harmony. The Japanese were now trying to divide and rule. The Malays were being encouraged to rob the Chinese in outlying districts. Thus, in Pangkor Island and in Lumut, all Chinese residents were ordered to leave the place suddenly. They were not allowed to take away any belongings besides the clothes they wore on their person. After two months, they were allowed to return, but then all their moveable possessions had been stolen by the Japanese, and what the Japanese had left were removed by the Malays.

Malays, Indians, and Chinese received different treatment from the Japanese. Thus, Malays were charged $1 a person, Indians $2, and Chinese $3 for crossing the Johore Causeway which links the Island of Singapore with the mainland. The Causeway was partially destroyed during hostilities but had since been repaired.

The three communities had each their own Peace Maintenance Committees appointed for them. These committees were responsible to the Japanese for the peace and good behaviour of their respective communities and for the collection of the exactions imposed upon the people. The members of such committees generally made use of their standing with the Japanese to squeeze the people. At the same time, they themselves were often subjected to indignities, insults, and outrages from their masters, the Japanese officers. In Ipoh, there was the case of Lau Ek Cheng, a Justice of the Peace under the British administration. He turned Quisling and went to welcome the Japanese soldiers to Ipoh. He was accordingly appointed a member of the Peace Maintenance Committee. One day, a Japanese officer visited him in his house. He took a fancy to his daughter. Forthwith, she was forcibly removed from his house. No one dared to protest.

No Nanking agent was allowed to engage in any activity in Malaya. The only concession to Nanking was that the photograph of Wang Ching Wei was allowed to be hung up by the people.

All the subordinate Chinese, Indian, and Malay government servants were ordered to return to their posts. So were all the officers and men in the police force except those of European descent. The Courts were open, presided over by Malay judges and magistrates. The language used in the Courts and government offices was still English. The Japanese had, however, conscripted about 2,000 young Chinese and sent them to Singapore to learn Japanese. They intimated that when these students returned, the official language would be Japanese.

===== Page 182 =====
All the land registers, it seemed, had been destroyed.

The sultans, Malay chiefs, and most of the Indians were cooperating closely with the Japanese. Rajah Omar, a former magistrate, was presiding over the Supreme Court at Ipoh. The following Chinese were also cooperating with the Japanese by serving as members of the Peace Maintenance Committees: Dr. Lim Boon Keng and Dr. Loh Seng Tak in Singapore; Lim Cheng Ean, a barrister, in Penang; Chang Kye Choo, a barrister, Fung Seong, a wealthy miner, and Lau Ek Cheng, a former Justice of the Peace, in Ipoh; Lam Weng Yun, the son of the Chinese millionaire Lam Looking, was Chief of Police for Perak.

ECONOMIC

All mines and rubber plantations had closed down. The Japanese were, however, forcing the Indian labourers to tap the European plantations by paying them 5 cents a catty. Rubber was not being sent out of the country either for want of transport or for fear of British and American submarines, which were known to be very active even near Penang waters. Rubber was being used for the extraction of gasoline. There were numerous small plants all over the country for this purpose.

None of the banks were functioning.

There was a great shortage of food. People in agricultural districts like Sitiawan were growing hill paddy and potatoes. But in the mining districts and urban areas, the people were suffering great hardship. For want of suitable land, they were unable to plant any food-stuff. Food from agricultural areas was unavailable for lack of transport. For example, at Kedah, there was plenty of cheap rice, the price being only about 5 per picul, but such rice could not be sent out of Kedah, there being no transport available. However, rice was not expensive when obtainable. Thus, in Singapore, rice was sold at 80 per picul whenever a rare shipment arrived. But the majority of the people were unable to get their requirements adequately met. Conditions were so bad that suicides were frequent.

There was also a scarcity of medical supplies; whatever stocks in the country had been seized by the Japanese. The doctors were therefore deprived of the means of practice. All lawyers were also unemployed.

The Perak Hydro-electric Plant at Chenderoh near Kuala Kangsar had not been dammed and was still functioning. The British Government refrained from destroying it to avoid flooding the villages and towns in the plain and causing great suffering to the people. The Parit pontoon bridge had been destroyed and so was the highway bridge at Kuala Kangsar, but the railway bridge had been repaired. Similarly, all the bridges from Kedah to Singapore were repaired. Trains were run only for the movement of troops and military supplies.

EXACTIONS IMPOSED BY THE MILITARY

Owners of land were forced to make a contribution of 45 Straits Currency for every acre they owned; owners of buildings and movable property, 45% of their valuation.

Gold, whether in the form of jewellery or bullion, tin, rubber, metal, gasoline, medicine, and motor vehicles [POSSIBLY MISSING END OF SENTENCE].

===== Page 183 =====
Were confiscated outright; currency notes were also seized, sometimes military bills were given in return but more often they were not. There was no escape as a thorough house-to-house search was made. All shops had been plundered clean of their goods by the Japanese soldiers on their first arrival.

In making the above-mentioned exactions, the Japanese were greatly helped by the Chinese, Indian, and Malay Quislings who also availed themselves of the opportunity of enriching themselves thereby. Thus, ¥8,500,000 was demanded from Perak, but a much greater sum was collected by the Quislings. The Japanese got ¥10,000,000 each from Singapore and Selangor; ¥7,000,000 from Penang, etc. Altogether, ¥50,000,000 Straits Currency of cash exactions reached the hands of the Japanese from the Federated Malay States and the Straits Settlements.

Unnumbered Military Bills were issued by the Japanese military authorities. Even the commanders of regiments were at liberty to issue such bills without limit. People were forced to give up their Straits currency notes in exchange for these military bills. The Japanese military seemed anxious to get hold of any foreign currency, thereby unconsciously betraying a lack of faith in their own ultimate success.

Malays were ordered to supply paddy to the troops as well as rubber at the rate of one third of their assessed annual yield of either.

Leong Yew Koh.

26. 2. 43.

===== Page 184 =====
10.32 SINGAPORE ECONOMIC RECONSTRUCTION
Shonan: With the beginning this month of the second year of reconstruction under the Japanese military administration, the economic structure of Shonan is being rapidly rehabilitated in light of experience gained in the first year. A noteworthy effort recently made in the economic field is the fostering of interchange of goods between the various sections of the southern regions.

On 15th March, Shonan military administration enforced control regulations by which the exchange remittance system, which hitherto was restricted to exchange with Japan only, has been extended to cover remittances to and from Malaya, Sumatra, Burma, Java, and North Borneo. The extension of the system to these sections will undoubtedly contribute greatly towards the promotion of interchange of goods in the southern regions.

Moreover, because all exchange transactions were placed on a permit basis under the military administration and as all exchange business has been concentrated in the hands of Japanese banks, with the Southern Regions Development Bank as the central organ, and the values of the Malay dollar, the Sumatra and Java guilder, and the Burmese rupee have been fixed at parity, there will be no room for currency speculation hereafter.

The local military administration also sponsored the establishment on 20th March of the Southern Regions Shipping Company composed of all the Japanese shipping interests in Shonan. The company will mobilize steamships and sailing vessels for the transport of goods between the various sections of the southern regions.

===== Page 185 =====
Year. They have two things: superb equipment and well-trained leaders, but they lack fighting experience. The story of the Eighth Army is evidence of how far we still have to go before the end is in sight.
