Extract from: FAR EASTERN WEEKLY INTELLIGENCE SUMMARY.

No.13 for week ending 26.3.43.

MALAYA

Broadcasts:

1. A Domei broadcast states that the manufacture of rope from coconut fibre is Malaya's latest industry. Formerly household requirements such as scrubbing brushes and brooms were the only products made from coconut husks. With the development of the new industry, a number of small factories, employing thousands of Chinese girls, have sprung up on the island of Singapore where coconut husk ropes of varying lengths are manufactured. These are sold on the market at prices well below those charged for imported hemp.

Information from other sources:

2. (a) A shortage of cement in Singapore is reported, and it is proposed to import from Siam.

(b) All the ingredients for the manufacture of cement are available in Malaya, but pre-war plans to start manufacture there were always defeated by the cheap supplies available from India and Indo-China. It is quite likely that the Japanese will install a factory though there is no evidence of any preparations to that end.

Meanwhile, supplies of cement in the Southern Areas may not meet the demand. Pre-war annual output was approximately:

- Burma 60,000 tons.
- Siam 100,000 tons.
- Indo-China 260,000 tons.
- Hongkong 70,000 tons.
- Malaya nil.

There were surpluses of 140,000 tons in Indo-China and of 30,000 tons in Hongkong, but, in addition to absorbing these, the neighbouring countries imported from India, Japan and Europe.

The Burma factory was scorched, but apparently none of the others

===== Page 187 =====
Others. Plans have been made to increase the Indo-China production by 100,000 tons.

===== Page 188 =====
09.33 MALAYA, SUMATRA RICE CULTIVATION PROGRESSING
Shonan: The programme of the Japanese military administration here to augment production of rice in Malaya and Sumatra is making excellent progress. The planting of imported high-grade rice seeds from Japan, Taiwan, and D'jawa is virtually completed. Seeds showing the best prospects will be selected for large-scale cultivation. By way of encouraging the two-crops-a-year plan, the foodstuff production section of the military administration imported the foregoing seeds. In Kedah province in Malaya, where planting took place before the other areas, one crop has already been harvested with good results. It is expected that the crop will be taken in other provinces in July. The planting of Horai rice seeds from Taiwan was recently completed in Malaya as well as in the areas of Acheh and Banca Billiton provinces and three other Eastern provinces of Sumatra.

10.06 SINGAPORE TO HAVE MEDICAL COLLEGE
Shonan: The Japanese military administration here has decided to establish a medical college for the benefit of the local inhabitants in the latter part of April. The building of the former Tantokusen Hospital, in the city, will be used to house the proposed college, which will also have dental and pharmaceutical departments. The course of study will occupy four or five years, and graduates will be attached to hospitals in Malaya and Sumatra for two years, after which they will be licensed to practise.

SECRET.

3. Japanese occupied territories.

* From the "Schweizerische Handelszeitung" of 18.6.42 (CEYLON NO.0418/42). * Translated from German.

Petrol from Rubber: "The newspaper "Nikkankogyo" from Singapore announces that the discovery of a method of producing petrol from rubber is soon to be industrially exploited. A comparatively easy method has been found which saves the construction of new plants."

Ship building: The "Nikkankogyo" reports the taking over of dockyards in the Southern region by Japanese ship-building concerns has been largely settled. Arrangements have been made for the yards of Singapore, Hongkong, Cholon, Soerabaja, and Batavia to be taken over and run by Japanese concerns. The Hakodate Shipping Yard has taken over the yards at Manila, while a sister shipping concern is to work the yards at Rangoon."

===== Page 191 =====
I lunched with M. Paul Boncour yesterday at the French Embassy on the South Bank ("La Bastille"). Present also were M. Saintes, former French Vice-Consul at Singapore, who left Singapore last July; M. Tulasnes, former French Consul at Keijo (Seoul), Korea, who was caught at Manila by the Japanese attack on it and stayed there for some months after it fell; M. Beaulieu, of the French China Consular Service and Captain Guillermas, French Assistant Military Attaché, who has just come from Indo-China. Also present was a large consignment of liquors just arrived from Indo-China!

I attach records of my conversations with MM. Paul Boncour and Sainte. The atmosphere of this luncheon party was extremely friendly and pro-Allied sentiments were expressed by all present. On the other hand, they all considered that they were doing their duty to France by staying in the service of the Vichy Government and endeavouring to keep the French Far Eastern Empire and interests as intact as possible so that when peace came it could be said that they still existed and possible claims by other nations for some or all of them could be effectively opposed.

These people seem anxious to make contact with us. Personally, I think we should encourage this as all of them are willing to talk freely and can doubtless give us useful information about areas in which we are interested, vide record of conversation with M. Sainte attached. I am willing to act as a go-between if this is agreed to. M. Sainte, in any case, is lunching with me on Wednesday in order that G.O.O. and Squadron Leader and Mrs. Baird, who know Singapore, may have an opportunity of questioning him.

I suggest that this minute be circulated to G.O.O., Mr. Watts, Mr. Canssel, Mr. Blewett, Mr. Crawford, and Mr. Sedgwick, all of whom may have an interest in contacting these people.

8th March, 1943.

===== Page 192 =====
M. Paul Boncour told me yesterday that the Waichiaopu (K.G. Wu) had agreed to his staying on in Chungking despite the departure of the Chinese Embassy from Vichy. He considered that the Vichy Government had handled this matter very cleverly. After the German occupation of unoccupied France, all the diplomatic representatives of countries at war with the Axis had been taken to Germany with the exception of the Chinese, who had been given specially good treatment in Vichy pending a decision as to their future and had then been allowed to leave for Portugal. This he attributed in part to the support of certain German influences who wished to keep in with China. In any case, the Chinese Government were so pleased with the way in which the matter had been handled that they had agreed to his staying on in Chungking.

M. Paul Boncour added that Mr. Vincent of the American Embassy had assured him that if he wished to leave for North Africa there would be no difficulty about granting him an American visa. He felt it his duty to France, however, to stay on here as long as possible to protect Indo-China interests. There was still a danger, though, that the Japanese who disapproved of his activities in Chungking might bring pressure to bear on the Vichy Government to have him recalled, and if they succeeded, he would then accept the offer of the United States Embassy to get him to North Africa. He expressed much pleasure that the French Ambassador was unable to communicate with him, thus giving him a free hand to act as he thought best here.

In the course of general conversation with members of the lunch party, it became clear that those who came from Indo-China were glad to be out and enjoyed the freedom of the atmosphere in Chungking. This, according to M. Paul Boncour, was because in Indo-China they suffered not only from the Japanese occupation but also from the authoritarian police regime which Admiral Decoux was compelled to impose on the country. He confirmed that the Japanese exerted no control on the Indo-China frontier but said that the French control was very strict. The number of Japanese troops in Indo-China was estimated at 20,000. They keep very much to themselves, apparently, and in Nanjí at least, are not seen at all in the town. With the exception of two or three ships, the Japanese have taken over the whole of the French merchant marine and there had been heavy losses from allied submarine attacks. The Japanese had wished these ships to continue to fly the French flag and to induce French crews to serve in them under Japanese officers but both these requests had been refused.

I was also told that Indo-China is now producing locally many materials previously imported. For instance, although rubber grew in Indo-China, there was no means of manufacturing it there. Motor cars were now running on types manufactured from some form of local gum. More information on this subject could doubtless be obtained.

Referring to Europe, M. Boncour said he was certain that Soviet influence would be very strong everywhere after the war, also in France. If he wished to be an opportunist, he would go to Moscow now and assure himself of a good position in France after the war!

With the Russian victories, he saw increasing signs of anti-Russian reactions in Chungking Government circles, though he was certain that communistic ideas were growing outside these circles, especially amongst the students. He was certain that there were as many Russians as ever in Dinkiang and that no Chinese troops had as yet crossed the border.

===== Page 193 =====
The Chinese Government attributed this to Russian influence and presented it. By coincidence, Mr. Han Ming of the Central News, whom I met yesterday evening, told me proudly that he had just completed a private memorandum on the Russian menace to China. I tried to discourage him from such activities against allies.

8th March, 1943.

===== Page 194 =====
M. Sainte, former French Consul General at Singapore, who left the port in July last year and is now working in the French Embassy here, expressed his willingness yesterday to give us all the information he could about Singapore. He gave the following information in reply to my questions.

1. The discipline of the Japanese troops in Singapore has been excellent. There had been no maltreatment of white prisoners, either civil or military. The latter had been compelled to work at the beginning of the occupation, but at the time he left were not compelled to do so. All prisoners were interned outside the town in different places, and the women and children had been interned separately. Food was short for everyone, but the prisoners were as well fed as possible. Some, but not many, prisoners had been moved to Formosa.

2. Of the military prisoners, the Indian, Australian and New Zealand prisoners appeared to be given preferential treatment, as the Japanese believed there was a hope of making a separate peace with the countries, but none of the treatment was bad. He thought that about half the Indian prisoners captured had gone over to the Japanese. They were to be seen about the town armed.

3. The French in Singapore received worse treatment than the British prisoners, as the Japanese looked upon them as spies.

4. 80,000 troops in all had surrendered.

5. The Chinese population had been abominably treated. They had fought very well against the Japanese previously.

6. As far as he knew, the Governor, Sir Shenton Thomas and Lady Thomas were well when he left. The Japanese had offered them special accommodation but they had refused and had gone into the internment camp with the rest. M. Sainte paid a high tribute to the behaviour of Sir Shenton Thomas during and after the siege. He had had a nervous breakdown immediately afterwards.

===== Page 195 =====
7. At the time he left, the Japanese had not repaired the harbour effectively or the docks, although they claimed they had.

M. Sainte will be meeting G.O.C., Squadron Leader, and Mrs. Baird on Wednesday, and they will doubtless be able to elicit further information from him.

8th March, 1943.

DOMEI (JUG) IN ENGLISH FOR EUROPE 15.3.43

18.08 SUMATRA DESCRIBED (Domei Correspondent) Medan: In anticipation of the first anniversary of the institution of the military administration in Sumatra on 27th March, 1942, I made an extensive 40-day tour of the island, inspecting the progress effected during the past year.

On the western coast of the province, where the climate is pleasant, there are about 15,000 Chinese, more rice is produced than is needed for local consumption. While the tea and quinine are world-famous. Besides sulphur and fluorspar, 500,000 tons of bituminous coal have been mined annually, since exploitation started in 1891. The Japanese advance was so swift that the Dutch had not time to destroy the mining facilities. A recent estimate of the coal deposits exceeded 100,000,000 tons.

In Palembang province, with oil the chief industry, life shows greater vigor than in any other place in Sumatra. The oil wells, saved from Dutch destruction by the swift stroke of Japanese parachute troops, are today humming with activity. About 200 km south-west is Bukit Assam, a large coal mine believed to possess a reserve of 900,000,000 tons.

In the East Coast province, known as the "Estate District", Sarea is famous for huge rubber, palm oil, hemp and tea plantations. This province was a treasure vault for the former N.E.I. Government.

01.27 OVERSEAS EDUCATIONAL ASSOCIATION FORMED
Chungking: The Overseas Educational Association was inaugurated on Sunday at a meeting of Overseas Chinese educationalists and others interested in overseas education. Reports on pre-war and post-war overseas education in the Philippines, Hongkong, Macao, Burma, Malaya, Siam, and French Indochina were given by Chinese teachers who escaped from the Japanese. 17.45 MALAYAN TIN MINES, RUBBER PLANTATIONS RESTORED
Ipoh: Nearly all tin mines and rubber plantations damaged by the British during their retreat down the Malay Peninsula have now been restored to full working order. This is due to the extensive reconstruction work done by the enemy Property Bureau of Perak State during the first year of the Japanese administration. Important factories are now operating at full capacity, while most damaged buildings have been completely repaired and are already rented out.

4. SINGAPORE RECONSTRUCTION:
Singapore: The reconstruction of the railway connecting North and South Malaya over a distance of 1,200 km, which had been damaged during the war, has been almost completed. Consequently, the travelling time from Singapore to the Malayan frontier has been shortened as from 1st March by 6 hours and 40 minutes. Harbour installations, too, have been repaired and extended. A large number of wooden ships are to be launched shortly. Institutes for the re-education of Chinese officials and training schools for native sailors have been opened. The Faculty of Medicine at Shonan University will be opened in April. As to the development of communications in Singapore, the network of autostradas has been considerably extended. The number of taxicabs has increased and tram fares have been reduced. Progress has been made by local light industries. Soaps, towels and other items in everyday use have been produced locally and have appeared on the market.

8. JAPAN ALLOWS MONEY REMITTANCES TO BURMA, BORNEO:
Singapore: Remittances of money from Sumatra and Malaya to Japan were resumed last June. The military administration in Singapore has now decided to permit remittances between Malaya, Sumatra, Burma and North Borneo as from 15th March with a view to furthering the trade between the peoples of the occupied areas. This decision was publicly announced on the 6th inst.

2. TOKIO-SINGAPORE RAILWAY PROJECT: YAMAMOTO
Tokio: During a committee meeting in the Diet yesterday, the Deputy Minister for Greater East Asia, Yamamoto, declared that the Southern Affairs Department of the Ministry for Greater East Asia was examining the problem of the construction of the Greater East Asia railway line, to link Tokio with Shonan. The Committee for Technological and Practical Research was in charge. Stressing the necessity for the railway, Yamamoto stated that the most expedient line to link up the various local towns had already been decided by the committee and that negotiations with Indochina and Siam for materials were already in progress.

7 MALAYAN RECONSTRUCTION: SUNADA
Kuala Lumpur: The Adviser to the Military Administration in Malaya and Sumatra, Sunada, at present on a tour on inspection in Malaya, stated: "General life in Malaya is today vastly different from what it was like in April, 1942. Particularly in the central area, life is very busy. Everything has been rebuilt for permanent use. The efforts made by the local authorities and the population are exceptional, since various difficulties like languages and customs had to be overcome. The general opinion that the Malaysians had no courage must be considered wrong. If one offers the natives serious guidance, one may safely expect the re-establishment of a united national group in Malaya."

DOMEI (JAU2) IN ENGLISH FOR EUROPEAN ZONE 2-3-43

17-33 SINGAPORE: JAPANESE BUDGET PROVISIONS
Shonan: The Japanese Military Administration, since its establishment a year ago, has gradually extended temporary measures to different fields and today is about to take permanent measures. This is clear from its Budget for the current fiscal year. Last year the Budget was divided into several different terms, and appropriations were made according to rapidly changing conditions. This year the estimates cover the whole year. A fairly large amount is allotted for shipbuilding and salvaging sunken vessels, while a huge sum is appropriated for industrial purposes and increased food production. A considerable amount is set aside for engineering enterprises such as repairing harbour facilities and constructing roads. A feature unknown [POSSIBLY MISSING TEXT].

===== Page 202 =====
With the compliments of the Far East Division
Ministry of Information,
Malet Street, W.C.1.

===== Page 203 =====
During British rule in Malaya, a fairly large sum was appropriated for training natives as government officials, for the establishment of a medical college, and other welfare undertakings. The chief sources of income were tariffs levied on commodities sent to the Japanese mainland (sic), sales by the military administration of sugar, rice, salt, and other daily necessities, and profits on monopoly goods and the lottery. In July last year, lottery tickets to a value of 1,000,000 Straits Settlement dollars were issued. Tickets amounting to 3,000,000 Straits Settlement dollars will be on sale this year from 1st April; 60 per cent of the income from this source will be used for relief work and sanitation for the native inhabitants. Industrial development in Malaya and Sumatra last year was phenomenal. Communications were completely restored. To assist in increasing food production, the Chinese Association recently dispatched experts to various areas in Malaya who will help in reclaiming farmland and improving farming implements and methods.

===== Page 205 =====
COPY

A Report Made by Mr. Lai about the Condition of His Trip to China

1. This time the Japanese forces attacking Malaya were the "Kiang" and the "Lee" regiments numbering over one hundred thousand men. The infantry was the main force with armored units. The fighting morale of the Japanese soldiers was excellent. In the battle of "Pei-ma," the Japanese used bicycle units as vanguard and gained a considerable result. The "Kiang" regiment now stations at Singapore, while the "Lee" regiment stations at "Pei-ma." The Japanese quarter their forces mostly in the "Tung-lin" district, West Coastal Shores, and Alexander. The West Coast and city district were constructed with machine-gun pillboxes. After the repairing of the Naval Harbour of Singapore and the San-san + 新山 Bridge, the better equipped Japanese forces were transferred to Burma, and part of the sentries in the streets are now changed to Indian soldiers.

2. The political organization of Malaya remains originally the same. The middle and lower classes of clerks are mostly overseas Chinese retained by the Japanese, and natives, but personnel are still insufficient. According to the newspaper, about three hundred thousand political workers will be sent from Japan to replenish the shortage, but they have not yet arrived.

3. The important Japanese organizations in Malaya:

a. The Headquarters of the Commander-in-Chief - housed in the former British Governor's residence. The Commander-in-Chief is "San Hai Von Ven" (Yamashita Tomoyuki).

b. The Department of Military-Political Affairs - is situated at the upstairs of the Head Post Office at the First Suspending Bridge. The responsible person is "Pee-to" (Watanabe Wataru?).

c. The Headquarters of the Gendarmes - Its office is in the Y.M.C.A. Building at "Tung-lin." The responsible person is unknown.

d. The Municipal Department - The office is in the original British Municipal Building. The Mayor is "Dah Ta Mao Hsiung" (Odate Shigeo).

e. The Concentration Camp - The place is located at Chong Nien, the newly built prisons by the British. The responsible person is unknown.

===== Page 206 =====
f. The Hostel for the Prisoners of War - The place is in the World Amusement House. The person responsible is unknown.

The Overseas Chinese Association - The Head Office is in Singapore, at the Gno Loo Club Cha Lan Ting. The Chairman is Mr. Lin Ven Ching (not active); the Chairman of the Board of Directors is Lee Tien Pao (a native of Nai-an, Fukien, age over 50, owner of the Tien Sien Hotel at Amoy). Lee was formerly engaged in activities for the Japanese and was ordered "to arrest" by the Government. Last October, Lee resigned from his post as chairman of the Board of Directors, the post was succeeded by Mr. Hwang, but Lee is still engaged in secret activities. The fund of the Association is maintained by the former Commercial Guild and other social organizations.

h. The League of Independent Indians - The person responsible and the address of the office are unknown.

4. After the fall of Malaya Island, the British captives amount to 70,000 including over 40,000 Australians and 30,000 Indians; the remainder being a few Scotch artillery forces. Among the captives, the Indian soldiers were better treated (transferred to Burma), the Australian soldiers were worse treated. They are forced to hard labour, naked at the upper part of the body and wearing a yellow short. Every meal one is given a ball of rice and little salt. The overseas Chinese often moved by sympathy secretly supply them with food. Last October, most of the Australian captives were sent to Thailand to construct a railroad.

5. The Japanese political slogan in ruling Malaya is "Help the natives and Indians, suppress overseas Chinese." The overseas Chinese were treated badly. Places like Sing-sen, Fu-yun, and Hai Too Pa Hei in three districts saw many Chinese killed. The overseas Chinese made under arrest were over seventy thousand. The chief overseas Chinese Mr. Tseng Man Lin is in prison. The whole family of Mr. Chen Sen Long of Fu-yun city was killed. The city district of Singapore is better, but Japanese soldiers in the suburb committed rape and killing; almost no overseas Chinese girl can escape this fate. The relation of the Indians towards the Chinese is good, but the Malaysians have totally changed their attitude and occasionally set traps for the overseas Chinese and insult Chinese girls.

6. The Japanese banks in Singapore are Yokohama Specie Bank, Formosa Bank, and Hwa Nai Bank. The Yokohama Specie Bank is at its original address, the Formosa Bank has been moved to the Hong Kong & Shanghai Bank Building, and the Hwa Nai Bank has been moved to the Dutch Shino Kung Bank. Banks run by the Overseas Chinese are Ta Hu.

===== Page 207 =====
Ta Hua, Overseas Chinese and Sze-hai, Tong Ven, and Hsing Lee five banks. The business is very few, without foreign remittance and without cash. The Yokohama has lent five million cash to them for their current capital.

7. The price for a picul of rubber (100 catties) is 20 dollars and the rubber business is monopolized and purchased by the Japanese merchant under the name of the "Tou Moo Syndicate." Owing to the destruction of the machines in the Tin mine, the Japanese is not able to reopen the work, besides it is further incapacitated by the lack of materials. Commodities in the market are almost denuded and very few business. Each district is ordered to raise a contribution of Fifty million of Malayan currency to the Japanese. Under such oppression, even the opulent Chinese are tending to bankruptcy.

8. At present the currencies used in Malaya are of two kinds, Malayan currency and the military yen. The value is the same. The Japanese intend to collect the Malayan currency, but most of the overseas Chinese secretly hoard such currency.

9. Malaya forbids the entry or exit of people. Therefore, people have to smuggle out without the notice of the Japanese. At present the main communication lines apart from Sumatra, were blocked. The delivery of letters is stopped except in Java where people write in Japanese indicating the sending place is Po Wen.

10. The Culture and Education in Malaya:

About March last year, the Japanese ordered all schools to reopen. All the English schools and middle schools were closed down by the Japanese. The Japanese amalgamated 100 odd schools into 26 primary schools which are under the control and supervision of the Municipal authorities. The syllabus of studies comprise four subjects, namely general knowledge, Japanese language, arithmetic, and singing. All teachers were ordered to learn Japanese, and if they fail the examination to be held in March this year their qualification as a teacher will be struck off. The enrolment of students is confined to the street where the school is sited. There are very few students in each school. As regards to Newspaper Press, there is a Chinese newspaper, the "Chiu Nan Yuet Poa," with a daily issue of one sheet, the persons responsible for this are "Fu Wu-min" and "Hu Long-man." The former chief editor and editor of the Singapore Daily News, and an English newspaper, the Chiu Nan Shih, Poa, which issues > 星洲日报

得天阅

11. There were 50,000 Chinese labourers in the Singapore Aerodrome, and their wages were only 80 a day. The hatred of these labourers towards the Japanese was very high.

12. Yang Sing-woon, formerly outdoor reporter of the Nan-yang Shang Poe, a native of Chin-chow, Kwangtung, was a Japanese fifth columnist in Singapore and now received a medal of merit from the Japanese Emperor.

SECRET.

EXTRACT FROM FAR EASTERN WEEKLY INTELLIGENCE SUMMARY.

No. 9 for week ending 26.2.43.

MALAYA

Broadcasts:

1. (a) A Japanese broadcast states that rubber production will be uncontrolled, in order to maintain employment in the plantation areas; that surplus supplies will be converted into petrol; that a refinery at Kuala Lumpur is already in operation to produce 100 tons of petrol a month; and that oil refinery equipment in Japan will also be used for this purpose.

(b) Papers published in recent years in the German and Japanese technical press allow some idea to be gained of the possible importance of plans to make liquid fuel from rubber. By low-temperature carbonisation of rubber, it has been found possible in the laboratory to obtain a yield of about 35 percent of a tar which may be further resolved on distillation into three fractions corresponding roughly to motor spirit, Diesel oil, and fuel oil. The respective yields of these three fractions in terms of the original rubber are roughly 5%, 20%, and 10%. The motor spirit fraction has a high resin content and does not respond satisfactorily to any normal refining treatment; it also has a low octane number. The Diesel oil has poor cetane and Conradson values; some samples were found to attack metals. Only the fuel oil fraction, either alone or mixed with the Diesel oil, was found to have relatively satisfactory properties.

Normal Malayan production would be about half a million tons a year, which by this process would in its entirety yield 25,000 tons of petrol, 100,000 tons of Diesel oil, and 50,000 tons of fuel oil, which is some 20 times the production claimed. The mixed fuel oil obtained is of a comparatively poor quality, and substantial plant would be necessary to carry out the carbonisation. Presumably, the oil refinery equipment mentioned would be shipped from Japan to Malaya. The labour required for this programme would be not far short of half a million, so that the output per head would be fantastically small. It could hardly be started for the sake of the trifling oil output, but might be the simplest and cheapest method of finding employment for the plantation labourers, who are Tamil immigrants from India and have no farms to fall back upon. That is to say, if subsistence has to be provided for these people in any case, a small quantity of something useful to Japan may be of more value than a large quantity of something temporarily worthless. No doubt, stocks of ordinary rubber would first be stored to the maximum of potential use. Another possible use for the rubber plantations would be the production of lubricant from rubber seeds, which are available for some three months in the year, but the fact that most estates have cover crops around the trees would make collection very difficult.

===== Page 211 =====
Salvage and Reconstruction Work.

5. A Japanese Naval reporter has described a visit to Singapore. The King George VI drydock, he says, is now ready for service. The s.s. "Talthybius" (10,250 tons, passenger/cargo, formerly owned by Ocean Steamship Co.) salvaged in December, is under repair. The salvaging of a 5,000 ton floating dock was completed by Japanese Naval Units on 29th January, and plans are under way for these Units to begin the salvaging of a 15,000 ton floating dock which was sunk at the time of the evacuation. The reporter was impressed by the general activity of Singapore docks and shipyards, and added that the Naval fort has been transformed into a Japanese fortification and will be an impregnable fortress against enemy attack.

Far Eastern Weekly Intelligence Summary
No. 6 for week ending 5.2.43.

Press:

2. (a) In mid-October 1942, 3½ million acres of rubber plantations existed, 60% of the curing centres had been rebuilt, and 400,000 workers were employed. The Dutch Singapore Rubber Works and the Nanyang Plantation were working a 24-hour day. The Bata factory at Klang was being rebuilt. (Hamburg Wirtschaftsdienst 16.11.42).

(b) Kedah produced about half the Malayan crop. Malaya was one-third self-sufficient for rice. Normally, therefore, Kedah produced only one-sixth of Malaya's requirements, and it would be quite impossible to increase the production to the amount indicated in the time. It is possible that by "Malay requirements" is meant the actual native Malays who constitute less than half the population.

FAR EASTERN ECONOMIC NOTES.

SINGAPORE FLOATING DOCK SALVED:

Singapore: Surmounting indescribable hardships during the past months, Japanese Navy units succeeded on 29th January in refloating a 5,000-ton floating dock, blasted and sent to the bottom by the enemy just before the Japanese occupation. ===== Page 217 =====
7. Administration. This is in the hands of Japanese military officers aided by Japanese civilians. The British Post Office officials are working with such of the old personnel as is available, under Japanese supervision. A Japanese stamp of similar value has been overprinted on the old British stamps; postal rates remain unchanged. Telegrams and money orders to a maximum value of £50 may be sent, and telephone calls are permitted. All are censored under Japanese control. Postal facilities are available to N.E.I., Philippines, and Burma.

8. Finance and Banking. Japanese currency and coins were in circulation in north Malaya before the fall of Singapore. Coins have now largely disappeared and are retained as curios. Both Japanese notes (in Malayan denominations issued by the invaders) and British currency notes are used, the latter being honoured everywhere. It is rumoured, however, that the British notes will all be withdrawn before 8th December, the anniversary of the opening of the Japanese attack on Malaya. Yen notes are not generally honoured. Indian currency notes in the possession of banks and money changers before the fall were seized by the Japanese. Indian residents in the country were allowed to exchange local currency for Indian and have done so secretly on a large scale because they fear a fall in the exchange value of the former.

9. Land and Agriculture. Ownership of land prior to 8/12/41 is recognised but sales are prohibited, and no transaction effected after this date is regarded as valid. It is compulsory to work all cultivated paddy land.

10. Communications and Transport. Railways, except in the Kotabharu area, have been restored and the Singapore-Bangkok express runs daily. Tram services are also running.

===== Page 218 =====
Synthetic petrol from rubber and coconut oil is being used for trucks, while buses operate on charcoal gas. All private cars, lorries, buses and cycles have been confiscated by the authorities without compensation. Movement from one province to another is restricted and a pass valid for one month only and containing a photograph of the holder is issued by the authorities for the purpose. Up to March, freedom of movement even inside towns was restricted.

11. Industry. Electric and water supplies have been restored. The operation of "enemy" owned tin mines and rubber estates commenced on 1/9/42. Local owners of mines and estates are allowed to work them but the output is controlled and the Government is the sole purchaser. There is no restriction on the output of rubber.

12. Food Supplies. Since August, rice and other foodstuffs have been arriving from Siam and sugar from Java. Rice, sugar, salt and other foodstuffs are rationed but there is sufficient for ordinary needs. Fresh milk is scarce but tinned milk is provided for children only.

13. Treatment of local population. Both Malays and Indians are generally badly treated. Good clothes are removed from the wearer by Japanese troops. Rates of pay are much lower than they were under British rule.

14. Attitude of Indian Residents. Those who were formerly pro-Japanese have slowly come to dislike the invaders as a result of their uncivilized behaviour and inhuman atrocities. The general feeling is one of discontent, fear and inward hatred of the Japanese.

15. Naval, Military and Air. Penang is to be used as a naval base and the aerodrome there has been enlarged to six times its former size. The seaplane base is being developed.

CONDITIONS IN SINGAPORE.

According to information from a reliable source, conditions in Singapore are as follows:

(1) Mr. TAN Ka-kei was brought back from Java to Singapore and kept under supervision, but he was not forced to do anything and was treated well.

(2) After the occupation, Mr. CHUN Cheng, Mr. YEN Chien, and others were arrested and beaten mercilessly. Mr. LI Wei-nan is said to have received serious head injuries, while several more men died as a result of the beatings. At present, Mr. CHUN Cheng is head of the Financial Affairs Department of the Puppet Government, and Mr. YEN Chien deals with the contribution of funds.

(3) After overseas Chinese leaders had been beaten, they were forced to sign letters of repentance, after which they were compelled to organize the so-called "Overseas Chinese Association" which is supposed to maintain peace and order.

(4) The presses were all confiscated. Mr. FU Wu-men is acting as General Manager and Editor of the SHAO NAN PAO.

(5) It is said that because of the relations of Mr. FU Wu-men with Mr. CHEN Kung-shih, the latter was able to avoid a beating by the Japanese.

===== Page 221 =====
(6) After the occupation, Singapore was divided into 12 sections. Chinese were ordered to register. After the registration, about 30,000 young Chinese (males and females) were arrested. Since their arrest, nothing has been heard of them.

(7) The Overseas Chinese Bank was forced to reopen, but details are lacking.

(8) There were only between 500 and 600 Chinese who managed to escape from Malaya.

Külnische Zeitung. 28th September, 1942.

Malaya and Sumatra.

The Japanese have unified Malaya and Sumatra in one administrative unit which is practical from the economic point of view. Malaya's chief role is that of a base for military defence, and this must be carried out even if the views of the native populations have to be subordinated. Rubber production has already reached 60 percent of the pre-war figure, and the remaining 40 percent will soon be reached. Japan has no intention of reducing production. This applies also to tin. Manufacture of soap, paper, matches and textiles is contemplated, but difficult at present. New railways are planned which will run across Sumatra, northeasterly from Padang, reducing the distance for transport from Padang to Singapore from 2,000 to 400 km. The natives are of relatively small employable value, but the local Chinese, on the contrary, are very usable (Brauchbar). The natives now get paid in full each month, whereas the British used to retain half the monthly pay. The new People's Bank, which gives credit to the natives up to 300 yen at 5 percent for 2 years, is of great benefit. The Japanese are now paying out the deposits which amount to $37,000,000 Straits, of which only $20,000 Straits were found after the capitulation. The Japanese are paying at present to each depositor the equivalent of $30 Straits, and thereafter $25 Straits every half-year.

While Malaya is of relatively small interest to the Japanese apart from the ores in Trengganu, Sumatra is regarded by all the experts as a marvellous paradise which the Dutch had hardly begun to develop. It is thinly populated and can take a large number of settlers.

Frankfurter Zeitung. 3rd October, 1942.

Malacca (i.e. Malaya) under the flag of the Rising Sun.

The Japanese administration in the southern occupied territories is described. The Generals who carried out the invading operations are, generally speaking, at the head of it. The earlier political divisions are disregarded. Malaya and Sumatra are united under General Yamashita in "Shonan".

Burma is under General Iida; Java, western Borneo and some of the Islands under General Imamura, the Philippines under General Tanaka; the Japanese navy administers the Celebes, eastern Borneo and very many Islands, including Christmas Island and Amboina, as well as some places within the military domains, e.g. Penang. The Malay military administration was the first to be set up. There are eleven Districts in Malaya and ten in Sumatra. There is a Governor for each District. All those for Malaya have been appointed, and they include officers, civil officials and economic "personalities". In contrast to the British method, Malaya and Sumatra will have centralised administration. Malaya, and presumably Sumatra, are to remain under Japanese administration in contrast to the countries which, like Burma and the Philippines, are to be given a large degree of autonomy. The aim of the Japanese is to bring all the various Asiatic races to a common feeling of belonging to Greater East Asia. At the start the most difficult are the Chinese, but the Japanese in Malaya have made it clear that there is no room there for adherents of Chungking, and the great majority have gone over to Nanking and Japan. This is an important success, as the economic co-operation of the Chinese in Malaya is indispensable.

===== Page 223 =====
Newspapers. In "Shonanko" there are eight newspapers: two in Malay (one in Javanese script and the other in Latin script for Indians and Chinese), one Japanese, one Chinese, one English, and three Indian in various languages and scripts.

The Malays are very keen on learning Japanese, and there is a big demand for Japanese instruction for which it is difficult to find material.

As there is a shortage of Japanese officials, natives are being recruited to help. Recently a police school was started which is attended by young Malays, Chinese, and Indians. According to available reports, the British in Malaya are not being used much for cooperation, but in the Netherlands East Indies some of the Dutch engineers have been released to work.

Rice production: The Japanese expect to get three harvests instead of two a year.

Tin Dredgers: It is expected shortly to repair forty of the original seventy.

There is at least a year's supply of consumer goods such as textiles, soap, and matches from stocks left over. In Malaya, not much is being produced; Japan and China will have to become sources of supply. Naturally, many consumer goods are rationed. The railways are almost restored, but mechanical transport suffers from a lack of petrol.

Nachrichten für den Aussenhandel. 6th November, 1942.

The importance of the Southern Regions in the economy of Greater East Asia. The article resumes one by the economist Fujioka in the Far Eastern Review. The success of Japan's economic policy in the Southern Regions will, in the end, determine the outcome of the war with Great Britain and the United States of America.

The problems Japan has to solve are the exploitation of natural resources and that of transport. The work must be carried through more rapidly.

From the Southern Regions, Japan expects to import mineral oil, rubber, tin, copper, chrome, manganese, bauxite, iron ore, hemp, jute, cotton, and quinine as the principal products.

In building up the economy of the Southern Regions, the Japanese military authorities are avoiding the use of development companies like those in Manchuria and Northern and Central China, and are instead selecting trusted industrialists who will be given great freedom of initiative, with priority given to those who were working there before the war. The only new organisation for the South Seas is the South Seas Development Bank controlled by the Ministers of Finance and War. This bank, however, is not a bank of issue.

In view of currency and transfer difficulties, all remittances between Japan and the occupied Southern Regions are prohibited. Japan hopes for a workable currency policy and expects to avoid inflation.

The principal problem is shipping. Fujioka also emphasises the great difficulties connected with native cooperation. In his opinion, it would be inadvisable to try to introduce large administrative reforms as Japan will suffer a very great labour shortage in any case.

===== Page 224 =====
RUBBER ESTATES IN PERAK AGAIN IN PRODUCTION.

Ipoh: In Perak, 280 enemy-owned rubber estates, with an acreage of 320,000 acres, employing over 40,000 labourers, have been reopened by Shonan Gomukumiai (Shonan Rubber Association). Rubber sheets are being bought by Kumiai every month—beginning this month—from smallholders. Rubber stocks are now being collected for bulk shipment to Japan. (Domei, 17 December, 1942)

===== Page 225 =====
PROSPERITY IN MALACCA

Malacca: Governor Ken Tsurumi of Malacca revealed at a press conference that a total of 45,000 acres of land are now under food cultivation in Malacca. It is expected the paddy rice crop this year will be double that of last year. Malacca produces more vegetables than it needs and is now exporting surplus to other states. An agricultural training institute has been established for the benefit of farmers. Bauxite mines are successfully developed by Ishihara Industry Company, and output will be exported to Japan next month. At present, 3,000 persons are engaged in the fishing industry, and there is a monthly catch of 160,000 piculs. Fish is salted and exported to other places. Nine tobacco factories are manufacturing cigarettes and cigars from local leaves.

===== Page 226 =====
As well as those imported from Sumatra, the forestry industry, from a total acreage of 40,000, is now supplying timber needed for the reconstruction of bridges and houses. (Domei, 24 October, 1942).

CHINA NEWS

Issued Daily by
The London Office of the Chinese Ministry of Information
9, Bentinck St., London, W.1. Tel: WELbeck 3477

No 64. Thursday, November 26, 1942. Gratis.

SINGAPORE TODAY

Dark Page In Japanese History

Conditions in Singapore under Japanese military administration are so bad as to make it one of the darkest pages in Japanese history, according to a recent arrival in free China from the Straits Settlements. He tells a story of systematic plundering and exploitation, which have left the local population in a state of utter misery.

According to this report, British soldiers taken prisoner when the city fell have been subjected to many indignities. For food, they are allowed only three bowls of congee (a thin rice gruel) daily, on which they are forced to undergo hard labour stripped almost naked in the sweltering heat of the tropics.

Soldiers of the Japanese Army in Singapore have committed the excesses which have become all too familiar in occupied China, and women and young girls dare not venture out of doors in the city, even in broad daylight.

Chinese Buried Alive

The harshest treatment of all is noted out to the large overseas Chinese community, who form the bulk of Singapore's population. Enraged by their continued loyalty and patriotism to Britain and China, the Japanese have arrested and third-degree many of their leaders. Altogether over 20,000 Singapore Chinese have been imprisoned by the army of occupation, labelled as "anti-Japanese elements." There have been many cases of prisoners being buried alive, while others have not been heard of since their arrest. As in occupied China, the Japanese have been quick to introduce their "slave-education" system into Singapore's more than a hundred Chinese schools. These have been forced to revise their curriculum in accordance with the "New Order" principles, and textbooks in Japanese have compulsorily replaced those in Chinese and English.

All British and Chinese newspaper plants in Malaya have been taken over by the Japanese, and all Chinese papers ordered to suspend publication. The Japanese themselves publish a propaganda sheet called the "Shonan Daily." (Shonan - Light of the South - is the new Japanese name for Singapore).

Rubber Plantations Seized

In the economic field, officially-sponsored looting by the Japanese army is rife. All rubber plantations and tin and zinc workings, which before the war were 90% Chinese-owned, have been seized by the Japanese. Compulsory purchase at only half pre-war prices of all rubber stocks has also been instituted, throwing many workers out of their jobs. Besides seizing all banks in Singapore, the Japanese have forced on the city a hundred million military yen issue of "war bonds" in the past eight months. All Chinese and British banks have been closed down, and all banking activities have to be carried on under the control of the Yokohama Specie Bank.

As a result of the sending of large stocks of foodstuffs back to Japan, Singapore is feeling an acute shortage of essential commodities. Rice has risen to eight times its former price.

===== Page 228 =====
PLANNED ECONOMY FOR CHINA

Measures To Curb Wartime Price Rises

Strict rationing, based on identity cards already issued, and the banning of the sale of luxury products are among measures expected to be enforced in China soon to curb the upward trend of commodity prices, which has given rise to questions about China's economic stability both domestically and abroad. Pegging of all official salaries at their present level is also forecast.

The Chinese government has already during the past year done much to adjust the price and commodity situation, but results so far are not thought to have reached the level required. The economic problem was brought to the forefront of consideration by Generalissimo Chiang Kai-shek at the People's Political Council meeting at the end of last month, and an intensive drive to eliminate hoarding and profiteering by some sections of the population is to be instituted. The relation between supply of and demand for commodities is one of the principal subjects being discussed by the current session of the Kuomintang Central Executive Council.

Subsistence Level If Necessary.

Exact details of the contemplated new economic programme have not yet been published, but it is believed that a strictly planned economy based on wartime conditions will be enforced. The People's Political Council has already in general endorsed the Government's programme, which calls for the wholehearted support of the Chinese people in enforcing government administration of commodities and control of prices. The people will if necessary be reduced to subsistence level and money will become valueless for the purchase of extras and luxuries.

At the same time the Chinese Government is devoting much attention to increasing the supply of staple goods and the improvement of transport facilities to permit of a freer flow of commodities from producer to consumer. It is pointed out that rises in the price index in an agricultural country like China, which can be self-supporting in essential commodities, is not so dangerously significant as it would be in the industrial countries of the West. "Food supply," according to a Government statement on the subject, "is assured in China. The country's potential strength cannot be measured purely by price index; provided war supplies flow in continuously, Chinese resistance will last any length of time until an all-out offensive can be launched."

===== Page 229 =====
UNION OF SOUTH AFRICA CENSORSHIP.

MEMORANDUM RE: INTERNAL CONDITIONS IN JAPAN.

The well-informed Swiss Newspaper "Die Weltwoche" carries a first-hand account of internal conditions in Japan in its issue of June 12, 1942. The whole article well repays studying, particularly portions dealing with attempts to repair damage done in the conquered territories, the growing shortage of rice, and the various administrative changes.

"DIE WELTWOCHE" (Zuerich) June 12, 1942.

"Greater Japan after six months of War.

Japan has experienced her first serious setback through the defeat of her Navy near the Midway Islands, exactly six months after they started the war in the Far East by their sudden attack on Pearl Harbour. Only ten days ago Navy-day (May 27) was celebrated with great pomp in Japan's big cities. Houses in Tokio were beflagged, Naval bands played in public squares, the Press issued special editions showing the surprising rise of Japanese Sea-Power from 1872 to 1942, several Naval Expositions were held, and the Navy paraded the streets. For the first time since the war began, the population had an opportunity of seeing the successful Admirals when they paraded through the streets in cars decorated with flowers.

Was the presence of so many leading Naval Officers in the Capital and in the Naval Port of Yokosuka—far away from the Fleet in the Pacific—perhaps an influence on the unfortunate naval action in the Midway? As a rule, Japanese warriors, whether in the Navy or Army, do not get any leave during the War, so that some of them have been away from their homes for years. This is the reason why uniforms do not dominate the streets of Tokio as much as they do in the Capitals of other belligerent countries. One of the Posters for the Naval Festival simply showed a reproduction of the service itinerary (Dienstplan) of Navy and Army for the last few years, which showed that not one Unit had been relieved during this time; since, moreover, warriors on sick-leave or those lightly wounded have to travel in transports specially reserved for them, the armed forces of Japan have hardly any contact whatsoever with civil population. Relatives have been separated for a long time and they know that, for a long time to come, they cannot see each other. Everyone who joins up for war service takes leave from his family as one who goes into his death. If he returns this is considered a special Blessing.

There is hardly any exchange of mail between the country and the fighting forces. Only the newspapers carry records about events in the big Southern Empire which Japan has conquered in so short a time. This "Southern Empire" (Suedreich) is considered to be the latest "Cell" in the Organism of the greater Japanese Empire, which Century after Century, Decade after Decade, and now Year after Year has added to the Japanese Empire.

Yamato near Nara Kyoto was the original cell of this nucleus; then came the grip on foreign islands, Formosa, the Marian Islands, [POSSIBLY MISSING PARTS]

===== Page 230 =====
The Bonin Islands, then the leap over to the Continent to Korea; then, after an interval of several decades, the expansion on the Continent to Manchuria and Northern China. The next step occurred last year: the penetration of Indo-China and Thailand, and now at last comes the extended outer ring—the Southern Empire with the Philippines, Malaya, and the Dutch East Indies. Will the next ring be drawn from the centre at Tokyo to South China, India, Australia?

"It is very likely that the war will necessitate such further expansion, though one is inclined in Tokyo to come to a standstill to gain the time necessary for the organisation of the territories recently incorporated in the 'GREATER ASIATIC EMPIRE.' The Japanese, as was to be expected, are up against numerous problems in these recently acquired territories. These problems are the subject of discussion among the Japanese public. Netherlands India is an example of how the Japanese deal with these problems. Immediately after the conquest, the Occupation Authorities addressed an appeal to the native population: 'Now get to work!' Working hours, but not wages, have been greatly increased on all plantations; Bank and Civil Administration offices used to work 4 hours daily; the number of working hours has now been doubled.

"The same applies to Malaya. The independence of the Malay States under their native rulers is a thing of the past. By a single stroke of the pen, joint administration of Singapore and Malaya has been established. British prisoners are forced to work together with the native population on the reconstruction of the town, which had been heavily damaged by air attacks. On the occasion of the above-mentioned Navy Day, the British were forced to put up the decorations for the parade of the victorious fleet, an event that was, of course, much discussed in the whole of the Southern realm and that will not soon be forgotten. Supplies of foodstuffs are still scarce in this former British Sea Fortress. Rice has almost entirely disappeared, and only fighting boats smuggle in some of the precious stuff from hidden or secret stocks. This rice is now sold at usurious prices. On the quays of Singapore, hundreds of people can be seen daily watching the 'Silver Divers.' The English had thrown ten million silver dollars into the muddy water so that it should not fall into the hands of the conquerors. Now the swift divers known to travellers dive after the silver coins and bring up dollar after dollar. They are, however, not allowed to keep the money but have to deliver it to the Occupation Authorities and receive a small percentage as a reward.

"Sunada, the adviser of the Japanese Army in Malaya, has declared, 'Years will go by before the Japanese aims are attained.' He said this after inspecting the whole territory taken from the English. With the exception of Penang, he found tin mines and rubber plantations destroyed everywhere. He must have noticed that engineers, work managers, organisers, etc., were lacking after the capture or flight of the white men who used to do this kind of work. Where is this technical leadership to come from now? Where are the experts necessary for Japan to build up their gigantic new Empire? Singapore, once flourishing, is a dead city now, where only the black market is kept alive, whilst the export and stock exchanges lie desolate. Seven newspapers are published again in Singapore, all under Japanese management. They are written in the Chinese, Indian, and English languages.

===== Page 231 =====
For the Japanese cannot do without English, the language of their enemy - it is the only tongue understood by everybody in the Babel of languages that is South East Asia. The Japanese try, of course, to introduce their own language. All the papers in the occupied territories give a course in the Japanese language. The creation of schools for the Japanese language operated by propaganda formations of the Army was often their first action taken in occupied territories. But even in Tokyo one is pessimistic as to the possibility of forcing people to learn the complicated language of Nippon with its many signs. A Japanese Language Society with the aim of simplifying the Japanese language in word and script has stated that one-third out of 2,000 school children they had tested were unable to read and write even the elementary parts of their own language. How should strangers be able to do so? Suggestions have been made to keep English as a language, but no longer to call it "English"; some Japanese name should be given to this idiom. It has been suggested to give up the complicated system of Chinese characters and to write the language with Latin letters, as had been done in Turkey. Nothing has yet been decided, though the question of language is of the greatest importance for the future of the Japanese World Empire with its many different people.

The question of religions offers another difficulty. Should these foreign people be taught Shinto Cult or should they be allowed to carry on with their own cults? In a way the problem is a political one, as in Japan the State with regard to religion is based on the person of the God-Emperor. Should missionary work, therefore, go on in the new Southern countries in order to unite them (with Japan)? It seems that the solution of these difficult questions has been put off for the time being. A great Congress of Religions has been arranged to take place in Tokyo in September, and Shintoists, Buddhists, Mohammedans and Christians will be given an opportunity to speak. The fact that Japan now has a representative at the Vatican, since its population now includes 15 million Catholics in the Philippines, seems to indicate that no interference with other religions is intended in the near future.

The question whether those Japanese who are now pouring into the new territories should mix with the conquered races is also one of the problems that has not been decided upon, though at present much discussed. Whilst one party is of the opinion that only intermarriage will create stronger bonds between Japan and the conquered peoples, another is against it. Mr. Koya, State Secretary in the Ministry for Welfare, in an article in "Nichi Nichi" strongly warned against intermarriage. The Japanese conquerors, he said, can learn a lot from the English in this respect; mixing would mean Japan's early decline.

These are the basic considerations for the Propaganda Organisation of the Army, which is fairly comprehensive and powerful and includes newspaper editors, theatre groups, publishing houses, etc. It tries to transmit, in comprehensible form, Japanese ways of thinking and living to the conquered peoples, either by pantomime or by using the foreign language. At present, for instance, 60 Chinese actresses from occupied Nangking-China are in Japan, making propaganda films in Tokyo studios; these films are to be used exclusively in occupied territories.

===== Page 232 =====
A group of six Japanese dramatists is touring Manchuria and occupied China to collect material for Chinese dramas and films. This is only one section of the Japanese propaganda offensive, which is carried on with the same eagerness in Java and Burma.

These cultural problems can, of course, be discussed much more openly than economic problems. Japanese at home have not yet been allowed to enjoy the fruits of victory, despite important conquests made. Shortages of many articles are still as bad as before the war, and in some ways they have become more intense due to lack of labor, as many workmen are now in the army. 200,000 girls will be sent to the country this summer by the Greater Japanese Youth Association. The slogan of the day is still "tighten your belts more". It is not mere coincidence that the only political movement in Japan today is the one that for many years has preached greater sobriety. The JOKAAN Movement was founded by His Excellency Horikiri, now Japanese Ambassador in Rome. Initially, this movement was not taken seriously. It proclaimed abolition of political parties and support of the Imperial Throne. The zealous preaching against lipstick, nail polish, Western clothes, smoking, and drinking was smiled upon. The political chances of this movement were regarded in Japanese political and economic quarters with about the same seriousness as the Vegetarian Movement would be in Europe. Well, Europe too seems to have underestimated asceticism. (Censor's Note: Reference to Hitler). It is a fact that the Jokaan Movement has gained strength and influence as living standards in Japan decreased; the Army supported it, as consumption reduction fitted their needs for increased armament production. Thus, the movement became a reality even the last Prime Minister Konoye could no longer deny. He tried to limit the Jokaan Movement's activities to reforming morale and keep it away from politics, but in the end, this silent fight lasted two years and was decided against Konoye.

When President Tojo went to the country on April 30 for re-election to the Japanese Reichstag, he already knew he would have a pliable majority. In theory, every Japanese could be a candidate; about 100 candidates ran for the 32 Tokyo District seats. However, there were no opposition candidates; they feared police attention. The result was as expected: 385 deputies of the Jokaan Party were elected and 71 "Independent" deputies. These 71 did not feel happy in their independence and, except for a small group of "honest men" who refuse to learn their lesson, preferred to join the government party. Since a certain meeting at the end of May, when 77 elected Jokaan deputies laid down the movement for the future, Japan has a one-party parliament. On almost the same day—fate sometimes makes history in an ironic way—Professor Kanako died at 90 years old, the creator of the Japanese parliamentary constitution, which is formally still valid.

===== Page 233 =====
The Jokaan Movement, under the leadership of the former Minister General Abe, now comprises practically all Japanese organisations of a political character; the new Unions (the old ones were all dissolved in April 1940) with their 6 million members are connected with this State-Party, the Peasants numbering 4 million, the Seamen's League with 1 million members, the Women's League with twenty million, the Youth Organisation with 15 million members. These are supposed to become the nucleus of the power which aims at becoming the "richest Empire in the World".

In the meantime, a play in Tokyo is running with great success, "U-Boat No. 6", featuring the loss of the first Japanese U-Boat in 1910, which was lost on a test voyage. Meanwhile, people stay in queues for rice at Osaka, and the taxis at Yokohama have gone to sleep in their garages. Harvesting has not yet started, and the defeat of the fleet at Midway Islands may lead many people to wonder whether by the end of the next six months of war Japan's hopes for the future will have become higher or more modest.

ERIC ROSENTHAL

JOHANNESBURG.

W. McCartney Major
Deputy Chief Censor.

9.10.1942/53.

===== Page 235 =====
INFORMATION REGARDING SINGAPORE
Furnished by Senior Mario Planet, Chilean Journalist.

1. What defences did you see?

The defences at the entrance to the port are intact, according to the Japanese officials. They are situated in the "mouth" of the port of Singapore. The old English batteries which used to be opposite Johore-Barhu, near the causeway, have been removed; as have all the anti-aircraft batteries in the town. The Japanese would not take us near the naval base, as they said that the military authorities were not giving any permits because they were building a new powder magazine. The long-distance guns were not used in the battle, and the Japanese also told us that they were ready for use.

The whole entrance to the port of Singapore is mined, and it took us nine hours in a tug to go ashore from the ship, which was anchored near the entrance to the straits of Johore. The route followed by the tug was marked by numbered buoys. Amongst the numbers were 17 and 19. We also passed some small buoys made of wooden barrels like water barrels. They have a wooden top, which enables them to remain afloat, and they apparently mark the position of submarine mines.

Before reaching the port, we passed a ship which looked very old. It had an aeroplane on board, and it was armed. We were soon able to see that it was the "flagship" of a fleet of "fishing boats". This fleet consists of about 80 very fast small boats, which are far from being fishing boats. They appear to be motor torpedo boats which defend the entrance. Before the tugboat was allowed past, the captain and the military police spent about ten minutes conferring with the crew of the "flagship". These boats must be the naval protection for the port. We did not see any large ships, and only met one convoy of three cargo ships and two tankers.

2. Did you see any new works, Naval, Army, or Air Force?

None, apart from roads being built by the prisoners of war. The aerodromes are in use. The naval base has been repaired except for the two large floating docks which were blown up.

3. Were you at liberty or taken round to selected places?

We had to wait more than ten hours in order to visit the battlefield of Johore. We made the trip in cars accompanied by military police - one policeman for each one of us. We were not allowed to walk alone in the streets, either. We were even forbidden to buy newspapers, and one which I succeeded in getting as far as the tug was confiscated by the Japanese Government representative who was in charge of the evacuation ship. During the few minutes we were in the street shopping, the police posted themselves all round and watched us like prisoners. They would not let any natives come near us.

4. Was there much activity? - Many troops, motor vehicles, aircraft, ships, etc.?

A fair amount of activity was noticeable from the morning onwards. I heard many explosions, and machine gun and anti-aircraft gun practice, and I saw about ten lorries full of soldiers pass by the Hotel Raffles. We were told that there were about 50,000 men. There are also about 400 aeroplanes. More than 100 aeroplanes of all types, bombers, fighters, etc., took part in a demonstration over the "Asama Maru", in front of the evacuated Americans.

===== Page 236 =====
5. Did you see any British prisoners, if so in what condition?

I saw around 3,000 British and Australian prisoners. They were working at building a road near the Ford Motor factories. They live in barracks surrounded by barbed wire fencing near the factory. I also saw some barracks with prisoners in the Indian quarter. All the barracks are made of wood. The prisoners' only clothes were shorts; a few still had hats; their shoes were in bad condition, and very few still had good ones.

The prisoners are occupied in road-building, transporting goods in lorries, etc. Some are drivers, others load, others have been taken to repair damage in the town, and others are building houses in the suburbs. The Indians have been concentrated in a place I didn't see, but I was told that they were all doing agricultural work.

I asked about General Percival, and was told that he was not receiving special treatment and was in the prison with all his staff.

6. Was there much destruction in the town or to the port and defences?

There is no destruction in the city, and the commercial centre is intact. In front of the Hotel Raffles, a building damaged by bombing has been demolished. This building was on the sea front. In the port only damage of small importance is noticeable. Some warehouses were hit by bombs; one small ship is aground, and in the entrance there are three or four huge lighters of which only the masts are visible. There is evident destruction in the Indian and Chinese quarters. At least two or three houses have been destroyed in each block, and the same is true on the road from Singapore to Johore. Almost all those houses were destroyed. In the residential district, on the other hand, no large-scale damage is visible. I would say that our drive covered about 8 kilometres, circling the town. We went in cars, accompanied by military police. Going out of the town towards the interior of the island, almost everything is completely destroyed. Even after several months of repair work and demolition of remains, there are signs of the effects of the bombing.

7. Was there a black-out at night?

There is no real black-out, only a control of the lights, which one might say is one degree less than a black-out. I was able to have my window open and the lights on in the Hotel, and I noticed that there were also lights in other rooms occupied by Japanese officials.

===== Page 237 =====
Memorandum re: JAPANESE RECONSTRUCTION OF PORT OF PENANG.

One of the most interesting features of the following report from the German Press is the reference to the programme of the Japanese for the construction of junks at Penang, which incidentally shows the shortage of shipping from which the enemy is suffering.

"Deutsche Allgemeine Zeitung", Berlin, July 19th, 1942. No.343/343.

"THE PORT OF PENANG IS REBUILT."

"Extensive Japanese Plan."

"Radio Report from our own Special Correspondent, Tichy. Shanghai 18.7.

Seven months after the occupation of the island of PENANG, the transport services of the North Malayan centre have been completely rebuilt. Shotaro Katayama, the Governor of the State of Penang, states that an extensive plan for the re-building of the harbour has been worked out with better quayage foundations than existed before the outbreak of the war. At the same time, an extensive building programme for junks is in progress."

===== Page 238 =====
Penang in Malay at 21:30 Tokyo time on 49.34 metres:

A talk was given entitled "Racial contacts and exchanges": "The Malays belong to one of the Asiatic races and in order to advance to the same level as other races, we must mix and benefit by our associations with other peoples. In the first place, we must learn to understand the other races, because we come into contact with them in our daily lives. We must learn to appreciate the culture, mentality, and customs of foreign races and not despise them or consider ourselves superior. After all, they are human beings like ourselves. We must learn to speak languages and we must mix with the Japanese. You must not feel indignant about learning new things which will help you, although the advantages are not visible yet."

===== Page 239 =====
HORROR IN MALAYA
Japs Massacre Chinese

An account of Jap cruelty in Malaya and Burma is contained in a document which has reached General Headquarters in New Delhi. For reasons of security, the source cannot be disclosed.

After the fall of Singapore, Chinese were rounded up and forced to dig their own graves. They were then made to stand in lines, and a Jap officer beheaded the first Chinese in the front line.

Then a subaltern beheaded the others, and the Chinese standing in the second line were forced to bury their dead. The second line was then forced to dig its own grave. Nine thousand girls were seized for immoral purposes.—Exchange.

Pering Radio (in Malay): at 22:00 hrs on 49.34 metres

A talk on business was given in which comment was made on the fact that there were few Malays in business. This was attributed to three causes: the Malays prefer steady wages to a precarious business; big British firms owned monopolies, leaving no chance for small shops; the Malays were poor owing to British policy. "Now, Japanese banks will give the Malays a chance to start businesses. The Asiatics have progressed, but the Malays have regressed."

The British in Malaya treated the Malays very badly, as though they were aliens, while foreigners dispossessed them. They are now rid of the intrigues practiced by the British, who bribed the Malay Rajahs to praise the British.

The Japanese

===== Page 243 =====

EXTRACT FROM MONITORING REPORT

FAR EAST

DATED October 27th, 1942.

In order to readjust the juridical affairs in Malaya, a group of Japanese jurists have arrived in Malaya.

===== Page 245 =====
Currency Policy

Local currency is still circulating for private transactions in Hong Kong, but the Japanese have also issued military yen. The rate of exchange was first officially fixed at 2 Hong Kong dollars = 1 military yen, but further devaluation followed and finally the Hong Kong dollar was decreed to be no longer legal tender. The note-issuing banks have been compelled to sign and deliver to the Japanese a certain number of unissued Hong Kong notes of high denomination (£500). We have very little information about currency in Malaya, but it is known that the local currency has been the medium used by the Japanese in making their purchases and that they have issued some Straits dollar notes of their own manufacture. It has been stated recently that the Japanese have arrangements underway to replace all the Straits dollars with yen.

It seems to be the Japanese Government's policy eventually to replace both military yen notes and the local currency notes throughout the occupied territories by different notes, but so far we have no information as to the nature of the permanent currency contemplated. This is apparently to make each occupied country financially self-sufficient.

There is no doubt that as far as Malaya is concerned the dollar is generally deflationary, but in Hong Kong there is an upward trend in prices set in motion by the devaluation of the local currency and this motion counteracts any deflationary course.

Control of Banks

Banks in Hong Kong are being liquidated; attached is a translation of the official Japanese instructions regarding the procedure.

No information has reached us about the banks in Malaya.

===== Page 246 =====
3. There have been no attempts by the Japanese in Hong Kong (or as far as can be known in Malaysia) to leave British officials in their own country. Such officials have been interned with three regiments of the European community. In individual cases, it suits the Japanese to leave individuals, e.g., directors, from internment for special duties.

4. There is no information on record in the Colonial Office on the constitution and powers of Japanese military courts in occupied territory.

5. In general, Japanese policy as regards functions in occupied territory appears to be complete transfer to Japanese from where the prisoners were batched or Allied notices; in some cases, however, Chinese or other assistive forms have been kept to "co-operate" with Japanese plans. It is known there are considerable quantities of manufacturing plants together with other materials used for the Japanese war efforts shipped from Hong Kong to Japan.
